Waqf — Endowment

10 min
Waqf of Trees in Mosques, Ribats, and Public Places
Ruling statements on waqf of trees in mosques, ribats, and public property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings for waqf of trees planted in mosques, on waqf ribat land, and on public property, and distinguish between ownership and removal obligations.

Core Idea
Planting trees in mosques makes them waqf by analogy to buildings; on ribat land, care determines waqf; on public land, the planter owns but must uproot.

This passage from al-Muhit and other sources addresses specific scenarios: planting in a mosque, in a ribat, and on public roads or waterways. The rulings depend on the nature of the land and the planter's authority. Understanding these cases clarifies how waqf of trees interacts with different property regimes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of land
Is the tree planted in a mosque, a waqf ribat, or a public area (road, riverbank, village pond)?
Each has a distinct ruling.
2
Determine the planter's intention and authority
For mosques, intention at planting matters. For ribat, whether the planter undertakes care (ta'ahud) of the land. For public land, the planter has no authority over the public.
Intention is crucial for mosque; care for ribat.
3
Apply the ruling
Mosque: tree belongs to mosque if intended as waqf at planting (like building). Ribat: if planter tends land, tree becomes waqf; otherwise planter owns and must uproot. Public: planter owns but must uproot.
Uprooting is required because the planter lacks authority to occupy public space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقف الشجر قال في الظهيرية : وإذا غرس شجرة ووقفها إن غرسها في أرض غير موقوفة لا يخلو إما أن يقفها بموضعها من الأرض أو لا، فإن وقفها بموضعها من الأرض صح تبعاً للأرض بحكم الاتصال، وإن وقفها دون أصلها لا يصح، وإن كانت في أرض موقوفة فوقفها على تلك الجهة جاز كما في البناء، وإن وقفها على جهة أخرى فعلى الاختلاف الذي ذكرناه آنفاً اهـ. وفي المحيط : رجل غرس في المسجد يكون للمسجد لأنه بمنزلة البناء بالمسجد، وكذا لو بنى في أرض الوقف أو نصب فيها باباً فإن نوى عند البناء أنه وقفاً لأنه جعله وقفاً ووقف البناء تبعاً لغيره يجوز، وإن لم ينو ذلك لا وقفاً لأنه لم يجعله وقفاً. ولو غرس في أرض موقوفة على الرباط ينظر؛ إن تولى الغارس تعاهد الأرض الموقوفة فالأشجار للوقف لأن هذا من جملة التعاهد، وإن لم يتول فهي للغارس وعليه قلعها لأنه ليس له هذه الولاية ولو غرس على طريق العامة أو على شط نهر العامة أو على شط حوض القرية فالشجرة للغارس وله قلعها لأنه ليس له ولاية على العامة اهـ. وفي الخانية : لو غرس الواقف للأرض شجراً فيها قالوا : إن غرس من غلة الوقف أو من مال نفسه لكن ذكر أنه غرس للوقف يكون للوقف وإن لم يذكر شيئاً وقد غرس من مال نفسه يكون له ولورثته من بعده ولا يكون وقفاً، وإذا صح وقف الشجرة تبعاً لأصلها فإن كان ينتفع بأوراقها وأثمارها فإنه لا يقطع أصلها إلا أن تفسد أغصانها، ولو كان لا ينتفع بأوراقها ولا بأثمارها فإنه يقطع ويتصدق بها مسجد فيه شجرة التفاح قال بعضهم يباح للقوم أن يفطروا بهذا التفاح والصحيح أنه لا يباح لأن ذلك صار وقفاً للمسجد يصرف إلى عمارته. شجرة على طريق المارة جعلت وقفاً على المارة يباح تناول ثمرها للمارة ويستوي فيه الفقير والغني. ولو كانت الثمار على أشجار رباط المارة. قال أبو القاسم : أرجو أن يكون النزال في سعة من تناولها إلا أن يعلم أن غارسها جعلها للفقراء. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : إذا لم يكن الرجل من ساكني الرباط فالأحوط له أن يحترز من تناولها إلا أن تكون ثماراً لا قيمة لها كالتوت اهـ . وقد وقعت حادثة أن المستأجر للدار الموقوفة المشتملة على الأشجار هل له أن يأكل من ثمارها إذا لم يعلم شرط الواقف فيها ؟ وفي الحاوي وما غرس في المساجد من الأشجار وفاعله حكر كفرح. وأقول : والأرض المحتكرة هي التي وقف بناؤها ولم توقف هي كأن استأجر أرضاً للبناء عليها وبنى فيها ثم وقف البناء ؛ كذا رأيت لبعض الشافعية. وأقول : الأرض للاحتكار أعم من أن تكون وقفاً أو ملكاً والاحتكار في العرف إجارة يقصد بها منع الغير واستبقاء الانتفاع بالأرض. قالوا : لو بنى على أرض مقررة للاحتكار فباع البناء لا شفعة فيه لأنه من قسم المنقول قوله : (أن تولى الغارس تعاهد الأرض) أي بأن كان له ولاية عليها وعبارة الإسعاف أظهر : فلو غرس رباطي شجرة في وقف الرباط وتعاهدها حتى كبرت ولم يذكر وقت الغرس أنها وهي: هي هي المقررة
Waqf of trees: It is said in al-Zahiriyyah: When someone plants a tree and makes it waqf, if he plants it on non-waqf land, it is either that he makes it waqf with its place in the land or not. If he makes it waqf with its place in the land, it is valid as a follow of the land by virtue of attachment. If he makes it waqf without its root, it is not valid. If it is on waqf land and he makes it waqf for that same purpose, it is permissible as in the case of a building. If he makes it waqf for another purpose, it follows the difference of opinion we mentioned earlier. In al-Muhit: A man plants in a mosque, it belongs to the mosque because it is like a building in the mosque. Similarly, if he builds on waqf land or installs a door, if he intended at the time of building that it be waqf, it is valid because he made it waqf; if he did not intend that, it is not waqf because he did not make it waqf. And if he plants on waqf land designated for a ribat, it is considered: if the planter undertook the care of the waqf land, then the trees are for the waqf because that is part of the care; if he did not undertake, then they belong to the planter and he must uproot them because he has no such authority. And if he plants on a public road, or on the bank of a public river, or on the bank of a village pond, the tree belongs to the planter and he may uproot it because he has no authority over the public. In al-Khaniyah: If the waqif of land plants trees in it, they said: if he planted from the revenue of the waqf or from his own money but mentioned that he planted for the waqf, then it belongs to the waqf. If he did not mention anything and planted from his own money, it belongs to him and his heirs after him and is not waqf. And if the waqf of the tree is valid as a follow of its origin, then if one benefits from its leaves and fruits, the trunk is not cut unless its branches become corrupt. If one does not benefit from its leaves or fruits, then it is cut and given in charity. A mosque containing an apple tree: some said it is permissible for the people to break their fast with this apple; the correct view is that it is not permissible because that has become waqf for the mosque to be spent on its maintenance. A tree on a public path made waqf for the passers-by: it is permissible for passers-by to consume its fruit, equally for poor and rich. And if the fruits are on trees of a travelers' ribat, Abu al-Qasim said: I hope that the residents are at ease in consuming them unless they know that the planter made them for the poor. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: If a man is not a resident of the ribat, it is precautionary for him to refrain from consuming them unless they are fruits of no value like mulberries. An incident occurred: the lessee of a waqf house containing trees – may he eat from its fruits if he does not know the condition of the waqif? In al-Hawi: And whatever is planted in mosques of trees, its doer has hukr like joy. I say: The land of hukr is that whose building is waqf but not the land itself, as if one leases land to build upon and builds, then makes the building waqf. Thus I saw from some Shafi'is. I say: Land for hukr is more general – it may be waqf or owned. Hukr in custom is a lease intended to prevent others and preserve benefit from the land. They said: If one builds on land designated for hukr and sells the building, there is no pre-emption (shuf'ah) because it is of the category of movable property. His saying: 'If the planter undertook the care of the land' meaning he had authority over it, and the expression of al-Is'af is more apparent: If a ribat resident planted a tree in the waqf of the ribat and cared for it until it grew, and did not mention at the time of planting that it... and it is: it is the designated.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on waqf of trees from various Hanafi sources. The author begins with al-Zahiriyyah, distinguishing between planting on owned land versus waqf land. On owned land, waqf of the tree is valid only if the tree is waqf along with its place in the land (as an extension of the land). On waqf land, if the tree is waqf for the same purpose as the land, it is valid; otherwise, the earlier disagreement on buildings applies. Al-Muhit adds that planting in a mosque makes the tree belong to the mosque, analogous to building. Intention matters: if the planter intended the tree as waqf at planting, it becomes waqf; otherwise not. For waqf land designated for a ribat, if the planter personally tends the land, the trees become part of the waqf; if not, they are his and he must remove them. Planting on public property or roads gives the planter ownership but with an obligation to uproot (since he has no authority over the public). Al-Khaniyah discusses the waqif's own planting: if he planted from waqf revenue or declared it for the waqf, it is waqf; otherwise it remains his private property. Issues of benefiting from fruits follow: if the tree is waqf, fruits go to the waqf purpose (e.g., mosque maintenance). Eating fruit from a tree on a public path is allowed for all, but from a ribat, scholars caution. The incident about a lessee eating fruits from waqf house trees is left unresolved in the text. The passage then transitions to hukr, clarifying that hukr land is where the building is waqf but the land is not; hukr is a lease to prevent others and retain benefit. The final commentary explains the phrase about the planter undertaking care, citing al-Is'af.

Key Terms
الغارس (al-ghāris): The planter; the person who plants the tree.
الرباط (al-ribāṭ): A hospice or fortress; waqf land designated for travelers or defenders.
تعاهد الأرض (ta'āhuḍ al-arḍ): Undertaking the care and maintenance of the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person plants a tree on a public road, what is the ruling regarding ownership and removal?
A) The tree belongs to the public and cannot be removed.
B) The tree belongs to the planter and he must uproot it.
C) The tree belongs to the state and requires permission.
D) The tree becomes waqf for the public.

Key Takeaway
For trees on mosque land, intention matters; on ribat land, care matters; on public land, the planter owns but must remove.

Waqf of Trees: The Waqif's Own Planting and Intention
Ruling statements on trees planted by the waqif on his own waqf land and on cutting waqf trees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether trees planted by a waqif on his own waqf land become part of the waqf based on the source of funds and declaration, and state the rules for cutting waqf trees.

Core Idea
When a waqif plants trees on waqf land, the source of funds and explicit declaration determine ownership; if validly waqf, cutting depends on benefit.

This passage from al-Khaniyah addresses a specific scenario: the waqif (endower) of land plants trees on that land. The ruling hinges on whether he used waqf revenue or his own money, and whether he declared the planting for the waqf. Additionally, if the tree is validly waqf, rules for cutting it depend on whether its leaves and fruits are useful.

Guided Path
1
Determine the source of funds and declaration
Was the tree planted from waqf revenue or the waqif's own money? Did he explicitly state it was for the waqf?
If from waqf revenue or declared, it becomes waqf. If from own money without declaration, it remains his property.
2
Check the validity of the waqf of the tree
If valid as a follow of the land, then the tree is waqf.
Validity depends on attachment and proper declaration.
3
Apply rules for cutting the tree
If the tree has beneficial leaves/fruits, the trunk is not cut unless branches are corrupted. If no benefit, it is cut and given in charity.
This preserves the waqf's benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقف الشجر قال في الظهيرية : وإذا غرس شجرة ووقفها إن غرسها في أرض غير موقوفة لا يخلو إما أن يقفها بموضعها من الأرض أو لا، فإن وقفها بموضعها من الأرض صح تبعاً للأرض بحكم الاتصال، وإن وقفها دون أصلها لا يصح، وإن كانت في أرض موقوفة فوقفها على تلك الجهة جاز كما في البناء، وإن وقفها على جهة أخرى فعلى الاختلاف الذي ذكرناه آنفاً اهـ. وفي المحيط : رجل غرس في المسجد يكون للمسجد لأنه بمنزلة البناء بالمسجد، وكذا لو بنى في أرض الوقف أو نصب فيها باباً فإن نوى عند البناء أنه وقفاً لأنه جعله وقفاً ووقف البناء تبعاً لغيره يجوز، وإن لم ينو ذلك لا وقفاً لأنه لم يجعله وقفاً. ولو غرس في أرض موقوفة على الرباط بأثمارها فإنه يقطع ويتصدق بها مسجد فيه شجرة التفاح قال بعضهم يباح للقوم أن يفطروا بهذا التفاح والصحيح أنه لا يباح لأن ذلك صار وقفاً للمسجد يصرف إلى عمارته. شجرة على طريق المارة جعلت وقفاً على المارة يباح تناول ثمرها للمارة ويستوي فيه الفقير والغني. ولو كانت الثمار على أشجار رباط المارة. قال أبو القاسم : أرجو أن يكون النزال في سعة من تناولها إلا أن يعلم أن غارسها جعلها للفقراء. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : إذا لم يكن الرجل من ساكني الرباط فالأحوط له أن يحترز من تناولها إلا أن تكون ثماراً لا قيمة لها كالتوت اهـ . وقد وقعت حادثة أن المستأجر للدار الموقوفة المشتملة على الأشجار هل له أن يأكل من ثمارها إذا لم يعلم شرط الواقف فيها ؟ وفي الحاوي وما غرس في المساجد من الأشجار وفاعله حكر كفرح. وأقول : والأرض المحتكرة هي التي وقف بناؤها ولم توقف هي كأن استأجر أرضاً للبناء عليها وبنى فيها ثم وقف البناء ؛ كذا رأيت لبعض الشافعية. وأقول : الأرض للاحتكار أعم من أن تكون وقفاً أو ملكاً والاحتكار في العرف إجارة يقصد بها منع الغير واستبقاء الانتفاع بالأرض. قالوا : لو بنى على أرض مقررة للاحتكار فباع البناء لا شفعة فيه لأنه من قسم المنقول قوله : (أن تولى الغارس تعاهد الأرض) أي بأن كان له ولاية عليها وعبارة الإسعاف أظهر : فلو غرس رباطي شجرة في وقف الرباط وتعاهدها حتى كبرت ولم يذكر وقت الغرس أنها وهي: هي هي المقررة
Waqf of trees: It is said in al-Zahiriyyah: When someone plants a tree and makes it waqf, if he plants it on non-waqf land, it is either that he makes it waqf with its place in the land or not. If he makes it waqf with its place in the land, it is valid as a follow of the land by virtue of attachment. If he makes it waqf without its root, it is not valid. If it is on waqf land and he makes it waqf for that same purpose, it is permissible as in the case of a building. If he makes it waqf for another purpose, it follows the difference of opinion we mentioned earlier. In al-Muhit: A man plants in a mosque, it belongs to the mosque because it is like a building in the mosque. Similarly, if he builds on waqf land or installs a door, if he intended at the time of building that it be waqf, it is valid because he made it waqf; if he did not intend that, it is not waqf because he did not make it waqf. And if he plants on waqf land designated for a ribat, it is considered: if the planter undertook the care of the waqf land, then the trees are for the waqf because that is part of the care; if he did not undertake, then they belong to the planter and he must uproot them because he has no such authority. And if he plants on a public road, or on the bank of a public river, or on the bank of a village pond, the tree belongs to the planter and he may uproot it because he has no authority over the public. In al-Khaniyah: If the waqif of land plants trees in it, they said: if he planted from the revenue of the waqf or from his own money but mentioned that he planted for the waqf, then it belongs to the waqf. If he did not mention anything and planted from his own money, it belongs to him and his heirs after him and is not waqf. And if the waqf of the tree is valid as a follow of its origin, then if one benefits from its leaves and fruits, the trunk is not cut unless its branches become corrupt. If one does not benefit from its leaves or fruits, then it is cut and given in charity. A mosque containing an apple tree: some said it is permissible for the people to break their fast with this apple; the correct view is that it is not permissible because that has become waqf for the mosque to be spent on its maintenance. A tree on a public path made waqf for the passers-by: it is permissible for passers-by to consume its fruit, equally for poor and rich. And if the fruits are on trees of a travelers' ribat, Abu al-Qasim said: I hope that the residents are at ease in consuming them unless they know that the planter made them for the poor. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: If a man is not a resident of the ribat, it is precautionary for him to refrain from consuming them unless they are fruits of no value like mulberries. An incident occurred: the lessee of a waqf house containing trees – may he eat from its fruits if he does not know the condition of the waqif? In al-Hawi: And whatever is planted in mosques of trees, its doer has hukr like joy. I say: The land of hukr is that whose building is waqf but not the land itself, as if one leases land to build upon and builds, then makes the building waqf. Thus I saw from some Shafi'is. I say: Land for hukr is more general – it may be waqf or owned. Hukr in custom is a lease intended to prevent others and preserve benefit from the land. They said: If one builds on land designated for hukr and sells the building, there is no pre-emption (shuf'ah) because it is of the category of movable property. His saying: 'If the planter undertook the care of the land' meaning he had authority over it, and the expression of al-Is'af is more apparent: If a ribat resident planted a tree in the waqf of the ribat and cared for it until it grew, and did not mention at the time of planting that it... and it is: it is the designated.

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on waqf of trees from various Hanafi sources. The author begins with al-Zahiriyyah, distinguishing between planting on owned land versus waqf land. On owned land, waqf of the tree is valid only if the tree is waqf along with its place in the land (as an extension of the land). On waqf land, if the tree is waqf for the same purpose as the land, it is valid; otherwise, the earlier disagreement on buildings applies. Al-Muhit adds that planting in a mosque makes the tree belong to the mosque, analogous to building. Intention matters: if the planter intended the tree as waqf at planting, it becomes waqf; otherwise not. For waqf land designated for a ribat, if the planter personally tends the land, the trees become part of the waqf; if not, they are his and he must remove them. Planting on public property or roads gives the planter ownership but with an obligation to uproot (since he has no authority over the public). Al-Khaniyah discusses the waqif's own planting: if he planted from waqf revenue or declared it for the waqf, it is waqf; otherwise it remains his private property. Issues of benefiting from fruits follow: if the tree is waqf, fruits go to the waqf purpose (e.g., mosque maintenance). Eating fruit from a tree on a public path is allowed for all, but from a ribat, scholars caution. The incident about a lessee eating fruits from waqf house trees is left unresolved in the text. The passage then transitions to hukr, clarifying that hukr land is where the building is waqf but the land is not; hukr is a lease to prevent others and retain benefit. The final commentary explains the phrase about the planter undertaking care, citing al-Is'af.

Key Terms
الواقف (al-wāqif): The endower; the person who establishes the waqf.
غلة الوقف (ghullat al-waqf): Revenue generated by the waqf property (e.g., rent, produce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a waqf tree that has no beneficial leaves or fruits be cut?
A) Only if the branches are corrupt.
B) It can be cut and given in charity.
C) It can never be cut.
D) It must be left to dry.

Key Takeaway
The waqif's planting on waqf land becomes waqf only if funded by waqf revenue or explicitly declared; waqf trees with benefit are not cut unnecessarily.

Benefiting from Fruits of Waqf Trees: Mosque, Public Path, and Ribat
Ruling statements on consuming fruits from waqf trees in different contexts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings on who may consume fruits from waqf trees in a mosque, on a public path, and in a ribat, and identify the conditions and precautions.

Core Idea
Fruits from waqf trees are dedicated to the waqf's purpose: mosque fruits for mosque maintenance, public path fruits for all passers-by, ribat fruits with caution for residents.

This passage addresses practical questions about consuming fruits from waqf trees. The rulings vary: an apple tree in a mosque is for mosque maintenance; a tree on a public path is for all passers-by; fruits from a ribat are generally permissible for residents unless designated for the poor. There is also an incident about a lessee of a waqf house with trees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the location of the waqf tree
Is it in a mosque, on a public path, or in a ribat? Each has a different ruling.
The purpose of the waqf determines who benefits.
2
Determine the purpose of the waqf
Mosque: fruits are for mosque maintenance. Public path: fruits are for passers-by. Ribat: fruits are for residents unless designated for the poor.
The waqf's original dedication governs.
3
Apply precautionary considerations
For ribat, if the planter intended the fruits for the poor, non-residents should avoid. For lessee of waqf house, the ruling is not definitive in the text.
Precaution (ihtiyat) is recommended when uncertain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقف الشجر قال في الظهيرية : وإذا غرس شجرة ووقفها إن غرسها في أرض غير موقوفة لا يخلو إما أن يقفها بموضعها من الأرض أو لا، فإن وقفها بموضعها من الأرض صح تبعاً للأرض بحكم الاتصال، وإن وقفها دون أصلها لا يصح، وإن كانت في أرض موقوفة فوقفها على تلك الجهة جاز كما في البناء، وإن وقفها على جهة أخرى فعلى الاختلاف الذي ذكرناه آنفاً اهـ. وفي المحيط : رجل غرس في المسجد يكون للمسجد لأنه بمنزلة البناء بالمسجد، وكذا لو بنى في أرض الوقف أو نصب فيها باباً فإن نوى عند البناء أنه وقفاً لأنه جعله وقفاً ووقف البناء تبعاً لغيره يجوز، وإن لم ينو ذلك لا وقفاً لأنه لم يجعله وقفاً. ولو غرس في أرض موقوفة على الرباط ينظر؛ إن تولى الغارس تعاهد الأرض الموقوفة فالأشجار للوقف لأن هذا من جملة التعاهد، وإن لم يتول فهي للغارس وعليه قلعها لأنه ليس له هذه الولاية ولو غرس على طريق العامة أو على شط نهر العامة أو على شط حوض القرية فالشجرة للغارس وله قلعها لأنه ليس له ولاية على العامة اهـ. وفي الخانية : لو غرس الواقف للأرض شجراً فيها قالوا : إن غرس من غلة الوقف أو من مال نفسه لكن ذكر أنه غرس للوقف يكون للوقف وإن لم يذكر شيئاً وقد غرس من مال نفسه يكون له ولورثته من بعده ولا يكون وقفاً، وإذا صح وقف الشجرة تبعاً لأصلها فإن كان ينتفع بأوراقها وأثمارها فإنه لا يقطع أصلها إلا أن تفسد أغصانها، ولو كان لا ينتفع بأوراقها ولا بأثمارها فإنه يقطع ويتصدق بها مسجد فيه شجرة التفاح قال بعضهم يباح للقوم أن يفطروا بهذا التفاح والصحيح أنه لا يباح لأن ذلك صار وقفاً للمسجد يصرف إلى عمارته. شجرة على طريق المارة جعلت وقفاً على المارة يباح تناول ثمرها للمارة ويستوي فيه الفقير والغني. ولو كانت الثمار على أشجار رباط المارة. قال أبو القاسم : أرجو أن يكون النزال في سعة من تناولها إلا أن يعلم أن غارسها جعلها للفقراء. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : إذا لم يكن الرجل من ساكني الرباط فالأحوط له أن يحترز من تناولها إلا أن تكون ثماراً لا قيمة لها كالتوت اهـ . وقد وقعت حادثة أن المستأجر للدار الموقوفة المشتملة على الأشجار هل له أن يأكل من ثمارها إذا لم يعلم شرط الواقف فيها ؟ وفي الحاوي وما غرس في المساجد من الأشجار وفاعله حكر كفرح. وأقول : والأرض المحتكرة هي التي وقف بناؤها ولم توقف هي كأن استأجر أرضاً للبناء عليها وبنى فيها ثم وقف البناء ؛ كذا رأيت لبعض الشافعية. وأقول : الأرض للاحتكار أعم من أن تكون وقفاً أو ملكاً والاحتكار في العرف إجارة يقصد بها منع الغير واستبقاء الانتفاع بالأرض. قالوا : لو بنى على أرض مقررة للاحتكار فباع البناء لا شفعة فيه لأنه من قسم المنقول قوله : (أن تولى الغارس تعاهد الأرض) أي بأن كان له ولاية عليها وعبارة الإسعاف أظهر : فلو غرس رباطي شجرة في وقف الرباط وتعاهدها حتى كبرت ولم يذكر وقت الغرس أنها وهي: هي هي المقررة
Waqf of trees: It is said in al-Zahiriyyah: When someone plants a tree and makes it waqf, if he plants it on non-waqf land, it is either that he makes it waqf with its place in the land or not. If he makes it waqf with its place in the land, it is valid as a follow of the land by virtue of attachment. If he makes it waqf without its root, it is not valid. If it is on waqf land and he makes it waqf for that same purpose, it is permissible as in the case of a building. If he makes it waqf for another purpose, it follows the difference of opinion we mentioned earlier. In al-Muhit: A man plants in a mosque, it belongs to the mosque because it is like a building in the mosque. Similarly, if he builds on waqf land or installs a door, if he intended at the time of building that it be waqf, it is valid because he made it waqf; if he did not intend that, it is not waqf because he did not make it waqf. And if he plants on waqf land designated for a ribat, it is considered: if the planter undertook the care of the waqf land, then the trees are for the waqf because that is part of the care; if he did not undertake, then they belong to the planter and he must uproot them because he has no such authority. And if he plants on a public road, or on the bank of a public river, or on the bank of a village pond, the tree belongs to the planter and he may uproot it because he has no authority over the public. In al-Khaniyah: If the waqif of land plants trees in it, they said: if he planted from the revenue of the waqf or from his own money but mentioned that he planted for the waqf, then it belongs to the waqf. If he did not mention anything and planted from his own money, it belongs to him and his heirs after him and is not waqf. And if the waqf of the tree is valid as a follow of its origin, then if one benefits from its leaves and fruits, the trunk is not cut unless its branches become corrupt. If one does not benefit from its leaves or fruits, then it is cut and given in charity. A mosque containing an apple tree: some said it is permissible for the people to break their fast with this apple; the correct view is that it is not permissible because that has become waqf for the mosque to be spent on its maintenance. A tree on a public path made waqf for the passers-by: it is permissible for passers-by to consume its fruit, equally for poor and rich. And if the fruits are on trees of a travelers' ribat, Abu al-Qasim said: I hope that the residents are at ease in consuming them unless they know that the planter made them for the poor. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: If a man is not a resident of the ribat, it is precautionary for him to refrain from consuming them unless they are fruits of no value like mulberries. An incident occurred: the lessee of a waqf house containing trees – may he eat from its fruits if he does not know the condition of the waqif? In al-Hawi: And whatever is planted in mosques of trees, its doer has hukr like joy. I say: The land of hukr is that whose building is waqf but not the land itself, as if one leases land to build upon and builds, then makes the building waqf. Thus I saw from some Shafi'is. I say: Land for hukr is more general – it may be waqf or owned. Hukr in custom is a lease intended to prevent others and preserve benefit from the land. They said: If one builds on land designated for hukr and sells the building, there is no pre-emption (shuf'ah) because it is of the category of movable property. His saying: 'If the planter undertook the care of the land' meaning he had authority over it, and the expression of al-Is'af is more apparent: If a ribat resident planted a tree in the waqf of the ribat and cared for it until it grew, and did not mention at the time of planting that it... and it is: it is the designated.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on waqf of trees from various Hanafi sources. The author begins with al-Zahiriyyah, distinguishing between planting on owned land versus waqf land. On owned land, waqf of the tree is valid only if the tree is waqf along with its place in the land (as an extension of the land). On waqf land, if the tree is waqf for the same purpose as the land, it is valid; otherwise, the earlier disagreement on buildings applies. Al-Muhit adds that planting in a mosque makes the tree belong to the mosque, analogous to building. Intention matters: if the planter intended the tree as waqf at planting, it becomes waqf; otherwise not. For waqf land designated for a ribat, if the planter personally tends the land, the trees become part of the waqf; if not, they are his and he must remove them. Planting on public property or roads gives the planter ownership but with an obligation to uproot (since he has no authority over the public). Al-Khaniyah discusses the waqif's own planting: if he planted from waqf revenue or declared it for the waqf, it is waqf; otherwise it remains his private property. Issues of benefiting from fruits follow: if the tree is waqf, fruits go to the waqf purpose (e.g., mosque maintenance). Eating fruit from a tree on a public path is allowed for all, but from a ribat, scholars caution. The incident about a lessee eating fruits from waqf house trees is left unresolved in the text. The passage then transitions to hukr, clarifying that hukr land is where the building is waqf but the land is not; hukr is a lease to prevent others and retain benefit. The final commentary explains the phrase about the planter undertaking care, citing al-Is'af.

Key Terms
الرباط (al-ribāṭ): A hospice or travelers' lodge; here, the waqf may have specific conditions for fruit consumption.
الاحتياط (al-iḥtiyāṭ): Precaution; a recommended avoidance in doubtful situations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the accepted view, what should be done with apples from a waqf tree in a mosque?
A) They may be eaten by worshippers.
B) They should be used for mosque maintenance.
C) They should be given to the poor.
D) They must be left to rot.

Key Takeaway
Fruits from waqf trees must be used in accordance with the waqf's purpose: mosque fruits for maintenance, public path fruits for all, ribat fruits with caution.

Hukr (Leasehold) and Its Effect on Waqf of Buildings and Trees
Definition and rulings on hukr and its implications for waqf buildings and pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hukr, explain its effect on waqf of buildings, and apply the ruling that buildings on hukr land are movable property for pre-emption (shuf'ah).

Core Idea
Hukr is a lease that prevents others from using the land; buildings on hukr land are treated as movable property.

This passage introduces the concept of hukr (also called ihtikar), a lease arrangement that gives the lessee the right to exclude others. The author clarifies that in hukr, the building is waqf but the land is not necessarily waqf. A key ruling is that buildings on hukr land are considered movable property for the purpose of pre-emption (shuf'ah).

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of hukr
Hukr is a lease intended to prevent others from using the land and to preserve the lessee's benefit. The land may be waqf or owned.
The author says: 'الأرض للاحتكار أعم من أن تكون وقفاً أو ملكاً'.
2
Identify the waqf status in hukr
In hukr, the building is typically waqf, but the land is not necessarily waqf. The building can be a separate waqf on leased land.
Example: someone leases land, builds, then makes the building waqf.
3
Apply the ruling on pre-emption
If a building on hukr land is sold, there is no right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) because the building is considered movable property.
The text says: 'لا شفعة فيه لأنه من قسم المنقول'.

Key Terms
الاحتكار (al-iḥtikār): Hukr; a lease arrangement where the lessee gains the right to prevent others from using the land, often used with waqf properties.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption; the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property when it is sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal status of a building built on hukr land?
A) It is immovable property like any other building.
B) It is considered movable property for the purpose of pre-emption.
C) It becomes part of the land.
D) It cannot be sold separately.

Key Takeaway
Hukr is a lease that makes buildings on the land legally movable; therefore, pre-emption (shuf'ah) does not apply when such buildings are sold.

Rulings on Trees Planted in Waqf Land
Details multiple scenarios for trees in waqf land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of taba'iyya (subsidiarity) to determine whether trees planted in waqf or non-waqf land become part of the waqf.

Core Idea
A tree becomes waqf only if physically attached to waqf land and intended for that purpose.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq covers the conditions under which trees planted in waqf land become part of the endowment. The key factor is physical connection (ittisal) and the planter's intention. Understanding these rules is essential for managing waqf properties and avoiding violations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the land type
Determine whether the tree is planted in waqf or non-waqf land. This governs the initial framework.
Non-waqf land requires the tree's place to be included; waqf land allows flexible intention.
2
Analyze the planter's intention
If the planter intends the tree for the same waqf purpose as the land, it becomes waqf. If not, or if no intention, it remains private property.
Intention can be expressed or inferred from the source of funds.
3
Apply the subsidiary principle
Trees attached to land are subsidiary (tabi') to it. In non-waqf land, the waqf of the tree alone is invalid unless the land's place is included.
This is analogous to buildings.
4
Consider special scenarios
Rules differ for mosques, ribats, public roads, and land planted by the waqf founder. These are detailed in the passage.
E.g., planting in a mosque automatically belongs to the mosque.
5
Apply consequences
If the tree is waqf, its usufruct (fruit, leaves) is dedicated to the waqf purpose. Cutting a beneficial tree is prohibited unless branches spoil.
Useless trees may be cut and given in charity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوقف
وقف الشجر قال في الظهيرية : وإذا غرس شجرة ووقفها إن غرسها في أرض غير موقوفة لا يخلو إما أن يقفها بموضعها من الأرض أو لا، فإن وقفها بموضعها من الأرض صح تبعاً للأرض بحكم الاتصال، وإن وقفها دون أصلها لا يصح، وإن كانت في أرض موقوفة فوقفها على تلك الجهة جاز كما في البناء، وإن وقفها على جهة أخرى فعلى الاختلاف الذي ذكرناه آنفاً اهـ. وفي المحيط : رجل غرس في المسجد يكون للمسجد لأنه بمنزلة البناء بالمسجد، وكذا لو بنى في أرض الوقف أو نصب فيها باباً فإن نوى عند البناء أنه وقفاً لأنه جعله وقفاً ووقف البناء تبعاً لغيره يجوز، وإن لم ينو ذلك لا وقفاً لأنه لم يجعله وقفاً. ولو غرس في أرض موقوفة على الرباط ينظر؛ إن تولى الغارس تعاهد الأرض الموقوفة فالأشجار للوقف لأن هذا من جملة التعاهد، وإن لم يتول فهي للغارس وعليه قلعها لأنه ليس له هذه الولاية ولو غرس على طريق العامة أو على شط نهر العامة أو على شط حوض القرية فالشجرة للغارس وله قلعها لأنه ليس له ولاية على العامة اهـ. وفي الخانية : لو غرس الواقف للأرض شجراً فيها قالوا : إن غرس من غلة الوقف أو من مال نفسه لكن ذكر أنه غرس للوقف يكون للوقف وإن لم يذكر شيئاً وقد غرس من مال نفسه يكون له ولورثته من بعده ولا يكون وقفاً، وإذا صح وقف الشجرة تبعاً لأصلها فإن كان ينتفع بأوراقها وأثمارها فإنه لا يقطع أصلها إلا أن تفسد أغصانها، ولو كان لا ينتفع بأوراقها ولا بأثمارها فإنه يقطع ويتصدق بها مسجد فيه شجرة التفاح قال بعضهم يباح للقوم أن يفطروا بهذا التفاح والصحيح أنه لا يباح لأن ذلك صار وقفاً للمسجد يصرف إلى عمارته. شجرة على طريق المارة جعلت وقفاً على المارة يباح تناول ثمرها للمارة ويستوي فيه الفقير والغني. ولو كانت الثمار على أشجار رباط المارة. قال أبو القاسم : أرجو أن يكون النزال في سعة من تناولها إلا أن يعلم أن غارسها جعلها للفقراء. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : إذا لم يكن الرجل من ساكني الرباط فالأحوط له أن يحترز من تناولها إلا أن تكون ثماراً لا قيمة لها كالتوت اهـ.
Book of Waqf. Chapter on waqf of trees. It is said in al-Zahiriyya: If one plants a tree and makes it a waqf, if he plants it in non-waqf land, it is not free from either that he makes it a waqf with its place in the land or not. If he makes it a waqf with its place in the land, it is valid, subsidiary to the land by virtue of connection. If he makes it a waqf without its root, it is not valid. If it is in waqf land and he makes it a waqf for that same purpose, it is permissible as in the case of a building. If he makes it a waqf for another purpose, then there is the disagreement mentioned earlier. In al-Muhit: A man who plants in a mosque, it belongs to the mosque because it is like a building in the mosque. Similarly, if he builds on waqf land or installs a door, if at the time of construction he intends it as a waqf because he makes it a waqf, and the building becomes waqf subsidiary to other (land), it is permissible. If he does not intend that, it is not waqf. If he plants in land waqf for a ribat, it is considered: if the planter undertakes the care of the waqf land, the trees are for the waqf; if he does not, they belong to him and he must remove them. If he plants on a public road, public riverbank, or village pond bank, the tree belongs to the planter and he may remove it. In al-Khaniyya: If the waqf founder plants trees in the land, they said: if he plants from the waqf produce or his own money but states he planted for the waqf, it is for the waqf. If he says nothing and planted from his own money, it belongs to him and his heirs, not waqf. If the waqf of the tree is valid subsidiary to its origin, if its leaves and fruits are beneficial, its trunk is not cut unless its branches spoil. If neither leaves nor fruits are beneficial, it is cut and given in charity. A mosque with an apple tree: some say it is permissible for people to break their fast with these apples, but the correct view is that it is not permissible because it has become waqf for the mosque to be spent on its maintenance. A tree on a public road made waqf for passersby, its fruit is permissible for them, rich and poor alike. If the fruits are on trees of a ribat for passersby, Abu al-Qasim said: I hope that the residents are at ease to eat them unless they know the planter intended them for the poor. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: If one is not a resident of the ribat, it is more cautious to refrain from eating them unless they are fruits of negligible value like mulberries.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rulings of trees planted in waqf land. It opens by stating various scenarios from al-Zahiriyya: if a tree is planted in non-waqf land and the planter designates it as waqf, it is valid only if he also includes its place in the land, because the tree is subsidiary to the land by physical connection. If he donates only the tree without its root, it is invalid. For trees in waqf land, if the planter intends the tree for the same waqf purpose, it is valid, akin to building on waqf land. If he intends a different purpose, there is disagreement among scholars. The text then quotes al-Muhit: planting in a mosque belongs to the mosque, similar to building. However, intention matters: if the planter intends it as waqf, it becomes so; otherwise not. For land waqf for a ribat (lodge), the tree becomes waqf only if the planter takes on the care of the land (tawalla 'ahd al-ard). Without that authority, the tree belongs to the planter and he must remove it. On public land (roads, riverbanks), the tree belongs to the planter and he may remove it because he has no authority over the public. From al-Khaniyya: if a waqf founder plants trees, the source of funds and intention matter. If he uses waqf income or his own money but states he planted for the waqf, it is waqf. Without such statement, if using his own money, it is his private property. The passage then discusses cutting trees: if the tree is beneficial (leaves/fruits), its trunk is not cut unless branches spoil; if useless, it is cut and given in charity. The example of an apple tree in a mosque: the correct view is that it is waqf for mosque maintenance, not for people to eat freely. For a tree on a public road made waqf for passersby, everyone, rich and poor, may eat its fruit. For trees in a ribat for passersby, Abu al-Qasim permits residents to eat unless the planter intended for the poor; Abu al-Layth advises caution for non-residents unless the fruit is of no value like mulberries.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): An endowment of property for charitable purposes, where the property is alienated from ownership and its usufruct is dedicated.
غرس (ghars): Planting a tree, often considered a form of improvement to land.
تبعية (tabe'iyya): The legal principle that something subsidiary follows the main object in ruling.
اتصال (ittisal): Physical connection; in this context, the tree's roots attaching it to the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person plants a tree in non-waqf land and declares it waqf without including its place in the land?
A) The waqf is valid.
B) The waqf is invalid.
C) It depends on the type of tree.
D) It is valid if the tree is fruit-bearing.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a tree waqf hinges on physical attachment to land and the planter's intention; without both, the tree remains private property.

Waqf — Endowment
intermediate

Tenant's Right to Fruits from Waqf Trees and Sale of Waqf Trees
Ruling on tenant's fruit consumption and sale of waqf trees with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi rules regarding a tenant's right to fruits from waqf trees and the conditions for selling waqf trees, and APPLY them to novel scenarios.

Core Idea
Tenant has no right to waqf fruits; mutawalli manages them. Waqf trees are inalienable unless they are non-fruit-bearing (like produce) or cut.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two practical questions that often arise in waqf management: may a tenant of a waqf property consume fruits from its trees, and under what circumstances may waqf trees be sold? The answers stem from the fundamental rule that waqf property is removed from private ownership—its corpus is inviolable and its usufruct belongs to the beneficiaries as designated by the waqif. We will examine the proofs and the school's position on these issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, identify the act in question: a tenant eating fruits from trees on a waqf house when the waqif's condition is unknown. State the ruling according to the text: it is not permissible; the mutawalli must take the fruits and spend them on waqf.
The ruling is based on the principle that waqf property is not owned by anyone and its usufruct belongs to the designated beneficiaries.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites several proofs: the hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) to Umar (r) 'give charity with its origin, not sold nor inherited', and the consensus of the jurists that waqf is not owned. Identify these proofs and note that the hadith establishes the inalienability of waqf corpus.
The hadith is a strong dalil for the prohibition of selling waqf trees.
3
Analyze the exceptions
The passage mentions an exception: non-fruit-bearing trees may be sold because they are considered like produce (ghalla). Fruit-bearing trees may only be sold after being cut. Trace the reasoning: the sale of produce is permissible, and these trees are akin to the waqf's income.
This exception is from al-Bazzaziyya quoting al-Fadli.
4
Apply the rule to mortgage and rent
The text extends the principle: waqf cannot be mortgaged. If a mutawalli improperly sells waqf property, the buyer must pay equivalent rent (ujr al-mithl) for the period of use. Note the difference of opinion from al-Qunyah about whether ujr al-mithl is due when the waqf is not properly established.
Al-Ramli favors the obligation of ujr al-mithl.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of the Hanafi position: a tenant who consumes fruits must compensate the waqf. In some Shafi'i views, the tenant might be allowed to eat, but according to Hanafis, the tenant's right is limited to the accommodation only.
This distinction affects real-world waqf management.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وقعت حادثة أن المستأجر للدار الموقوفة المشتملة على الأشجار هل له أن يأكل من ثمارها إذا لم يعلم شرط الواقف فيها ؟ وفي الحاوي وما غرس في المساجد من الأشجار وفاعله حكر كفرح. وأقول : والأرض المحتكرة هي التي وقف بناؤها ولم توقف هي كأن استأجر أرضاً للبناء عليها وبنى فيها ثم وقف البناء ؛ كذا رأيت لبعض الشافعية. وأقول : الأرض للاحتكار أعم من أن تكون وقفاً أو ملكاً والاحتكار في العرف إجارة يقصد بها منع الغير واستبقاء الانتفاع بالأرض. قالوا : لو بنى على أرض مقررة للاحتكار فباع البناء لا شفعة فيه لأنه من قسم المنقول قوله : (أن تولى الغارس تعاهد الأرض) أي بأن كان له ولاية عليها وعبارة الإسعاف أظهر : فلو غرس رباطي شجرة في وقف الرباط وتعاهدها حتى كبرت ولم يذكر وقت الغرس أنها المثمرة إن غرس للسبيل وهو الوقف على العامة كان لكل من دخل المسجد من المسلمين أن يأكل منها، وإن غرس للمسجد لا يجوز صرفها إلا إلى مصالح المسجد الأهم فالأهم كسائر الوقوف، وكذا إن لم يعلم غرض الغارس اهـ ومقتضاه في البيت الموقوف إذا لم يعرف الشرط أن يأخذها المتولي ليبيعها ويصرفها في مصالح الوقف ولا يجوز للمستأجر الأكل منها. وفي القنية : يجوز للمستأجرين غرس الأشجار والكروم في الأراضي الموقوفة إذا لم يضر بالأرض بدون صريح الإذن من المتولي دون حفر الحياض. وإنما يحل للمتولي الإذن فيما يزيد الوقف به خيراً. قال مصنفها قلت : وهذا إذا لم يكن لهم حق قرار العمارة فيها، أما إذا كان لا يحرم الحفر والغرس لوجود الإذن في مثلها اهـ.
وفي فتح القدير : وسئل أبو القاسم الصفار عن شجرة وقف يبس بعضها وبقي بعضها فقال : ما يبس منها فسبيله سبيل غلتها وما بقي متروك على حالها .اهـ. وفي البزازية وقال الأشجار الموقوفة مع الأرض لا يجوز قبل القلع كبيع الأرض. وقال أيضاً: إن الفضلي : وبيع لم تكن مثمرة يجوز بيعها قبل القلع أيضاً لأنه غلتها والمثمرة لا تباع إلا بعد القلع كبناء الوقف اهـ قوله : (ولا يملك الوقف بإجماع الفقهاء كما نقله في فتح القدير، ولقوله عليه السلام لعمر رضي الله عنه تصدق بأصلها لا تباع ولا تورث ولأنه باللزوم خرج عن ملك الواقف وبلا ملك لا يتمكن من البيع أفاد بمنع تمليكه وتملكه منع رهنه فلا يجوز للمتولي رهنه. قال في الخانية : المتولي إذا رهن أرض الوقف بدين لا يصح وكذلك أهل الجماعة إذا رهنوا، فإن سكن المرتهن الدار قال بعضهم عليه أجر المثل، سواء كانت الدار معدة للاستغلال أو لم تكن نظراً للوقف، وكذلك متولي المسجد إذا باع منزلاً موقوفاً على المسجد فسكنها المشتري ثم عزل هذا المتولي وولي غيره فادعى الثاني المنزل على المشتري وأبطل القاضي بيع المتولي وسلم الدار إلى المتولي الثاني فعلى المشتري أجر المثل اهـ . ولا فرق بين أن يكون البائع المتولي أو غيره بل وجوب أجر المثل فيما إذا باعه غير المتولي بالأولى. وذكر في القنية أنه لا يجب وهو للرباط. قال الفقيه أبو جعفر إن كان إليه ولاية الأرض الموقوفة فالشجرة وقف وإلا فهي له وله رفعها. قوله : (ومقتضاه في البيت الموقوف إلى قوله (ليبيعها أي ليبيع الأثمار لا الأشجار فإنه لا يجوز بيعها لاحتمال أن غرض الغارس وقفها، وسيأتي في المسألة الرابعة عشرة عن الظهيرية : شجرة وقف في دار وقف خربت ليس للمتولي أن يبيع الشجرة ويعمر الدار ولكن يكري الدار ويستعين بالكراء على عمارة الدار لا بالشجرة .اهـ وهذا مع خراب الدار فكيف يجوز بيعها مع عمارتها؟ ثم الظاهر أنه يدفعها للمستأجر معاملة قال في الإسعاف ولو كان في أرض الوقف شجر فدفعه معاملة بالنصف مثلاً جاز اهـ. فتأمل قوله : فسكنها المشتري) قال المقدسي : لعله اتفاقي بل وضع يده عليه كاف. قوله: (وذكر في القنية أنه لا يجب) ونصه سم مح : سكن الدار سنين يدعى ثم استحقت للوقف بالبيئة العادلة لا يجب عليه أجر ما مضى .اهـ. قال الرملي : ما في القنية مذهب الملك
And an incident occurred: the tenant of a waqf house containing trees, may he eat from their fruits if he does not know the condition of the waqif? In al-Hawi: whatever is planted in mosques, the planter has a right of preemption (hukr) like in land. I say: the land held on hire (muhtakara) is that whose building is waqf but the land is not, as if one rents land to build, then builds and then makes the building waqf. I saw this from some Shafi'is. I say: land for hire is broader, whether waqf or owned. Hire in custom is a lease intended to prevent others and retain usufruct. They said: if one builds on land designated for hire and sells the building, there is no preemption because it is movable. His saying: (if the planter undertakes the care of the land) meaning he has authority over it. The expression of al-Is'af is clearer: if a ribat dweller plants a tree in the waqf of the ribat and cares for it until it grows, and he did not mention at the time of planting whether it is fruit-bearing, if he planted for the public (sabeel) which is waqf for the public, every Muslim entering the mosque may eat from it. If he planted for the mosque, its use is only for the mosque's needs, the most important first, like other waqfs. Similarly if his intention is unknown. And its implication for a waqf house when the condition is unknown is that the mutawalli takes it to sell it and spend on waqf needs, and the tenant may not eat from it. In al-Qunyah: tenants may plant trees and vines in waqf land if it does not harm the land, without explicit permission from the mutawalli, but may not dig wells. The mutawalli may permit only what increases the waqf's benefit. Its author said: this is if they have no right of settled tenancy; otherwise, digging and planting are not prohibited due to existing permission. In Fath al-Qadir: Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar was asked about a waqf tree part of which dried up and part remained, he said: what dried up is like its produce, and what remains is left as is. In al-Bazzaziyya: waqf trees with land may not be sold before being cut, like sale of land? Also: al-Fadli said: non-fruit-bearing trees may be sold before cutting because it is their produce; fruit-bearing trees are not sold except after cutting, like waqf building. His saying: waqf is not owned by consensus, as transmitted in Fath al-Qadir, and due to his (peace be upon him) saying to Umar (r) 'give charity with its origin, not sold nor inherited', and because by binding it leaves the ownership of the waqif, and without ownership one cannot sell, so it prevents its ownership and mortgaging. The mutawalli may not mortgage it. In al-Khaniyya: if the mutawalli mortgages waqf land for a debt, it is invalid, and similarly if the community mortgages. If the mortgagee lives in the house, some say he must pay equivalent rent, whether the house was prepared for investment or not, out of consideration for the waqf. Similarly, if the mutawalli of a mosque sells a house waqf for the mosque, and the buyer lives in it, then this mutawalli is removed and another appointed, and the second claims the house from the buyer and the judge nullifies the sale and delivers the house to the second mutawalli, then the buyer must pay equivalent rent. There is no difference between the seller being the mutawalli or another, rather obligation of equivalent rent is even more so when sold by a non-mutawalli. It is mentioned in al-Qunyah that it is not obligatory. And that is for the ribat. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: if he has authority over the waqf land, the tree is waqf; otherwise it is his and he may remove it. His saying: (and its implication in the waqf house until his saying 'to sell it') means to sell the fruits, not the trees, for it is not permissible to sell them because of the possibility that the planter intended them as waqf. It will come in the fourteenth issue from al-Zahiriyya: a waqf tree in a ruined waqf house, the mutawalli may not sell the tree to repair the house, but may rent the house and use the rent for repair, not the tree. This is when the house is ruined, how then can it be sold when it is sound? Then apparently he gives it to the tenant as mu'amala. It is said in al-Is'af: if there are trees in waqf land, he may give them as mu'amala for half, etc. Consider his saying: 'then the buyer lived in it' said al-Maqdisi: perhaps it is by agreement, rather his possession is sufficient. His saying: 'and it is mentioned in al-Qunyah that it is not obligatory' and its text: he lived in the house for years, then it was claimed and proven to be waqf by just evidence, he is not liable for past rent. Al-Ramli said: what is in al-Qunyah is the madhhab of property?
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses several practical issues regarding waqf trees and the rights of tenants and mutawallis. It begins with an incident: a tenant of a waqf house with trees asks whether he may eat from the fruits if he does not know the waqif's condition. The text then discusses various scenarios. The key ruling is that if the tenant does not know the waqif's intention, the mutawalli should take the fruits, sell them, and use the proceeds for waqf maintenance; the tenant may not eat them. This is based on the principle that waqf property is not owned by anyone and its usufruct must go to the designated beneficiaries. The passage then distinguishes between public waqf (sabeel) and private waqf. For example, if someone plants a tree in a mosque without specifying, if intended for the public everyone may eat, if for the mosque it must be used for its needs. Later, it discusses the sale of waqf trees: generally, waqf trees cannot be sold because the corpus is inalienable. However, non-fruit-bearing trees (not productive) may be sold because they are considered like produce (ghalla). Fruit-bearing trees can only be sold after being cut. The passage also emphasizes that waqf cannot be mortgaged, and if a mutawalli improperly sells waqf property, the buyer must pay equivalent rent (ujr al-mithl) for the period of use. There is also a mention of the concept of land held on hire (muhtakara) which is like a long-term lease of waqf land for building. The text ends with a debate on whether the tenant must pay back rent if the waqf is reclaimed; al-Qunyah says no, but al-Ramli says this is the madhhab of ownership (perhaps indicating it is disputed). Overall, the passage reinforces the inviolability of waqf property and the strict rules governing its use.

Key Terms
مستأجر (musta'jir): Tenant who rents waqf property.
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Equivalent rent, the market rate for similar property, imposed on one who uses waqf property without right.
غلة (ghalla): Produce or income from waqf property, including fruits from trees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a tenant lives in a waqf house with fruit trees and the waqif's condition is unknown, what should the mutawalli do?
A) Allow the tenant to eat the fruits.
B) Take the fruits and sell them for waqf maintenance.
C) Leave the fruits for anyone to take.
D) Give the fruits to the poor.

Key Takeaway
Waqf trees are part of the inalienable corpus; tenants have no right to their fruit, and only non-fruit-bearing or cut trees may be sold as produce.

Waqf — Endowment
beginner
6 min
Fruit from Waqf Trees: Public vs. Mosque Benefit
Ruling on fruit from waqf trees based on planter's intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating fruit from waqf trees based on the planter's intention.

Core Idea
The use of fruit from waqf trees depends on the waqf's purpose: public or specific.

This lesson examines the first part of a passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on trees in waqf. It outlines the Hanafi position on who may benefit from fruit when trees are planted on waqf land attached to a mosque, depending on whether the planter intended the benefit for the general public or specifically for the mosque.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage explicitly mentions two cases: trees planted for 'al-sabil' (public benefit) and trees planted for 'al-masjid' (the mosque). Note that 'al-sabil' here refers to waqf for the general public.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For public benefit: every Muslim entering the mosque may eat the fruit. For mosque benefit: the fruit must be spent on the needs of the mosque, prioritizing the most important needs.
3
Apply the underlying principle
The fruit follows the waqf's purpose. If the waqf is for public benefit, the fruit is for public; if for mosque, the fruit is for mosque maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثمرة إن غرس للسبيل وهو الوقف على العامة كان لكل من دخل المسجد من المسلمين أن يأكل منها، وإن غرس للمسجد لا يجوز صرفها إلا إلى مصالح المسجد الأهم فالأهم كسائر الوقوف، وكذا إن لم يعلم غرض الغارس اهـ ومقتضاه في البيت الموقوف إذا لم يعرف الشرط أن يأخذها المتولي ليبيعها ويصرفها في مصالح الوقف ولا يجوز للمستأجر الأكل منها. وفي القنية : يجوز للمستأجرين غرس الأشجار والكروم في الأراضي الموقوفة إذا لم يضر بالأرض بدون صريح الإذن من المتولي دون حفر الحياض. وإنما يحل للمتولي الإذن فيما يزيد الوقف به خيراً. قال مصنفها قلت : وهذا إذا لم يكن لهم حق قرار العمارة فيها، أما إذا كان لا يحرم الحفر والغرس لوجود الإذن في مثلها اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : وسئل أبو القاسم الصفار عن شجرة وقف يبس بعضها وبقي بعضها فقال : ما يبس منها فسبيله سبيل غلتها وما بقي متروك على حالها .اهـ. وفي البزازية وقال الأشجار الموقوفة مع الأرض لا يجوز قبل القلع كبيع الأرض. وقال أيضاً: إن، الفضلي : وبيع لم تكن مثمرة يجوز بيعها قبل القلع أيضاً لأنه غلتها والمثمرة لا تباع إلا بعد القلع كبناء الوقف اهـ
The fruit-bearing tree, if planted for the public benefit (which is waqf for the general public), then every Muslim who enters the mosque may eat from it. If planted for the mosque, it is not permissible to spend [its fruit] except for the needs of the mosque, the most important first, like other endowments. Likewise, if the planter's intention is unknown, the trustee takes it to sell it and spend its proceeds on the affairs of the waqf, and it is not permissible for the lessee to eat from it. In al-Qunyah: It is permissible for lessees to plant trees and grapevines in waqf lands if it does not harm the land, without explicit permission from the trustee, but not digging cisterns. The trustee may only authorize what increases the waqf in goodness. Its author said: I say: This is if they have no right to settle in the [waqf] buildings; but if they do, then digging and planting are not prohibited due to the existence of implicit permission for such. In Fath al-Qadir: Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar was asked about a waqf tree, part of which withered and part remained, and he said: What withered of it, its way is the way of its income, and what remains is left as it is. In al-Bazzaziyya: It is said: Trees endowed along with land cannot be sold before uprooting, like selling land. And he also said: ... al-Fadali: And a non-fruit-bearing tree can be sold before uprooting as well because it is its income, whereas a fruit-bearing tree is not sold except after uprooting, like the building of a waqf.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings concerning trees (ashjar) that are part of a waqf. The first ruling distinguishes between trees planted for public benefit (like for a mosque's courtyard) versus those planted specifically for the mosque: if for the public, anyone in the mosque may eat the fruit; if for the mosque, the fruit goes to mosque maintenance. If the purpose is unknown, the trustee may sell the fruit for waqf expenses, and the lessee may not eat it. The second ruling, from al-Qunyah, permits lessees to plant trees in waqf land as long as it does not harm the land, even without explicit permission, but digging cisterns is not allowed. The trustee may only authorize actions that benefit the waqf. The author of al-Qunyah clarifies that if lessees have no right to settle, they may not dig or plant; but if they have such right, it is allowed by implicit permission. The third ruling, from Fath al-Qadir, deals with a tree that partially dries: the dry part is treated as income (like rent), and the living part remains as waqf. The fourth ruling, from al-Bazzaziyya, states that trees endowed together with land cannot be sold before uprooting, like the land itself. However, a non-fruit-bearing tree can be sold before uprooting because it is itself the income (like rent from land), whereas a fruit-bearing tree is sold only after uprooting, analogous to a waqf building which cannot be sold while standing.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): An endowment; property withheld from ownership and its usufruct dedicated to charitable or religious purposes.
المتولي (al-mutawalli): The trustee or administrator of the waqf, appointed to manage its affairs.
القلع (al-qal'): Uprooting or removing trees from the ground, a condition for selling them when they are part of the waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on fruit from trees planted for the mosque?
A) Any Muslim may eat.
B) Only the trustee may eat.
C) It must be spent on mosque needs.
D) It is given to the poor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on fruit from waqf trees depends on whether the planter intended public benefit or mosque benefit; the fruit follows the waqf purpose.

When the Planter's Intention is Unknown
Ruling on fruit when planter's intention is unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the role of the trustee in disposing of fruit from waqf trees when the planter's intention is unknown.

Core Idea
An unknown intention leads to trustee intervention: sell fruit and use for waqf.

This lesson covers the continuation of the previous ruling. When the planter's intention for a waqf tree is unknown, the trustee (mutawalli) must take charge. He sells the fruit and spends the proceeds on the waqf's affairs. The lessee of the waqf land is not allowed to eat the fruit.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition
The previous ruling assumes the planter's intention is known (public or mosque). Here, the intention is unknown.
2
Identify the trustee's duty
The trustee takes the fruit, sells it, and spends the proceeds on the waqf's affairs (masalih al-waqf).
3
Recognize the lessee's prohibition
The lessee (musta'jir) is not allowed to eat from the fruit, even if he is the one cultivating the land.

Lessees Planting Trees and Selling Waqf Trees
Compilation of additional rulings on waqf trees: planting by lessees, disposal of withered parts, and sale conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on lessees planting trees in waqf land and the conditions for selling waqf trees.

Core Idea
Lessees may plant trees without explicit permission if no harm, but fruit-bearing trees can only be sold after uprooting.

This lesson covers the remaining rulings from the passage. It addresses the rights of lessees to plant trees on waqf land, the trustee's authority to permit beneficial actions, and the crucial distinction between fruit-bearing and non-fruit-bearing trees when it comes to selling them as part of the waqf.

Guided Path
1
Understand the lessee's planting right
According to al-Qunyah, lessees may plant trees and grapevines on waqf land if it does not harm the land, without explicit permission from the trustee. Digging cisterns is not allowed.
This is a permissive rule to encourage cultivation.
2
Note the trustee's authorization limit
The trustee may only authorize actions that increase the waqf in goodness (khayr).
3
Distinguish the right to settle
If lessees have a right to settle (right of qarar al-'imarah), then digging and planting are implicitly permitted.
This is a clarification from the author of al-Qunyah.
4
Apply the ruling on withered trees
From Fath al-Qadir: if part of a waqf tree withers, the withered part is treated as income (rent) and can be sold; the living part remains waqf.
5
Apply the ruling on selling waqf trees
From al-Bazzaziyya: trees endowed with land cannot be sold before uprooting. However, non-fruit-bearing trees can be sold before uprooting because they are considered income themselves, whereas fruit-bearing trees must be uprooted first, like a waqf building.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text

The Inalienability of Waqf: Ownership, Sale, and Mortgage
Establishes the inalienability of waqf with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that waqf property is not owned and identify which transactions are prohibited (sale, inheritance, mortgage) based on the proof from the hadith and ijma.

Core Idea
Waqf property is owned by Allah and permanently removed from human ownership—it cannot be sold, inherited, or mortgaged.

This passage begins with a fundamental principle: waqf is not owned by any person. This is established by consensus (ijma) and a clear hadith. From this principle, several legal consequences follow: the property cannot be sold, inherited, or used as collateral. Even the trustee has no authority to mortgage waqf land. Understanding this bedrock ruling is essential before moving to the more detailed issues of compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Locate the statement 'ولا يملك الوقف' (waqf is not owned). This is the central hukm of the passage. The text immediately supports it with multiple proofs.
The ruling is absolute—no one can own waqf property.
2
Examine the first proof: ijma
The passage states there is consensus of the jurists (بإجماع الفقهاء) on this point. This is a strong evidence (dalil qat'i).
Consensus is a primary source of law.
3
Examine the second proof: hadith
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Umar: 'تصدق بأصلها لا تباع ولا تورث' (Give charity with its capital, it is not sold nor inherited). The explicit negation of sale and inheritance shows that the property is removed from circulation.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear (dalala qat'i).
4
Derive the consequences
Because waqf lacks ownership, it cannot be the subject of a sale or mortgage. The trustee cannot mortgage it, and any such transaction is void. This extends to any transfer of ownership.
The principle of luzum (binding effect) removes the founder's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يملك الوقف بإجماع الفقهاء كما نقله في فتح القدير، ولقوله عليه السلام لعمر رضي الله عنه تصدق بأصلها لا تباع ولا تورث ولأنه باللزوم خرج عن ملك الواقف وبلا ملك لا يتمكن من البيع أفاد بمنع تمليكه وتملكه منع رهنه فلا يجوز للمتولي رهنه. قال في الخانية : المتولي إذا رهن أرض الوقف بدين لا يصح وكذلك أهل الجماعة إذا رهنوا، فإن سكن المرتهن الدار قال بعضهم عليه أجر المثل، سواء كانت الدار معدة للاستغلال أو لم تكن نظراً للوقف، وكذلك متولي المسجد إذا باع منزلاً موقوفاً على المسجد فسكنها المشتري ثم عزل هذا المتولي وولي غيره فادعى الثاني المنزل على المشتري وأبطل القاضي بيع المتولي وسلم الدار إلى المتولي الثاني فعلى المشتري أجر المثل اهـ . ولا فرق بين أن يكون البائع المتولي أو غيره بل وجوب أجر المثل فيما إذا باعه غير المتولي بالأولى. وذكر في القنية أنه لا يجب وهو للرباط. قال الفقيه أبو جعفر إن كان إليه ولاية الأرض الموقوفة فالشجرة وقف وإلا فهي له وله رفعها. قوله : (ومقتضاه في البيت الموقوف إلى قوله (ليبيعها أي ليبيع الأثمار لا الأشجار فإنه لا يجوز بيعها لاحتمال أن غرض الغارس ،وقفها، وسيأتي في المسألة الرابعة عشرة عن الظهيرية : شجرة وقف في دار وقف خربت ليس للمتولي أن يبيع الشجرة ويعمر الدار ولكن يكري الدار ويستعين بالكراء على عمارة الدار لا بالشجرة .اهـ وهذا مع خراب الدار فكيف يجوز بيعها مع عمارتها؟ ثم الظاهر أنه يدفعها للمستأجر معاملة قال في الإسعاف ولو كان في أرض الوقف شجر فدفعه معاملة بالنصف مثلاً جاز اهـ. فتأمل قوله : فسكنها المشتري) قال المقدسي : لعله اتفاقي بل وضع يده عليه كاف. قوله: (وذكر في القنية أنه لا يجب) ونصه سم مح : سكن الدار سنين يدعى ثم استحقت للوقف بالبيئة العادلة لا يجب عليه أجر ما مضى .اهـ. قال الرملي : ما في القنية مذهب ضعيف لأنه وإن سكن بتأويل الملك يجب أجر المثل مراعاة للوقف. وفي القنية : سكنها ثم بان أنها وقف أو لصغير يجب أجر المثل بخلاف ما مر . قوله : (بخلاف ما مر الإشارة إلى عدم الوجوب في العبارة التي نقلناها عنه.
His statement: (Waqf is not owned) by consensus of the jurists, as transmitted in Fath al-Qadir, and because of his statement (peace be upon him) to Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Give charity with its capital, it is not sold nor inherited,' and because by being binding it leaves the ownership of the founder, and without ownership one cannot effect a sale. This implies prevention of its being owned and of owning it, thus preventing its mortgage, so it is not permissible for the trustee to mortgage it. It is stated in al-Khaniyya: If the trustee mortgages waqf land for a debt, it is not valid, and likewise if the community mortgages it. If the mortgagee lives in the house, some say he owes the equivalent rent (ujrat al-mithl), whether the house is prepared for rent or not, out of consideration for the waqf. Likewise, if the trustee of a mosque sells a house endowed to the mosque and the buyer lives in it, then this trustee is removed and another is appointed, and the second trustee claims the house from the buyer, and the judge voids the sale of the first trustee and delivers the house to the second trustee, then the buyer must pay the equivalent rent. There is no difference between the seller being the trustee or someone else; rather, the obligation of the equivalent rent is even more applicable when sold by someone other than the trustee. It is mentioned in al-Qunyah that it is not obligatory, and it is for the ribat. Abu Ja'far said: If he has authority over the waqf land, then the tree is waqf; otherwise it is his and he may remove it. His statement: (And its implication in the waqf house until his statement) (to sell it) i.e., to sell the fruits, not the trees, because it is not permissible to sell them due to the possibility that the planter's intention was to make them waqf. It will come in the fourteenth issue from al-Zahiriyya: A waqf tree in a ruined waqf house: the trustee may not sell the tree and repair the house, but rather he should rent the house and use the rent for its repair, not the tree. This is when the house is ruined, so how can it be permissible to sell it when it is sound? Then it is apparent that he should give it to the lessee as a mu'amala (sharecropping). It is stated in al-Is'af: If there are trees in waqf land, and he gives it as mu'amala for half, etc., it is permissible. Reflect on his statement: 'so the buyer lived in it.' al-Maqdisi said: Perhaps it is coincidental; rather, his placing his hand on it is sufficient. His statement: (And it is mentioned in al-Qunyah that it is not obligatory) and its text: 'He lived in the house for years by claim, then it was proved to be waqf by just evidence, he does not owe rent for the past.' al-Ramli said: The view in al-Qunyah is weak, because even if he lived under the assumption of ownership, the equivalent rent is obligatory out of consideration for the waqf. And in al-Qunyah: If he lived in it and then it became clear that it was waqf or belonged to a minor, the equivalent rent is obligatory, unlike what passed. His statement: (Unlike what passed refers to the absence of obligation in the expression we transmitted from him.)
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage contains a fundamental ruling on the legal nature of waqf: it cannot be owned by anyone, based on ijma (consensus) and the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to Umar: 'Give charity with its capital, it is not sold nor inherited.' Because waqf becomes binding (luzum), it leaves the possession of the founder, and since no one owns it, it cannot be sold or mortgaged. The trustee therefore cannot mortgage waqf land; any such mortgage is invalid. The passage then addresses a consequence: if someone occupies waqf property under a void sale (e.g., from a trustee who had no right to sell), the occupant must pay ujrat al-mithl (equivalent market rent) for the period of occupancy, regardless of whether the property is usually rented out. This is the position of the majority, as stated in al-Khaniyya. A minority view from al-Qunyah says no rent is due, but al-Ramli explicitly calls this view weak (da'if) and affirms that rent must be paid, as a precaution for the waqf (ri'ayatan lil-waqf). The passage also includes a detail about trees: if a tree on waqf land is owned by the planter, and the planter has no authority over the land, the tree is not waqf and he may remove it. But if the planter had authority (wilayah) over the land, the tree becomes waqf. The overall thrust is that waqf is protected from loss, and any transaction that would diminish it is invalid; those who benefit from it unjustly must compensate.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): Equivalent market rent; the standard rent that would be paid for similar property in similar conditions.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Binding effect; once waqf is declared, it becomes permanent and irrevocable.
الرهن (al-rahn): Mortgage or pledge of property as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a direct consequence of the fact that waqf is not owned?
A) The trustee can sell the property if it is profitable
B) The waqf property can be mortgaged with a court order
C) The waqf property cannot be sold or inherited
D) The waqf can be transferred to another charity

Key Takeaway
Waqf is owned by Allah and permanently removed from human ownership, making it inalienable.

Rent Obligation for Occupants of Waqf Property under Void Transactions
Discusses intra-Hanafi dispute on rent obligation for occupant of waqf under void sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the two Hanafi positions on whether an occupant of waqf property under a void sale must pay ujrat al-mithl, and apply the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling.

Core Idea
Even if you bought waqf property in good faith, you must pay market rent for the time you lived there.

After establishing the inalienability of waqf, the passage turns to a practical problem: what happens if someone occupies waqf property under a void sale? The occupant often claims good faith, but the waqf must be protected. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether rent must be paid for the past period. This lesson walks through the dispute and the resolution by later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage discusses two cases: (1) a mortgagee who occupies waqf house under an invalid mortgage, and (2) a buyer who occupies a waqf house sold by a trustee and later the sale is invalidated by a judge.
In both, the occupant had no valid right to occupy.
2
State the majority position
The majority, including Abu Hanifa and the later scholars, hold that the occupant must pay ujrat al-mithl (equivalent rent) for the entire period of occupancy. This is based on protecting the waqf from loss (ri'ayatan lil-waqf).
The obligation applies whether the property is usually rented out or not.
3
State the minority position
A minority opinion, attributed to some early Hanafis and reported in al-Qunyah, says no rent is owed if the occupant entered under a claim of ownership and the sale was not yet invalidated. They argue based on the maxim 'لا ضمان على من دخل بإذن' (no liability on one who enters with permission).
This view is considered weak (da'if).
4
Analyze the tarjih (preponderance)
Al-Ramli explicitly rejects the minority view as weak, stating that even with an assumption of ownership, rent is obligatory to protect the waqf. He also notes that if the property is later proven to be waqf, the rent is due regardless of the occupant's good faith.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is the obligation of ujrat al-mithl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يملك الوقف بإجماع الفقهاء كما نقله في فتح القدير، ولقوله عليه السلام لعمر رضي الله عنه تصدق بأصلها لا تباع ولا تورث ولأنه باللزوم خرج عن ملك الواقف وبلا ملك لا يتمكن من البيع أفاد بمنع تمليكه وتملكه منع رهنه فلا يجوز للمتولي رهنه. قال في الخانية : المتولي إذا رهن أرض الوقف بدين لا يصح وكذلك أهل الجماعة إذا رهنوا، فإن سكن المرتهن الدار قال بعضهم عليه أجر المثل، سواء كانت الدار معدة للاستغلال أو لم تكن نظراً للوقف، وكذلك متولي المسجد إذا باع منزلاً موقوفاً على المسجد فسكنها المشتري ثم عزل هذا المتولي وولي غيره فادعى الثاني المنزل على المشتري وأبطل القاضي بيع المتولي وسلم الدار إلى المتولي الثاني فعلى المشتري أجر المثل اهـ . ولا فرق بين أن يكون البائع المتولي أو غيره بل وجوب أجر المثل فيما إذا باعه غير المتولي بالأولى. وذكر في القنية أنه لا يجب وهو للرباط. قال الفقيه أبو جعفر إن كان إليه ولاية الأرض الموقوفة فالشجرة وقف وإلا فهي له وله رفعها. قوله : (ومقتضاه في البيت الموقوف إلى قوله (ليبيعها أي ليبيع الأثمار لا الأشجار فإنه لا يجوز بيعها لاحتمال أن غرض الغارس ،وقفها، وسيأتي في المسألة الرابعة عشرة عن الظهيرية : شجرة وقف في دار وقف خربت ليس للمتولي أن يبيع الشجرة ويعمر الدار ولكن يكري الدار ويستعين بالكراء على عمارة الدار لا بالشجرة .اهـ وهذا مع خراب الدار فكيف يجوز بيعها مع عمارتها؟ ثم الظاهر أنه يدفعها للمستأجر معاملة قال في الإسعاف ولو كان في أرض الوقف شجر فدفعه معاملة بالنصف مثلاً جاز اهـ. فتأمل قوله : فسكنها المشتري) قال المقدسي : لعله اتفاقي بل وضع يده عليه كاف. قوله: (وذكر في القنية أنه لا يجب) ونصه سم مح : سكن الدار سنين يدعى ثم استحقت للوقف بالبيئة العادلة لا يجب عليه أجر ما مضى .اهـ. قال الرملي : ما في القنية مذهب ضعيف لأنه وإن سكن بتأويل الملك يجب أجر المثل مراعاة للوقف. وفي القنية : سكنها ثم بان أنها وقف أو لصغير يجب أجر المثل بخلاف ما مر . قوله : (بخلاف ما مر الإشارة إلى عدم الوجوب في العبارة التي نقلناها عنه.
