Judge's Ruling on Waqf Sales and Demolition Liability
Later Hanafi ruling on waqf sale and demolition liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between faskh and istibdal in waqf transactions and apply the later Hanafi position on judicial validation of waqf sales.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling on an unadjudicated waqf is istibdal, not faskh, and can validate its sale.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq combines two practical waqf scenarios: a lessee who demolishes a waqf building and a judge authorizing the sale of a waqf whose validity was never ruled upon. Both hinge on the judge's discretionary power and the concept of istibdal. Understanding when a judge's decree can override the general inalienability of waqf is essential for those dealing with waqf administration or disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, the fatwa from Qari' al-Hidayah about a lessee who demolishes a waqf house and converts it into a mill or oven. Second, al-Ramli's discussion on whether a judge can validate the sale of a waqf that was never adjudicated.
Both scenarios test the limits of waqf protection.
2
Analyze the demolition case
The lessee's action is a clear violation, but the judge has discretion: if the new use brings more benefit and rent, it may be allowed; if not, the lessee must restore and is punished.
The judge's ruling is based on maslahah (benefit) for the waqf.
3
Grasp al-Ramli's distinction between faskh and istibdal
Al-Ramli argues that selling a waqf that was never properly adjudicated is an exchange (istibdal), not a revocation (faskh). Faskh would nullify a prior binding ruling, whereas istibdal does not.
This distinction is key to the permissibility of the sale.
4
Apply the distinction to the judge's ruling
Since no prior ruling existed, the judge's decision is a new ijtihad in a disputed matter. According to later Hanafis, such a ruling resolves the conflict and validates the sale, provided conditions are met.
The judge's authority is not absolute; it depends on the ijtihad context.
5
Note the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the early view, the sale is void unconditionally; under the later view (mufta bihi), it is valid if the judge finds it beneficial and fulfills all conditions.
This has implications for ancient waqfs with lost documentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن هدم المشتري البناء الخ) في فتاوى قارئ الهداية : سئل إذا استأجر شخص داراً وقفاً من مؤجر شرعي ثم إنه هدمها بيده العادية وغير معالمها وجعلها طاحوناً أو فرناً أو غير ذلك، فهل يلزم المستأجر هدم ما بناه وإعادة العين الموقوفة كما كانت أولا؟ أجاب : ينظر القاضي في ذلك، إن كان ما غيرها إليه أنفع لجهة الوقف وأكثر ريعاً أخذ منه الأجرة وبقي ما عمر لجهة الوقف وهو متبرع بما أنفقه في العمارة الأجرة، فإن لم يكن أنفع لجهة الوقف ولا أكثر ريعاً ألزم ما صنع وإعادة الوقف إلى الصفة التي كان عليها بعد تعزيره بما يليق بحاله .اهـ. قوله : (قلت أنه في وقف لم يحكم بصحته ولزومه الخ) قال الرملي أقول : الذي يظهر الإطلاق لأن بيعه استبدال لا فسخ والاستبدال ليس فيه فسخ القضاء السابق حتى يمتنع ، فإذا رآه حاکم وقضى به بعد استكمال شرائطه فهو قضاء في محل مجتهد فيه والقضاء في مثله يرفع الخلاف فتأمل الفرق يظهر لك الحق وفرق بين الفسخ ولا يحسب له من بهدم
His statement: (If the buyer demolishes the building, etc.) in the Fatawa of Qari' al-Hidayah: He was asked: If a person rents a waqf house from a lawful lessor, then demolishes it with his own hand, erases its landmarks, and makes it into a mill or oven or something else, is it incumbent on the lessee to demolish what he built and return the waqf property to its original state or not? He answered: The judge looks into it. If what he changed it to is more beneficial for the waqf and yields more rent, he takes the rent from him and what he built remains for the waqf, and he is considered a voluntary donor of what he spent on construction; if it is not more beneficial for the waqf nor more productive, he is forced to undo what he did and return the waqf to its former condition after being punished appropriately. His statement: (I say that it is a waqf whose validity and bindingness has not been judged...) al-Ramli said: I say: The apparent view is generality, because its sale is an exchange (istibdal) not a revocation (faskh), and exchange does not involve revocation of a prior judgment to prevent it. So if a judge sees it and rules on it after completing its conditions, it is a ruling in a matter subject to ijtihad, and a ruling in such a case removes the disagreement. So reflect on the difference, the truth will become clear to you. And there is a difference between revocation and ... and he is not credited for the demolition.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected issues from later Hanafi sources. The first is a fatwa from Qari' al-Hidayah about a lessee who demolishes a waqf building and converts it to a different use (e.g., mill or oven). The ruling gives the judge discretion: if the new use is more beneficial and brings more rent to the waqf, it is allowed, and the lessee is considered a donor of the cost; if not, the lessee must restore the property and is punished. The second issue concerns the effect of a judge's ruling on the sale of a waqf that was never properly established (i.e., its validity and bindingness not yet ruled upon). Al-Ramli argues that such a sale is an 'istibdal' (exchange) rather than a 'faskh' (revocation of a prior ruling). Since there was no prior binding ruling that the property is waqf, the judge's ruling validating the sale is a new ijtihad that resolves disagreement. The passage ends abruptly but clearly distinguishes between faskh (which would nullify a prior judgment) and istibdal (which does not).

Key Terms
الاستبدال (al-istibdal): Exchange of waqf property for another asset; a substitute that preserves the waqf's value.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Revocation or annulment of a prior binding ruling; here, the nullification of the waqf status.
القضاء (al-qada): A judge's judicial decree or ruling, which can settle disputed matters and is binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ramli, what is the legal nature of a judge's ruling that validates the sale of a waqf whose status was never adjudicated?
A) It is a faskh (revocation) of the waqf
B) It is an istibdal (exchange) that does not revoke a prior ruling
C) It is absolutely void because waqf is inalienable
D) It is only valid if the waqf was created recently

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling can validate the sale of a waqf as an exchange (istibdal) when no prior binding ruling exists, but cannot revoke an established waqf (faskh).

Rulings on Occupying and Demolishing Waqf Property
Ruling statement on two waqf scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ujrat al-mithl for those occupying waqf under an invalid claim of ownership and explain the judge's options when a buyer demolishes a building on waqf land.

Core Idea
Occupying waqf under a mistaken ownership claim still requires paying market rent—the waqf's right prevails.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two critical rulings concerning waqf property: first, the obligation of ujrat al-mithl for someone who resides under a mistaken belief of ownership; second, the judge's discretion when a buyer demolishes a building on waqf land. Both rulings demonstrate the Hanafi school's robust protection of waqf assets, prioritizing the endowment over individual claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario
Read the passage from al-Khulasa and al-Qunyah: someone resides in a property under the interpretation that it is their own (ta'wil al-milk). Later it becomes clear that the property is waqf or belongs to a minor.
This is an occupation under a defective title, not outright usurpation.
2
Note the ruling on ujrat al-mithl
The text states that ujrat al-mithl (equivalent rent) is obligatory. The reason is to protect the waqf (or the minor's property). Even if the occupant had a plausible claim, the waqf's right to compensation takes precedence.
This differs from cases mentioned earlier where rent might not be due.
3
Identify the second scenario
From al-Muhit: a buyer purchases a property that includes a building on waqf land and then demolishes the building.
The building's status is in question—it may be considered part of the waqf.
4
Understand the judge's discretion
The judge may choose to hold the seller liable (making the sale valid retroactively) or hold the buyer liable (voiding the sale). If buyer is liable, the compensation goes to the waqf, not the beneficiaries.
This protects the waqf from loss and gives the judge flexibility to achieve justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفذ قال الخلاصة ضعيف لأنه وإن سكن بتأويل الملك يجب أجر المثل مراعاة للوقف. وفي القنية : سكنها ثم بان أنها وقف أو لصغير يجب أجر المثل بخلاف ما مر . وفي المحيط : فإن هدم المشتري البناء فالقاضي بالخيار إن شاء ضمن البائع قيمة البناء وإن شاء ضمن المشتري ; فإن ضمن البائع لأنه ملكه بالضمان فصار كأنه باع ملك نفسه، وإن ضمن المشتري لا ينفذ البيع بيعه ويملك المشتري بالبناء بالضمان ويكون الضمان للوقف لا للموقوف عليهم اهـ.
Enforced. He said in al-Khulasa: It is weak because if he resides under an interpretation of ownership, the equivalent rent is obligatory out of consideration for the waqf. In al-Qunyah: He resides in it, then it becomes clear that it is waqf or belongs to a minor, the equivalent rent is obligatory, contrary to what has passed. And in al-Muhit: If the buyer demolishes the building, the judge has the option: if he wishes, he holds the seller liable for the value of the building, and if he wishes, he holds the buyer liable. If he holds the seller liable, because he owned it through liability, it becomes as if he sold his own property. And if he holds the buyer liable, the sale is not executed, the buyer becomes owner of the building through liability, and the liability is for the waqf, not for the beneficiaries.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings from Hanafi sources. The first ruling from al-Khulasa and al-Qunyah concerns someone who occupies a property under an invalid claim of ownership (ta'wil al-milk) and later discovers it is waqf or belongs to a minor. He must pay the market rental value (ujrat al-mithl) because the property is waqf or the minor's property, and the occupant is considered a usurper in effect. This reflects the Hanafi principle that the rights of the waqf and minors take precedence. The second ruling from al-Muhit deals with a buyer who demolishes a building on waqf land. The judge has discretion: he can hold the seller liable for the value of the building (if the building was sold as part of the property) or hold the buyer liable. If the seller is made liable, the buyer's purchase is validated retroactively. If the buyer is made liable, the sale is voided, and the buyer must compensate the waqf, not the beneficiaries individually. This demonstrates the Hanafi school's strict protection of waqf assets and the flexibility given to the judge to protect the endowment's interests.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): The market rental value for property of similar type and location, used as compensation for benefit taken without right.
الوقف (al-waqf): The permanent dedication of a property for charitable or religious purposes, such that the corpus is inalienable and its usufruct is used for the specified beneficiaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who resides in a property under an invalid claim of ownership and later discovers it is waqf?
A) He is not liable for rent because he had a valid interpretation.
B) He must pay the equivalent rent (ujrat al-mithl) as compensation.
C) He must return the property but no rent is due.
D) He can claim ownership through adverse possession.

Key Takeaway
The waqf's rights override individual claims of ownership, and judges have flexibility to protect the endowment by choosing between two liability options in demolition cases.

Conditions for a Judge's Ruling to Nullify Waqf
Ruling with proof: effect of judge's ruling on waqf sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge's ruling can validate the sale of waqf property and apply them to distinguish valid from invalid judicial rulings.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling can only nullify a waqf if it is unregistered, the seller is the founder's heir, and the sale is necessary.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines the delicate question of when a judge's ruling can override a waqf. It draws on multiple authoritative Hanafi works to delineate the precise conditions under which a judicial ruling can validate the sale of waqf property. Understanding these conditions is crucial for protecting waqf assets while allowing flexibility in genuine hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the passage deals with whether a judge's ruling can nullify a waqf through a sale. Note that the answer depends on several variables.
2
Determine the status of the waqf
Check if the waqf is registered (musajjal) or unregistered (ghayr musajjal). Only unregistered waqf can be revoked by a judge according to the cited sources.
Registered waqf is irrevocable once a court ruling has been made.
3
Identify the seller
The ruling's validity depends on whether the seller is an heir of the founder or a non-heir. The text specifies that permission to sell given to an heir constitutes a valid ruling; to a non-heir, it does not.
4
Examine the judge's language
Analyze the exact wording used by the judge. Merely recording the sale testimony does not nullify the waqf; only explicit language like 'sold a valid correct sale' counts as a ruling.
The judge must explicitly rule the sale valid, not just document it.
5
Apply the old waqf case
Consider the example of an old waqf of unknown status: if the beneficiary (heir) sells out of necessity and the judge rules valid, the sale is executed. But if the heir sells again, the sale is void despite the ruling—'this door is not opened'.
The principle prevents repeated circumvention of waqf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : وفي فوائد شمس الإسلام الواقف إذا افتقر واحتاج إلى الوقف يرفع الأمر في إلى القاضي حتى يفسخ إن لم يكن مسجلاً اهـ. وفي البزازية والخلاصة: ولو وقف محدوداً ثم باعه وكتب القاضي شهادته في صك البيع وكتب في الصك باع فلان منزل كذا أو كان كتب وأقر البائع بالبيع لا يكون حكماً بصحة البيع ونقض الوقف ولو كتب باع بيعاً جائزاً صحيحاً كان حكماً بصحة البيع وبطلان الوقف، وإذا أطلق الحاكم وأجاز بيع وقف غير مسجل إن أطلق ذلك للوارث كان حكماً بصحة بيع الوقف، وإن أطلقه لغير الوارث لا يكون ذلك نقضاً للوقف ; أما إذا بيع الوقف وحكم بصحته قاض كان حكماً ببطلان الوقف اهـ. وفي القنية: وقف قديم لا يعرف صحته ولا فساده باعه الموقوف عليه لضرورة وقضى القاضي بصحة البيع ينفذ إذا كان وارث الواقف، ثم رقم باعه الوارث لضرورة فالبيع باطل ولو قضى القاضي بصحته ولا يفتح هذا الباب ..اهـ.
If you say: In Fawa'id Shams al-Islam: If the waqf founder becomes poor and needs the waqf, he raises the matter to the judge so that he may revoke it if it is not registered. In al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa: If he endowed a limited [property] and then sold it, and the judge wrote his testimony in the deed of sale and wrote in the deed 'so-and-so sold such-and-such house' or he wrote and the seller acknowledged the sale, it does not count as a ruling on the validity of the sale and nullification of the waqf. If he wrote 'he sold a valid, correct sale', it counts as a ruling on the validity of the sale and invalidity of the waqf. If the ruler (hakim) gives an absolute permission and allows the sale of an unregistered waqf, if he gives that permission to the heir, it counts as a ruling on the validity of the sale of the waqf. If he gives it to someone other than the heir, it does not constitute nullification of the waqf. If the waqf is sold and a judge rules on its validity, that constitutes a ruling on the invalidity of the waqf. And in al-Qunyah: An old waqf whose validity or invalidity is unknown, the beneficiary sold it out of necessity, and the judge ruled on the validity of the sale, it is executed if the seller is an heir of the founder. Then if the heir sold it out of necessity, the sale is void even if the judge ruled on its validity, and this door is not opened.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage addresses the critical issue of whether a judge's ruling can validate the sale of waqf property. It begins with the case of a needy founder: according to Fawa'id Shams al-Islam, the founder may request the judge to revoke the waqf if it is not registered (i.e., not yet made irrevocable by a court ruling). This is based on the Hanafi position that waqf becomes binding only after a court ruling (Abu Hanifa's view) or upon declaration (Sahibayn's view). The text then cites al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa to clarify the effect of a judge's written statement. If the judge merely records the sale testimony without explicitly ruling it valid, it does not nullify the waqf. Only if the judge writes 'sold a valid correct sale' does it count as a ruling. Moreover, if a ruler (hakim) authorizes the sale of an unregistered waqf to an heir, it is a valid ruling; to a non-heir, it is not. Finally, al-Qunyah presents a case: an old waqf of unknown status, the beneficiary sells it out of necessity, and the judge rules it valid. If the seller is the founder's heir, the sale is executed; if the heir sells again out of necessity, the sale is void despite the judge's ruling. This shows that the Hanafi school strictly limits the conditions under which a judge's ruling can override waqf, emphasizing the protection of waqf assets.

Key Terms
مسجل (musajjal): A waqf that has been officially registered or ruled upon by a judge, making it irrevocable.
حكم القاضي (hukm al-qadi): A judge's judicial ruling that settles a dispute or validates a transaction, carrying legal force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It does not affect the waqf.
B) It counts as a ruling on the validity of the sale and nullifies the waqf.
C) It only validates the sale if the waqf is registered.
D) It is invalid because the judge cannot rule on waqf sales.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly limits the power of a judge to nullify a waqf, requiring the waqf to be unregistered, the seller to be an heir, and the sale to be necessary.

The Bindingness of Waqf and the Effect of a Judge's Ruling: An Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Resolving the dispute on the effect of a judge's ruling on waqf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the bindingness of waqf, state the preponderant ruling on sale before a judge's decision, and identify the conditions under which a judge's ruling can validate sale as istibdal.

Core Idea
Waqf is binding upon declaration; sale before a ruling is prohibited, but a judge's ruling in ijtihad may permit istibdal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a critical refinement of the earlier ruling regarding the sale of waqf property. While the preponderant opinion (Sahibayn) holds that waqf is immediately binding and cannot be sold, later scholars introduced the possibility of a judge's ruling to validate sale under the construct of istibdal (exchange), distinguishing it from outright sale. In this lesson, we will dissect this intra-Hanafi dispute and understand the precise boundaries of a judge's authority in waqf matters.

Guided Path
1
State the preponderant Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that waqf is binding immediately upon declaration. Therefore, sale of waqf property before a judge's ruling on its bindingness is not permissible for anyone, including the heir.
This is the mufta bihi (fatwa-worthy) view.
2
Identify the opposing view within the madhhab
Abu Hanifa holds that waqf is not binding until a judge rules on it or until the founder dies after attaching it to his death. Under this weaker (marjuh) opinion, sale before such a ruling is permissible.
This view is not relied upon for fatwa.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, a needy founder can revoke an unregistered waqf and sell it. Under the Sahibayn's view, he cannot, and even a judge's ruling to the contrary is invalid if it contradicts the preponderant opinion.
The difference is whether the waqf is immediately binding (lazim) or revocable.
4
Examine the later refinement: istibdal vs. faskh
Later Hanafi scholars like al-Ramli argued that a judge's ruling on sale is actually istibdal (exchange), not faskh (rescission). Istibdal does not rescind the prior waqf but replaces the property, and when a judge rules in a matter of ijtihad, his ruling is binding.
This refinement allows sale under strict conditions, not as a simple sale (bay').
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi) today
The preponderant ruling remains that waqf is binding, and sale is prohibited. However, if a Hanafi judge, after fulfilling conditions, rules that sale is permissible as istibdal for the benefit of the waqf, the sale is valid and the ruling overrides the prior dispute.
The judge must act within the bounds of ijtihad and the madhhab's principles.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

معينة ولم يتصل بحاكم شرعي ثم بعد ذلك وقف ذلك الشيء بعينه على نفسه ثم من بعده على جهة أخرى غير وحكم بصحة هذا الوقف الثاني ولزومه حاكم حنفي في وجه الواقف في ساعة الوقف ولم يتصل الوقف الأول بحاكم أصلاً، ثم بعد موت الواقف واتصال العين الموقوفة إلى الجهة الثانية حكم حاكم حنفي بصحة الوقف الأول لعدم علمه بالوقف الثاني والحكم به، فأي الوقفين هو الصحيح المعمول به؟ أجاب رحمه الله : الوقف الأول هو الصحيح لاتفاق المشايخ على أن الفتوى على قولهما بلزوم الوقف، وحيث كان لازماً لا يصح تغييره بلا شرط منه ولا يضر اتصاله في لزومه عدم بحاكم لأن الحاكم ممنوع شرعاً من الحكم بخلاف ما عليه الفتوى والله أعلم.
I say: It is concerning a waqf that has not been ruled upon for its validity and bindingness, as evidenced by his statement in al-Khulasa 'if it is not registered' meaning ruled upon; and despite that, the interpretation also is according to the weaker opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa. According to their opinion (Sahibayn), which is the preponderant and fatwa-worthy, its sale is not permissible before a ruling on its bindingness, neither for the heir nor for anyone else. And if a judge rules on its sale, if he is a Hanafi muqallid, his ruling is invalid because it is only valid according to the sound fatwa-worthy opinion; he is therefore dismissed with respect to the weak opinion. Hence it is said in al-Qunyah as a branch of the earlier scholars, and the obligation of rent is the opinion of the later scholars, as stated in al-Is'af, and the author of al-Qunyah transmitted both opinions. His statement: 'contrary to what has passed' refers to the non-obligation in the phrase we transmitted from him. His statement: 'If the buyer demolishes the building etc.' In the fatawa of Qari' al-Hidayah: He was asked: If a person leases a waqf house from a legitimate lessor and then demolishes it with his own hand and alters its features, making it a mill or oven or the like, is the lessee obliged to demolish what he built and return the waqf property to its original state? He answered: The judge will look into it. If what he changed it to is more beneficial for the waqf and yields more rent, he takes the rent from him, and what he built remains for the waqf, and he is a donor of what he spent on construction with the rent. If it is not more beneficial nor more profitable, he is compelled to undo what he did and restore the waqf to its original condition after ta'zir suitable to his case. His statement: 'I say: It is concerning a waqf that has not been ruled upon for its validity and bindingness etc.' Al-Ramli said: I say: What appears is the absolute [view] because its sale is istibdal (exchange), not faskh (rescission), and istibdal does not involve rescission of prior judicial rulings that would prevent it. So if a judge sees it and rules accordingly after fulfilling its conditions, it is a ruling in a matter of ijtihad, and a ruling in such a matter settles the difference. So consider the difference, and the truth will become clear to you. And there is a difference between faskh and ibtal (invalidation) and between sale and istibdal. His statement: 'As for what the scholar Sirr al-Din gave fatwa on etc.' I say: The author in his fatawa agreed with what Sirr al-Din gave fatwa on, so he gave fatwa on permissibility, then said: This is what Sirr al-Din Qari' al-Hidayah gave fatwa on, and it is a witness to the correctness of what I gave fatwa on: that if the waqf founder sells an unregistered waqf and a judge rules on the validity of the sale, the sale is executed, even though the mashayikh (scholars) affirm the opinion of the two (Sahibayn) regarding waqf, because the ruling occurs in a subject of ijtihad. This is explicitly stated by Imam al-Bazzazi in the book of waqf, so let it be consulted. The text of al-Bazzaziyya states: Shams al-Islam mentioned: If the waqf founder becomes poor and needs the waqf, he raises the matter to the ruler so that he may revoke it if it is not registered. This is apparent according to the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa, and according to their madhhab (Sahibayn) it is also valid because it occurs in a chapter of ijtihad. And according to the student of the author in the matn of al-Tanwir and its commentary, he said: This refutes what was mentioned by Allamah Qasim and those who follow him, because in al-Sirajiyyah it is established that the mufti should give fatwa according to the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa absolutely, then according to Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar, then al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and he should not pick and choose if he is not a mujtahid. And the opinion of Imam is also considered sound, and some of the authors of the matn have affirmed that and did not rely on others. Similar was attributed in al-Durr al-Mukhtar to Mawla Abu al-Su'ud, the mufti of Rum. I say: Sheikh Sirr al-Din gave a fatwa contrary to his first fatwa, for he mentioned after it: He was asked about a man who endowed an endowment for certain purposes and no judge ruled on it; then he went back and endowed it for purposes other than the first, and a Hanafi judge ruled on this [second] endowment as valid and binding. Is this permissible? He answered: The madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the endowment is not binding except by a ruling or by attaching it to one's death and then dying before retracting; on this basis, the first endowment is invalidated and the second is valid. However, the fatwa in waqf is according to their opinion (Sahibayn) that nothing of what Abu Hanifa stipulated for its bindingness is required. On this basis, the first endowment is valid, and what he did secondly is of no consideration, unless he stipulated in his first endowment that he has the right to change the purposes and recipients to other than the first, then that is valid. In the fatawa of Allamah Qasim, the text: He was asked about a man who endowed a specific property from his wealth to himself, then after him to a specific purpose, and this was not brought to a judge. Then after that, he endowed that same property to himself, then after him to another purpose, and a Hanafi judge ruled on this second endowment as valid and binding in the presence of the waqif at the time of the endowment, and the first endowment was not brought to a judge at all. Then after the death of the waqif, the endowed property was transferred to the second purpose. Then a Hanafi judge ruled on the validity of the first endowment, being unaware of the second endowment and the ruling on it. Which of the two endowments is correct and acted upon? He answered: The first endowment is correct, because the mashayikh agree that the fatwa is according to their opinion (Sahibayn) on the bindingness of waqf. Since it is binding, it cannot be changed without a condition from him, and the fact that it was not connected to a judge does not harm its bindingness, because the judge is prohibited by Shari'ah from ruling contrary to what is the fatwa. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the author (likely Zayn al-Din Ibn Nujaym) of the previous rulings. It begins by stating that the earlier discussion about the needy founder seeking the judge's revocation applies only to an unregistered waqf (not yet ruled upon). According to the weaker opinion of Abu Hanifa, such revocation is possible, but according to the preponderant opinion of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), which is the basis for fatwa, the waqf is binding immediately upon declaration, so its sale is not permissible even for the heir, and a judge's ruling to the contrary is invalid if the judge is a Hanafi muqallid. The author then quotes a fatwa from Qari' al-Hidayah about a lessee who demolishes a waqf house and alters it; the judge must assess whether the change benefits the waqf. If not, the lessee must restore the property. The author then cites al-Ramli, who distinguishes between faskh (rescission) and istibdal (exchange), arguing that a judge's ruling on sale is actually istibdal, not faskh, and thus permissible even if the prior waqf was binding, because the ruling occurs in a matter of ijtihad. This is followed by the author's own view (أقول) agreeing with Sirr al-Din that a judge's ruling on sale of unregistered waqf is valid and binding. The passage then includes a quote from al-Bazzaziyya, a note from the student of the author in al-Tanwir, and two final fatwas: one from Sirr al-Din (contradicting his earlier) and one from Allamah Qasim. The final fatwa from Qasim addresses a conflict of two waqfs with different judges' rulings; the first waqf is upheld because the fatwa is on the Sahibayn's view that waqf is binding immediately, so the second ruling is invalid. This extensive discussion illustrates the complexity of Hanafi waqf jurisprudence and the interplay between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, the effect of a judge's ruling, and the conditions for valid istibdal.

Key Terms
الاستبدال (al-istibdal): The exchange of waqf property for another property, which is permissible under certain conditions, as opposed to outright sale (bay').
القول المرجوح (al-qawl al-marjuh): The weaker or less preponderant opinion within the madhhab, which is not relied upon for fatwa.
محل اجتهاد (mahall ijtihad): A matter where there is legitimate scope for differing interpretations among qualified mujtahids, such that a judge's ruling can settle the issue within his school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant opinion (rajih) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling waqf property before a judge rules on its bindingness?
A) It is permissible for the heir if the waqf is unregistered.
B) It is not permissible for anyone, neither heir nor other.
C) It is permissible only for the founder himself.
D) It is always permissible if the judge later validates it.

Key Takeaway
The bindingness of waqf is immediate according to the preponderant Hanafi view, so sale is prohibited unless a judge rules it as istibdal in a matter of ijtihad.

The Invalidity of Selling Waqf and the Effect of a Judge's Ruling
Intra-Hanafi dispute on waqf sale and judicial authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the relied-upon Hanafi position holds that a judge's ruling cannot validate the sale of waqf property, and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on waqf binding.

Core Idea
Waqf is inalienable; a judge's ruling cannot override the school's established fatwa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: can a judge's ruling make the sale of waqf property valid? The author firmly maintains that waqf property cannot be sold or substituted, even if ruined. He critically examines the fatwa of Siraj al-Din Qari' al-Hidayah, who permitted sale under a judge's ruling, but the majority consensus (including al-Allamah Qasim) affirms the binding nature of waqf based on the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The discussion highlights the limits of judicial authority when it contradicts the school's relied-upon doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
What is the contested issue? The question is whether a judge's ruling can validate the sale of waqf property, and whether waqf can be substituted when ruined.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute involving different opinions within the school.
2
State the Hanafi position and its dalil
The relied-upon view is that waqf is binding and inalienable. The proof is the principle 'الوقف لا يملك ولا يباع' (Waqf is not owned and cannot be sold).
This phrase is foundational in Hanafi waqf doctrine.
3
Examine the opposing view
Some Hanafis (like Siraj al-Din Qari' al-Hidayah) allowed the sale if a judge rules it valid, on the basis that the ruling occurs in a place of ijtihad.
The opposing view relies on the binding nature of a judge's ijtihad.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority of Hanafi scholars respond that the judge is prevented by Sharia from ruling contrary to the fatwa of the school. The fatwa is on the binding view of the Sahibayn, so the first waqf remains valid.
This response limits the scope of judicial authority.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a judge rules a sale valid, some Hanafis would enforce it, but the majority would consider it invalid and revert to the original waqf.
The thamarah is the real-world effect of the dispute.

على الصحيح فالبيع باطل ولو قضى القاضي بصحته، وقد أفتى به العلامة قاسم. وأما ما أفتى به العلامة سراج الدين قارئ الهداية من صحة الحكم ببيعه قبل الحكم بوقفه فحمول على أن القاضي مجتهد أو سهو منه وظاهر قول المؤلف في فتاواه ما أفتى به سراج الدين فأفتى بالجواز ثم قال : وبهذا أفتى سراج الدين قارئ الهداية وهو شاهد لصحة ما أفتيت به أن الواقف لو باع الوقف غير المسجل وحكم بصحة البيع حاكم نفذ البيع وإن صحح المشايخ قولهما في الوقف لوقوع القضاء في محل الاجتهاد. وقد صرح بذلك الإمام البزازي في كتاب الوقف فليراجع .اهـ. وعبارة البزازية نصها وذكر شمس الإسلام رحمه الله تعالى : افتقر الواقف واحتاج إلى الوقف يرفع إلى الحاكم حتى يفسخ إن لم يكن مسجلاً. وهذا ظاهر على مذهب الإمام رحمه الله ، وأما على مذهبهما فيصح أيضاً لوقوعه في فصل مجتهد اهـ. وعلى تلميذ المؤلف في متن التنوير وشرحه وقال : به يندفع ما ذكره العلامة قاسم ومن تبعه لما في السراجية من تصحيح أن المفتي يفتي بقول الإمام أبي حنيفة على الإطلاق ثم بقول أبي يوسف ثم بقول محمد ثم بقول زفر والحسن بن زياد ولا يتخير إذا لم يكن مجتهداً، وقول الإمام مصحح أيضاً فقد جرم به بعض أصحاب المتون ولم يعولوا على غيره .اهـ. وعزا مثله في الدر المختار إلى المولى أبي السعود مفتي الروم . قلت : وقد أفتى الشيخ سراج الدين بخلاف فتواه الأولى فإنه ذكر بعدها : سئل عن رجل وقف وقفاً على جهات ولم يحكم به حاكم ثم رجع ووقفه على جهات غير الأول وحكم بهذا حنفي هل يصح أو لا؟ أجاب : مذهب الإمام أن الوقف لا يلزم إلا بالحكم أو تعليقه بموته ثم يموت قبل أن يرجع عما علقه فعلى هذا يبطل الوقف ويصح الثاني لكن الفتوى في الوقف على : قولهما أنه لا يشترط للزومه شيء مما شرطه أبو حنيفة، فعلى هذا الوقف هو الأول وما فعله ثانياً لا اعتبار به إلا أن يكون شرط في وقفه الأول أن له أن يغيره بما شاء من الجهات والمصارف غير الأول فيصح ذلك اهـ. وفي فتاوى العلامة قاسم ما نصه : وسئل عن رجل وقف شيئاً معيناً من ماله على نفسه ثم من بعده على جهة معينة ولم يتصل بحاكم شرعي ثم بعد ذلك وقف ذلك الشيء بعينه على نفسه ثم من بعده على جهة أخرى غير وحكم بصحة هذا الوقف الثاني ولزومه حاكم حنفي في وجه الواقف في ساعة الوقف ولم يتصل الوقف الأول بحاكم أصلاً، ثم بعد موت الواقف واتصال العين الموقوفة إلى الجهة الثانية حكم حاكم حنفي بصحة الوقف الأول لعدم علمه بالوقف الثاني والحكم به، فأي الوقفين هو الصحيح المعمول به؟ أجاب رحمه الله : الوقف الأول هو الصحيح لاتفاق المشايخ على أن الفتوى على قولهما بلزوم الوقف، وحيث كان لازماً لا يصح تغييره بلا شرط منه ولا يضر اتصاله في لزومه عدم بحاكم لأن الحاكم ممنوع شرعاً من الحكم بخلاف ما عليه الفتوى والله أعلم.
According to the correct view, the sale is invalid even if the judge rules it valid. And al-Allamah Qasim gave a fatwa to that effect. As for the fatwa of al-Allamah Siraj al-Din Qari' al-Hidayah that the ruling of selling it before the ruling of its being waqf is valid, it is interpreted that the judge was a mujtahid or it was an error from him. The apparent meaning of the author's statement and the authors of the matn and al-Hidayah is that it is not permissible to substitute it even if it becomes ruined, and that it does not revert to being the property of the waqif or his heirs because they did not make any exception from their statement 'it is not owned'. Their apparent statement 'Waqf is not owned and cannot be sold' necessitates that the waqf status is not nullified by ruin and does not revert to the ownership of the waqif and his heir, and that substitution is not permissible. Hence Imam Qadikhan said: This is the way and the invalidation, and between sale and substitution... Etc. His statement: (As for what al-Allamah Siraj al-Din gave a fatwa) I say: The author has agreed in his fatwas with what Siraj al-Din gave a fatwa, so he gave a fatwa of permissibility, then he said: By this Siraj al-Din Qari' al-Hidayah gave a fatwa, and it is a witness to the correctness of what I have given a fatwa: that if the waqif sells the unregistered waqf and a ruler rules on the validity of the sale, the sale is executed, even though the mashayikh confirm their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) statement regarding waqf because the ruling occurred in a place of ijtihad. Imam al-Bazzazi has explicitly stated this in the Book of Waqf, so it should be consulted. The text of al-Bazzaziyyah states: Shams al-Islam mentioned: If the waqif becomes needy and needs the waqf, he raises it to the judge so that he may annul it if it is not registered. This is apparent according to the madhhab of the Imam (Abu Hanifa). As for according to their madhhab (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is also valid because it occurs in a chapter of ijtihad. And the student of the author in Matn al-Tanwir and its commentary said: By this is repelled what al-Allamah Qasim and those who followed him mentioned, because in al-Sirajiyyah it is established that the mufti gives fatwa according to the qawl of Imam Abu Hanifa absolutely, then according to the qawl of Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and he does not choose if he is not a mujtahid. And the qawl of the Imam is also correct; some authors of matn have affirmed it and did not rely on others. He attributed similarly in al-Durr al-Mukhtar to Mawla Abu al-Su'ud, mufti of Rum. I say: Shaykh Siraj al-Din gave a fatwa contradicting his first fatwa, for he mentioned after it: He was asked about a man who made a waqf on certain purposes and no judge ruled on it, then he went back and made a waqf on different purposes, and a Hanafi judge ruled on this second waqf. Is it valid or not? He answered: According to the madhhab of the Imam, waqf is not binding except by a ruling or by linking it to his death, then he dies before retracting what he linked. On this basis, the first waqf is nullified and the second is valid. But the fatwa regarding waqf is according to their qawl (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that nothing conditioned by Abu Hanifa is required for its binding. On this basis, the first waqf is the valid one, and what he did second is not considered unless he had stipulated in his first waqf that he has the right to change it to whatever purposes and channels other than the first, in which case it is valid. In the Fatawa of al-Allamah Qasim it states: He was asked about a man who made a specific property a waqf on himself then after him on a specific purpose, and it was not connected to a judge. Then he made that same property a waqf on himself then after him on a different purpose, and a Hanafi judge ruled on the validity and binding of this second waqf in the presence of the waqif at the time of the waqf, and the first waqf was never connected to a judge at all. Then after the death of the waqif and the connection of the waqf property to the second purpose, a Hanafi judge ruled on the validity of the first waqf, unaware of the second waqf and the ruling on it. Which of the two waqfs is correct and operative? He answered: The first waqf is correct because the mashayikh agree that the fatwa is according to their qawl on the binding of waqf. Since it is binding, it cannot be changed without a condition from him, and the lack of connection to a judge does not harm its binding, because the judge is prevented by Sharia from ruling contrary to the fatwa. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale and substitution of waqf property. The author begins by stating the relied-upon position (al-sahih) that the sale of waqf is invalid, even if a judge rules it valid. He then addresses the fatwa of al-Allamah Siraj al-Din Qari' al-Hidayah, who permitted the sale of waqf property under certain circumstances, interpreting it as either a case where the judge was a mujtahid or an error. The author aligns with the view of the majority of Hanafi scholars that waqf property cannot be sold or substituted, even if it becomes ruined, and does not revert to ownership. He quotes Imam Qadikhan to support this. He then notes that the author of the text (likely Ibn al-Humam) initially agreed with Siraj al-Din in some fatwa, but later the passage presents a more detailed discussion on the effect of a judge's ruling. The discussion includes the opinion of Imam al-Bazzazi and the student of the author (possibly from al-Tanwir) regarding the order of giving fatwa according to the Hanafi school. Finally, a specific problem is presented to al-Allamah Qasim, who rules that the first waqf is valid because the fatwa is on the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that waqf is binding without a judge's ruling, and thus a subsequent waqf cannot override it. The passage highlights the tension between the authority of a judge's ruling and the school's relied-upon doctrine.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): The endowment of property in perpetuity for charitable purposes, rendering it inalienable.
استبدال (istibdal): The substitution of waqf property by selling it and purchasing another property to replace it.
حكم القاضي (hukm al-qadi): A judicial ruling by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon view on substituting (istibdal) waqf property when it becomes ruined?
A) It is permissible if the waqf is ruined.
B) It is not permissible; the waqf remains inalienable.
C) It is permissible if a judge rules it valid.
D) It is permissible if the waqif's heirs agree.

Key Takeaway
Waqf property is inalienable; a judge's ruling cannot override the school's established prohibition on selling waqf.

Waqf — Endowment

Waqf Substitution When Unproductive: The Hanafi Prohibition
Discussion of Hanafi views on istibdal of waqf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi position on selling and substituting unproductive waqf property and explain why the school prohibits it despite some early permissive narrations.

Core Idea
Waqf is perpetual; substituting it even when unproductive is prohibited unless the waqif stipulated otherwise.

When waqf property becomes barren or unproductive, a natural question arises: can it be sold and replaced with something more beneficial? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on istibdal (substitution). While some early scholars like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani permitted it under certain conditions, the later school consensus and the relied-upon fatwa is that waqf may not be sold or substituted without a clear condition from the waqif. We will trace the arguments, the concerns about judicial corruption, and the final ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Rule
State the default ruling for an unconditional waqf (waqf mursal): it cannot be sold or substituted, even if completely unproductive.
The passage begins with this clear rule before discussing exceptions.
2
Understand the Basis for Prohibition
Explain the reason: waqf is perpetual (mu'abbad) and inalienable. The authority for substitution only comes from a condition in the waqf deed.
The text compares it to an absolute sale without a right to return.
3
Examine the Permissive Views
Identify which Hanafi scholars allowed substitution (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani) and under what conditions (weak yield, idle mosque, threat of loss).
Note that even permissive views had restrictions: not when the property is still productive.
4
Analyze the School's Final Position
Understand why the later fatwa prohibits substitution: widespread corruption, judges abusing the rule to nullify awqaf.
Key statement: 'al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The fatwa is that he does not sell.'
5
Apply the Ruling to a New Scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a mosque waqf becomes unusable), decide whether sale is allowed and for whom (only judge? only with condition?).
The mufta bihi is prohibition; any sale would be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الوقف مرسلاً لم يذكر فيه شرط الاستبدال لم يكن له أن يبيعها ويستبدل بها وإن كانت أرض الوقف سبخة لا ينتفع بها لأن سبيل الوقف أن يكون مؤبداً لا يباع، وإنما تثبت ولاية الاستبدال بالشرط وبدون الشرط لا تثبت فهو كالبيع المطلق عن شرط الخيار لا يملك المشتري رده وإن لحقه في ذلك غبن .اهـ. وفي الخلاصة وفي فتاوى النسفي : بيع عقار المسجد لمصلحة المسجد لا يجوز وإن كان بأمر القاضي وإن كان خراباً، فأما بيع النقض فيصح . ونقل عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني أنه يجوز للقاضي وللمتولي أن يبيعه ويشتري مكانه آخر وإن لم ينقطع ولكن يؤخذ بثمنه ما هو خير منه للمسجد لا يباع وقد روي عن محمد إذا ضعفت الأرض الموقوفة عن الاستغلال والقيم يجد بثمنها أخرى هي أكثر ريعاً كان له أن يبيعها ويشتري بثمنها ما هو أكثر ريعاً. وفي الفتاوى: قيم وقف خاف السلطان أو يغلب على أرض وقف يبيعها ويتصدق بثمنها، وكذا كل قيم إذا خاف شيئاً من ذلك له أن يبيع ويتصدق بثمنها قال الصدر الشهيد والفتوى على أنه لا يبيع. وما يوافق هذا ما روى الإمام السرخسي في السير الكبير في بابي الأسير في الدفتر الثاني ذكر مسألة ثم قال : وبهذا خطأ من يجوز استبدال الوقف والشيخ الإمام ظهير الدين كان يفتي بجواز الاستبدال تبين من وارث من ثم رجع . اهـ. ما في الخلاصة. وفي شرح الوقاية أن أبا يوسف يجوز الاستبدال في الوقف من غير شرط إذا ضعفت الأرض من الريع ونحن لا نفتي به، وقد شاهدنا في الاستبدال من الفساد ما لا يعد ولا يحصى، فإن ظلمة القضاة جعلوه حيلة إلى إبطال أكثر أوقاف المسلمين وفعلوا ما فعلوا اهـ. وفي الذخيرة سئل شمس الأئمة الحلواني عن أوقاف المسجد. إذا تعطلت وتعذر استغلالها هل للمتولي أن يبيعها ويشتري مكانها أخرى؟ قال: نعم. قيل: إن لم تتعطل ولكن يؤخذ بثمنها ما هو خير منها هل له أن يبيعها ؟ قال : لا . ومن المشايخ من لم يجوز بيعه تعطل أو لم يتعطل، وكذا لم يجوز الاستبدال بالوقف وهكذا فتوى شمس الأئمة السرخسي، وقد روينا عن محمد في فصل العمارة إذا ضعفت الأرض الموقوفة عن والقيم بجد بثمنها أرضاً أخرى أكثر ريعاً له أن هذه الأرض ويشتري. وفي المنتقى قال يبيع هشام سمعت محمداً يقول : الوقف إذا صار بحيث لا ينتفع به المساكين فللقاضي ويشتري بثمنه غيره وليس ذلك إلا للقاضي. وذكر محمد في السير الكبير مسألة تدل على الاستغلال أن يبيعه قوله : (فللقاضي أن يبيعه ويشتري بثمنه غيره الخ) قال الرملي : لا تنسى ما قدمه بأسطر عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني بنقل الذخيرة حين سئل عن أوقاف المسجد إذا تعطلت هل للمتولي أن يبيعها ويشتري مكانها أخرى؟ قال : نعم . ولا قولهم الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة ولا اتفاق المشايخ المتأخرين على أن الأفضل لأهل المسجد أن ينصبوا متولياً ولا يعلموا القاضي في زماننا لما علم من طمع القضاة في أمور الأوقاف ؛ صرح به في التتارخانية وغيرها في كثير من كتب المذهب. قوله : (وذكر محمد في السير الكبير مسألة (الخ) قال الرملي : يجب تقييد المسألة بما إذا كان استيلاء الكفار
And if the waqf was unconditional, not mentioning the condition of substitution, he does not have the right to sell it and substitute it, even if the waqf land is saline and not beneficial, because the way of waqf is that it is perpetual and not sold. The authority for substitution is established by condition; without condition it is not established. It is like an absolute sale without a condition of option; the buyer does not have the right to return it even if he suffers loss. And in al-Khulasah and in the Fatawa of al-Nasafi: Selling the real estate of the mosque for the benefit of the mosque is not permissible even if by order of the judge, even if it is ruined. As for selling the debris, it is valid. It is reported from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani that it is permissible for the judge and the trustee to sell it and buy another place instead, even if it is not ruined, but the sale is made from its price what is better for the mosque; it is not sold. And it is narrated from Muhammad that if the waqf land becomes weak in yield and the custodian finds another land that is more productive for its price, he may sell it and buy with its price what is more productive. In al-Fatawa: If the custodian of a waqf fears the ruler or something prevails over the waqf land, he sells it and gives its price in charity. Similarly, any custodian who fears something like that has the right to sell and give its price in charity. al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The fatwa is that he does not sell. And what agrees with this is what Imam al-Sarakhsi narrated in al-Siyar al-Kabir in the chapter of the prisoner in the second volume; he mentioned a case then said: By this, those who permit substitution of waqf are mistaken. And al-Shaykh al-Imam Zahir al-Din used to give fatwa permitting substitution, then he retracted. This is from al-Khulasah. In Sharh al-Wiqayah: Abu Yusuf permits substitution in waqf without condition if the land becomes weak in yield, but we do not give fatwa according to it. We have witnessed in substitution countless corruption; for oppressive judges made it a trick to nullify most waqfs of Muslims and did what they did. In al-Dhakhirah: Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani was asked about mosque waqfs: if they become idle and their exploitation is difficult, does the trustee have the right to sell them and buy another place? He said: Yes. It was said: If they are not idle but something better is bought with their price, does he have the right to sell? He said: No. Some mashayikh did not permit its sale whether idle or not, and likewise did not permit substitution of waqf. Such is the fatwa of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And we have narrated from Muhammad in the chapter of building: if the waqf land becomes weak in yield and the custodian finds another land with its price that is more productive, he has the right to sell this land and buy. In al-Muntaqa: Hisham said: I heard Muhammad say: When waqf becomes such that the poor cannot benefit from it, the judge [may sell it] and buy another with its price, and that is only for the judge. And Muhammad mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir a case indicating that he may sell it. His statement: (The judge may sell it and buy another with its price) al-Ramli said: Do not forget what he presented a few lines earlier from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani as transmitted in al-Dhakhirah when he was asked about mosque waqfs if they become idle: does the trustee have the right to sell them and buy another place? He said: Yes. And their saying: the special authority is stronger than the general authority, and the consensus of the later mashayikh that it is better for the people of the mosque to appoint a trustee and not inform the judge in our time because of the known greed of judges in waqf affairs; this is stated in al-Tatarkhaniyyah and other many books of the school. His statement: (And Muhammad mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir a case...) al-Ramli said: The case must be restricted to when the domination is of the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the permissibility of selling and substituting (istibdal) waqf property when it becomes unproductive or ruined. The author begins by stating a clear ruling: if the waqf was made unconditionally (mursal) without a stipulation allowing substitution, the waqif or trustee has no right to sell or substitute it, even if the land is completely unproductive. This is because waqf is meant to be perpetual and inalienable. The authority for substitution can only be established by a condition in the original waqf deed, analogous to an absolute sale without a right to return. The passage then presents various opinions from Hanafi scholars. For mosque waqf, it is reported that selling the land itself is not allowed even by order of the judge, but selling the debris is allowed. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani is quoted as permitting the judge or trustee to sell and replace mosque property if it becomes idle, but not if it is still productive but a better property is available. There is a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani that if the land becomes weak in yield, the custodian may sell and buy a more productive one. However, the fatwa is said to be against this, as stated by al-Sadr al-Shahid that the custodian does not sell. Imam al-Sarakhsi is quoted as saying that those who permit substitution are mistaken, and Shaykh Zahir al-Din initially permitted it but later retracted. The commentary in Sharh al-Wiqayah acknowledges that Abu Yusuf permitted substitution without condition if the land becomes weak, but the author says 'we do not give fatwa according to it' due to the widespread corruption observed. The passage concludes with al-Ramli's note that the permissive narration from Muhammad in al-Siyar al-Kabir should be restricted to cases where the waqf is under threat from disbelievers (istila' al-kuffar). This demonstrates the Hanafi school's caution in allowing any alienation of waqf property, despite some narrations that seem to permit it, due to concerns about abuse and the principle of waqf perpetuity.

Key Terms
وقف مرسل (waqf mursal): A waqf made without any conditions, especially without a condition allowing substitution (istibdal).
استبدال (istibdal): The act of replacing waqf property by selling it and purchasing another property as a substitute.
سبخة (sabakhah): Saline or barren land that is unproductive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on selling waqf land that has become salty and unproductive if the waqf was unconditional (mursal)?
A) The trustee may sell it and buy better land with permission of the judge.
B) It is permissible if the custodian finds a more productive property.
C) It cannot be sold because waqf is perpetual and sale is not permitted without a condition.
D) It may be sold if the custodian fears loss of the property.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position is that an unconditional waqf cannot be sold or substituted, even if unproductive; the prohibition is maintained to preserve the perpetuity of waqf and prevent corruption.

Selling and Replacing Waqf Without a Condition
Explaining the prohibition of istibdal without an explicit condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling and replacing waqf property without an explicit condition, identify the condition that permits istibdal, and explain the mufta bihi position in light of intra-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Without a condition, the mutawalli cannot sell or replace waqf property – even if unproductive.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains the Hanafi ruling on istibdāl (selling waqf property and using the proceeds to purchase another). The core principle is that waqf is perpetual; any sale requires explicit authorization in the waqf deed. Even if the land becomes useless, the mutawalli lacks authority without a condition. However, there are differing opinions within the school, and the later fatwa prohibits it to prevent abuse.

Guided Path
1
State the core ruling
The passage states that if a waqf is mursal (no condition for istibdal), the mutawalli cannot sell or replace it, even if the land is unproductive.
2
Understand the rationale
Waqf is perpetual (mu’abbad); its purpose is to provide ongoing benefit. Selling defeats this purpose.
3
Identify the condition for istibdal authority
The authority to sell only arises if explicitly stated in the waqf deed. Without it, any sale is batil (void). The passage compares this to a sale without a condition of option.
Comparison to a sale without a right of return.
4
Note the intra-madhhab disagreement
Some early Hanafi scholars (Shams al-A’imma, Imam Muhammad) allowed istibdal under necessity, but the later fatwa prohibits it.
Al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Sarakhsi uphold the prohibition.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for practice is that istibdal without a condition is not allowed, based on the majority of later scholars and to prevent corruption.
Zahir al-Din initially permitted it but later retracted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الوقف مرسلاً لم يذكر فيه شرط الاستبدال لم يكن له أن يبيعها ويستبدل بها وإن كانت أرض الوقف سبخة لا ينتفع بها لأن سبيل الوقف أن يكون مؤبداً لا يباع، وإنما تثبت ولاية الاستبدال بالشرط وبدون الشرط لا تثبت فهو كالبيع المطلق عن شرط الخيار لا يملك المشتري رده وإن لحقه في ذلك غبن .اهـ. وفي الخلاصة وفي فتاوى النسفي : بيع عقار المسجد لمصلحة المسجد لا يجوز وإن كان بأمر القاضي وإن كان خراباً، فأما بيع النقض فيصح . ونقل عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني أنه يجوز للقاضي وللمتولي أن يبيعه ويشتري مكانه آخر وإن لم ينقطع ولكن يؤخذ بثمنه ما هو خير منه للمسجد لا يباع وقد روي عن محمد إذا ضعفت الأرض الموقوفة عن الاستغلال والقيم يجد بثمنها أخرى هي أكثر ريعاً كان له أن يبيعها ويشتري بثمنها ما هو أكثر ريعاً. وفي الفتاوى: قيم وقف خاف السلطان أو يغلب على أرض وقف يبيعها ويتصدق بثمنها، وكذا كل قيم إذا خاف شيئاً من ذلك له أن يبيع ويتصدق بثمنها قال الصدر الشهيد والفتوى على أنه لا يبيع. وما يوافق هذا ما روى الإمام السرخسي في السير الكبير في بابي الأسير في الدفتر الثاني ذكر مسألة ثم قال : وبهذا خطأ من يجوز استبدال الوقف والشيخ الإمام ظهير الدين كان يفتي بجواز الاستبدال تبين من ثم رجع . اهـ. ما في الخلاصة.
If the waqf was unconditional (mursal) with no condition of istibdal mentioned in it, he may not sell it and replace it, even if the waqf land is saline and cannot be benefited from, because the way of waqf is to be perpetual, not to be sold. Authority for istibdal is established only by condition, and without condition it is not established. It is like an absolute sale without a condition of option; the buyer does not have the right to return it, even if he suffers loss. End. In al-Khulasa and in Fatawa al-Nasafi: Sale of a mosque's real estate for the benefit of the mosque is not allowed, even if by order of the qadi and even if it is ruined. As for sale of the debris, it is valid. It is transmitted from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani that it is permissible for the qadi and the mutawalli to sell it and buy another place instead, even if it is not discontinued, but its price is taken to buy something better for the mosque; it is not sold for something else. It is narrated from Muhammad: If the endowed land becomes weak in yield and the administrator finds another land with its price that yields more rent, he may sell it and buy with its price that which yields more rent. In al-Fatawa: The administrator of a waqf fears the sultan or [someone] will take over the waqf land, he sells it and gives its price in charity. Likewise every administrator, if he fears something like that, may sell and give its price in charity. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The fatwa is that he does not sell. Supporting this is what Imam al-Sarakhsi narrated in al-Siyar al-Kabir in the chapter of the captive in the second register: he mentioned an issue then said: and by this, those who permit istibdal of waqf are mistaken, and the Shaykh Imam Zahir al-Din used to give fatwa permitting istibdal, then it became clear from [his opinion] then he retracted. End from al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school regarding the sale and replacement (istibdal) of waqf property. The basic ruling is that if the waqf was made unconditionally (mursal) without mentioning any permission for istibdal, the trustee (mutawalli) has no authority to sell the waqf property and buy another with its price, even if the land is saline and unproductive. This is because waqf is intended to be perpetual and not subject to sale. The authority to replace waqf property only comes from an explicit condition in the waqf deed; without it, it is not allowed. The passage compares this to an absolute sale without a right of return: the buyer cannot return the item even if he suffers loss. Then it mentions alternative views from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and Imam Muhammad, who allowed sale under certain conditions (when land is weak or unproductive), but the fatwa (operative ruling) is against it due to widespread corruption. The passage concludes by quoting al-Sarakhsi that those who allow istibdal are mistaken, and that the great scholar Zahir al-Din initially gave fatwa allowing it but later retracted.

Key Terms
استبدال (istibdal): The act of selling waqf property and using the proceeds to purchase another property for the same purpose.
مرسل (mursal): A waqf made without any conditions or specifications, especially regarding istibdal.
متولي (mutawalli): The trustee or manager of a waqf responsible for its administration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because it is no longer beneficial.
B) Yes, but only with the permission of the qadi.
C) No, because waqf is perpetual and the authority to sell requires an explicit condition.
D) No, but he can lease it out.

Key Takeaway
Without an explicit condition, the mutawalli cannot sell or replace waqf property, even if it becomes unproductive; waqf is perpetual.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Selling Unproductive Waqf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on istibdal of waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different Hanafi positions on selling unproductive waqf and identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling) along with its rationale.

Core Idea
Selling unproductive waqf is prohibited in the later Hanafi fatwa due to widespread corruption, despite some early scholars permitting it.

The sale of waqf property (istibdal) when it becomes unproductive is a contested issue within the Hanafi school. This lesson examines the varying opinions of early authorities like Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, the conditions they placed, and the later shift to absolute prohibition because of judicial abuse. Understanding this dispute reveals how practical corruption influenced legal doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Istibdal is the sale of a waqf property to replace it with another. The dispute arises when the original property becomes unproductive (ta'atutal). Determine what triggers the need for sale.
2
State Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf permitted istibdal without any condition if the land's yield weakens. This is the most permissive view.
From Sharh al-Wiqaya.
3
State al-Halwani's Position
Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani allowed sale only if the waqf is completely unusable (idle). If it still yields any benefit, sale is not permitted even for a better property.
From al-Dhakhira.
4
State Imam Muhammad's Position
Imam Muhammad allowed sale only by the qadi (judge) if the waqf no longer benefits the beneficiaries. The mutawalli does not have this authority.
From al-Muntaqa.
5
State the Mufta Bihi
Later scholars (al-Sarakhsi, al-Sadr al-Shahid) prohibited sale entirely due to rampant corruption by unjust judges and administrators. This is the relied-upon fatwa.
Authority: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي شرح الوقاية أن أبا يوسف يجوز الاستبدال في الوقف من غير شرط إذا ضعفت الأرض من الريع ونحن لا نفتي به، وقد شاهدنا في الاستبدال من الفساد ما لا يعد ولا يحصى، فإن ظلمة القضاة جعلوه حيلة إلى إبطال أكثر أوقاف المسلمين وفعلوا ما فعلوا اهـ. وفي الذخيرة سئل شمس الأئمة الحلواني عن أوقاف المسجد. إذا تعطلت وتعذر استغلالها هل للمتولي أن يبيعها ويشتري مكانها أخرى؟ قال: نعم. قيل: إن لم تتعطل ولكن يؤخذ بثمنها ما هو خير منها هل له أن يبيعها ؟ قال : لا . ومن المشايخ من لم يجوز بيعه تعطل أو لم يتعطل، وكذا لم يجوز الاستبدال بالوقف وهكذا فتوى شمس الأئمة السرخسي، وقد روينا عن محمد في فصل العمارة إذا ضعفت الأرض الموقوفة عن والقيم بجد بثمنها أرضاً أخرى أكثر ريعاً له أن هذه الأرض ويشتري. وفي المنتقى قال يبيع هشام سمعت محمداً يقول : الوقف إذا صار بحيث لا ينتفع به المساكين فللقاضي ويشتري بثمنه غيره وليس ذلك إلا للقاضي.
And in Sharh al-Wiqaya: Abu Yusuf permits istibdal in waqf without a condition if the land weakens in yield, but we do not give fatwa with it. We have witnessed in istibdal corruption beyond count, for oppressive judges made it a trick to invalidate most Muslim awqaf and they did what they did. End. And in al-Dhakhira: Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani was asked about mosque awqaf. If they become idle and their exploitation is impossible, may the mutawalli sell them and buy others in their place? He said: Yes. It was said: If they are not idle, but with their price something better than them is taken, may he sell them? He said: No. And some mashayikh did not permit their sale whether idle or not, and likewise did not permit istibdal of the waqf, and such is the fatwa of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And we have narrated from Muhammad in the chapter of buildings: If the endowed land weakens and the administrator finds with its price another land yielding more rent, he has the right to [sell] this land and buy. And in al-Muntaqa, Hisham said: I heard Muhammad say: If the waqf reaches a state where the poor cannot benefit from it, then the qadi [may sell it] and buy another with its price, and that is only for the qadi.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school about selling waqf when it becomes unproductive. Abu Yusuf allowed istibdal without a condition if the land's yield weakens, but the later fatwa rejects it due to corruption. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani allowed replacement only if the waqf is completely unusable; if it is still usable, even if a better property could be bought, it is not allowed. Other scholars prohibited selling regardless. Imam Muhammad, as narrated in al-Muntaqa, allowed the qadi alone to sell if the waqf no longer benefits the beneficiaries, and only the qadi has this authority. The passage highlights the tension between permissibility in principle and the practical prohibition due to abuse by unjust judges.

Key Terms
الاستبدال (al-istibdal): The process of selling a waqf property and using the price to buy another property for the same purpose.
تعطل (ta'atutal): The state of a waqf property becoming unusable or unproductive.
القاضي (al-qadi): The Islamic judge who has authority to authorize istibdal according to some scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa prohibits selling unproductive waqf altogether due to corruption, overriding earlier permissions.

Priority of Maintenance in Waqf Income
States the general Hanafi rule that waqf income is first allocated to maintenance, and specifies the duty for dwelling waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the priority of waqf income expenditure and identify who bears maintenance costs for a dwelling waqf.

Core Idea
Waqf income must first be spent on maintenance — the beneficiary's right to receive income is secondary to preserving the property.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a fundamental rule in Hanafi waqf law: the income generated by an endowed property must first be used for its upkeep. Only after maintenance expenses are covered can income be distributed to beneficiaries. Additionally, if the waqf is a house given as a dwelling right (sukna), the dweller is responsible for maintenance. This ensures the sustainability of the endowment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is managing waqf income. The ruling is that maintenance is the first priority, unconditionally.
the text says 'يبدأ من غلته بعمارته بلا شرط'
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
General waqf for children vs. specific house waqf with dwelling right. In the first, maintenance is from waqf income; in the second, maintenance is on the dweller.
the house scenario uses 'فعمارته على من له السكنى'
3
Note the legal effect
Maintenance takes precedence over distribution to beneficiaries. The waqf must be preserved for perpetual benefit.
this is a priority rule, not optional
4
Identify consequences of neglect
If maintenance is ignored, the property may deteriorate, potentially voiding the waqf's purpose. In case of insufficient income, the waqf may be sold or leased to fund repairs (per other Hanafi sources).
the passage doesn't detail consequences, but they are implied
5
Apply the rule to a modern example
A waqf building generates rent. The rent must first be used for repairs; only the surplus is distributed to beneficiaries.
this demonstrates the practical application

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أولاده ويبدأ من غلته بعمارته بلا شرط ولو داراً فعمارته على من له السكنى
On his children, and he begins from its income with its maintenance without condition, and if it is a house, its maintenance is on the one who has the right to dwell.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary. It states the general rule for a waqf (endowment) made on one's children: the income of the waqf property is first used for its maintenance (like repairs) without any condition. If the waqf property is a house, the responsibility for maintenance falls on the person who has the right to live in it (the beneficiary of the dwelling right). This is a clear statement of the Hanafi position on the priority of maintenance expenses.

Key Terms
عمارة (ʿimārah): Maintenance and repair of the waqf property.
سكنى (suknā): The right to dwell in a house that is waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Distribution among the children
B) Maintenance of the property
C) Charity to the poor
D) Saving for future

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, maintenance of the endowed property is the first charge on its income, and for a dwelling waqf, the dweller bears the maintenance cost.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Waqf Validity in Recovered Enemy Land
Intra-Hanafi dispute on waqf validity in recovered land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on waqf validity in the scenario of land recovered from infidels, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, waqf is invalid except for a mosque; for the Sahibayn, waqf permanently removes ownership — the original owner can reclaim only under Abu Hanifa's view.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a specific scenario: infidels take Muslim land, Muslims recover it, and a soldier makes his share a waqf. The original owner later appears. The Hanafi school splits: Abu Hanifa holds the waqf invalid (except mosque), so the owner reclaims; the Sahibayn hold it valid. Let's dissect the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Infidels capture Muslim town, Muslims recover and divide spoils. A soldier gets land and makes it waqf for the poor, appoints a custodian. Later, the original pre-Islamic owner appears.
2
State the majority Hanafi position (Sahibayn) and its reasoning
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the waqf is valid. The property left the founder's ownership permanently and is now inalienable. Therefore, the original owner cannot reclaim it.
This is the position of the two companions (Sahibayn).
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and its reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that waqf is invalid (except for a mosque). The founder retains ownership and can sell the property; upon his death it passes to heirs. Thus the original owner can reclaim the land.
For a mosque, Abu Hanifa agrees it is valid and ownership leaves the founder.
4
Identify the exception for mosque
Both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that making a mosque is a valid waqf that removes ownership. So for a mosque, the original owner cannot reclaim even according to Abu Hanifa.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under the Sahibayn, the waqf stands and the original owner loses his right. Under Abu Hanifa (for non-mosque waqf), the original owner can reclaim the land from the soldier's waqf.
The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) follows the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو عدم جواز الاستبدال بالوقف وصورتها الكفار إذا استولوا على بلدة من بلاد المسلمين ثم ظهر عليها المسلمون وقسموها فيما بينهم فأصاب رجل من الغانمين أرضاً فجعلها صدقة موقوفة للمساكين ودفعها إلى قيم يقوم عليها ثم حضر المالك القديم فليس له أن يأخذها . قالوا: وهذا لأنه زال عن ملك الواقف وصار بحال لا يقبل النقل من ملك إلى ملك فلا يكون للمالك القديم حق الملك، أما على قول أبي حنيفة الوقف باطل حتى كان للواقف أن يبيع الوقف حال حياته، فإذا مات يصير ميرا ميراثاً : عنه فكان للمالك القديم حق الأخذ إلا في المسجد خاصة فإن اتخاذ المسجد عنده صحيح ويزول عن ملكية متخذه فلا يكون للمالك القديم حق الأخذ فيه .اهـ.
And if there is no permissibility of substitution of the waqf, and its example is the infidels when they take over a town of the Muslims, then the Muslims overcome them and divide it among themselves, and a man from the spoils gets land and makes it a charity waqf for the poor and hands it to a guardian to manage it, then the original owner comes, he cannot take it. They said: This is because it left the ownership of the waqf-founder and became in a state that does not accept transfer from ownership to ownership, so the original owner has no right of ownership. But according to Abu Hanifa, the waqf is invalid, so the waqf-founder can sell the waqf during his life, and when he dies it becomes inheritance, so the original owner has the right to take it, except for a mosque specifically, for making a mosque according to him is valid and it leaves the ownership of the maker, so the original owner has no right to take it.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The general opinion (of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that waqf is valid and once property is made waqf, it leaves the founder's ownership and becomes inalienable. Therefore, if infidels take the land and then Muslims recover it and divide it as spoils, and a Muslim makes his share a waqf, the original owner (from before the infidel takeover) cannot reclaim it. However, Abu Hanifa holds that waqf is invalid (except for a mosque), so the founder still owns it and can sell it; upon death it is inherited. Thus the original owner can reclaim the land. But for a mosque, even Abu Hanifa says it is valid and leaves ownership.

Key Terms
استبدال (istibdāl): Replacement of waqf property with another property.
مالك قديم (mālik qadīm): The original owner of the land before it was taken by infidels.
وقف (waqf): An endowment of property for charitable purposes, removing it from private ownership.
صاحبين (Sāḥibayn): The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, senior students of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under whose opinion would the original owner be able to reclaim the land made into waqf?
A) Abu Yusuf
B) Muhammad al-Shaybani
C) Abu Hanifa
D) Imam al-Shafi'i

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of waqf in recovered enemy lands depends on whether one follows Abu Hanifa's view (invalid except mosque) or the Sahibayn (valid); the mufta bihi is the latter.
