Waqf Custodian's Authority to Borrow and Reimburse Personal Expenses
Analyzing conditions for waqf custodian's borrowing and reimbursement of personal expenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conditions under which a waqf custodian may borrow on the waqf or recoup personal expenses, and apply the mufta bihi ruling in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A waqf custodian may recoup personal expenses from the waqf's income without a judge's permission if the waqf has available income; otherwise, only with permission for indispensable needs.

The authority of a waqf custodian to borrow and seek reimbursement is a nuanced area where multiple Hanafi positions exist. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the conditions: the presence of waqf income (ghalla) and the indispensability of the expense. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for proper waqf management and fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and positions
The debate is whether the waqf custodian can borrow on behalf of the waqf or recoup personal expenses without the judge's permission. Scholars like Hilal, Abu al-Layth, Qadikhan, and later authorities have different views.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; no other madhhab is discussed.
2
Distinguish the key factor: presence or absence of ghalla
Qadikhan and others make permission hinge on whether the waqf has available income. If ghalla exists, the custodian may spend his own money and recoup from that income without judicial approval.
Ghalla refers to any income generated by the waqf property.
3
Apply the indispensability condition
For indispensable expenses like repairs, borrowing requires judge's permission when no ghalla. For non-indispensable expenses (e.g., distribution to beneficiaries), borrowing is not allowed even with permission because there is an alternative.
Indispensability is determined by necessity for the waqf's operation.
4
Recognize the mufta bihi ruling
The passage states that if the waqf has an alternative (بدل), the custodian may borrow absolutely; if indispensable, only with the judge's order. Repair is indispensable, so it requires permission.
Al-Tarsusi and al-Ramli support this view; the text calls it 'المعتمد في المذهب'.
5
See the practical difference
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the custodian can independently fund urgent repairs and recoup, or must always seek judicial approval. With ghalla, independence; without, judicial approval needed for indispensable expenses.
This affects the speed and efficiency of waqf maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوقف
يرجع في غلة الوقف
الغلة لأن للقاضي هذه الولاية وإن كان لها بد ليس للقاضي هذه الولاية. وفي واقعات الناطفي : المتولي إذا أراد أن يستدين على الوقف ليجعل ذلك في ثمن البذر؛ إن أراد ذلك بأمر القاضي فله ذلك بلا خلاف لأن القاضي يملك الاستدانة على الوقف فيملك المتولي ذلك بإذن القاضي، وإن أراد ذلك بغير أمر القاضي ففيه روايتان، وصرح في الخلاصة بأن الأصح ما قاله الفقيه أبو الليث وفي الخانية قيم الوقف إذا اشترى شيئاً لمرمة المسجد بدون إذن القاضي قالوا : لا يرجع بذلك في مال المسجد، وله أن ينفق على المرمة من ماله كالوصي في مال الصغير، وإن أدخل المتولي جذعاً من ماله في الوقف جاز وله أن اهـ. وفي الخلاصة في مسألة الجذع والاحتياط أن يبيع الجذع من آخر ثم يشتريه لأجل الوقف ثم يدخله في دار الوقف .اهـ. وفسر قاضيخان الاستدانة على الوقف بتفسيرين فقال في الثاني وتفسير الاستدانة بما ذكر إنما هو فيما إذا لم يكن في يده شيء من الغلة، وأما إذا كان في يده شيء منها واشترى شيئاً للوقف ونقد الثمن من ماله جاز له أن يرجع بذلك من غلته، وإن لم يكن بأمر القاضي كالوكيل بالشراء إذا نقد الثمن من ماله فإنه يجوز له الرجوع به على موكله. وقال في الأول أن لا يكون للوقف غلة فيحتاج إلى القرض والاستدانة، أما إذا كان للوقف غلة فأنفق من مال نفسه لإصلاح الوقف فإن له أن يرجع في غلة الوقف اهـ . وفي القنية برقم (يو)، قيم أنفق في عمارة المسجد من مال نفسه ثم رجع بمثله
اهـ.
أبو الليث أي أنه ليس له ذلك إلا بإذن القاضي فيما لا بد منه رملي قوله: (وفي الخانية قيم الوقف الخ) أقول في فتاوى شيخنا الحانوتي : إذا أشهد عند الإنفاق أنه أنفق ليرجع على الوقف يرجع وسيأتي ذكره منقولاً عن جامع الفصولين رملي قوله ثم يشتريه لأجل الوقف) أي بإذن القاضي ليوافق ما قبله عن الخانية تأمل قوله : وفسر قاضيخان الاستدانة الخ) أقول : عبارة قاضيخان بعد أن ذكر أن القيم لا يملك الاستدانة إلا بأمر القاضي وتفسير الاستدانة أن يشتري للوقف شيئاً وليس في يده شيء من غلة الوقف ليرجع بذلك فيما يحدث من غلة الوقف، أما إذا كان في يده شيء من غلات الوقف فاشترى للوقف شيئاً ونقد الثمن من مال نفسه ينبغي أن يرجع بذلك في غلة المسجد وإن لم يكن ذلك بأمر القاضي. ثم قال بعد ورقة : وليس للقيم أمر القاضي وتفسير الاستدانة أن لا يكون للوقف غلة فيحتاج إلى القرض والاستدانة، أما إذا كان للوقف غلة فأنفق من مال نفسه لإصلاح الوقف كان له أن : يرجع بذلك في غلة الوقف اهـ . قلت : ويؤخذ من مجموع كلاميه أنه لو أنفق من ماله أو اشترى مع وجود مال للوقف يرجع ولو بلا أمر قاض وإن لم يكن معه مال للوقف فاشترى أو أنفق لا يرجع إلا بأمر، ويظهر منه أن مراده بالقرض الإقراض لا الاستقراض لدخوله في الاستدانة. وعلى هذا فقوله قبل هذا «قيم الوقف إذا اشترى الخ» أي عند عدم مال في يده للوقف. وقوله وله أن ينفق على المرمة من ماله» أي إذا كان
الوقف جاز، سواء كانت غلته مستوفاة أو غير مستوفاة .اهـ. ثم قال : وللقيم الاستدانة على الوقف لضرورة العمارة لا لتقسيم ذلك على الموقوف عليهم ثم رقم (بنك) استقرض القيم لمصالح المساجد فهو على نفسه وبرقم (عك) لا أصدقه في زماننا وبرقم (حم) له ذلك وبرقم (بق) لا يستدين إلا بأمر القاضي ثم ذكر ما اختاره الفقيه أبو الليث اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين من الفصل السابع والعشرين : ولو أخذ المتولي دراهم الوقف وصرف دنانير إلى عمارة الوقف صح لو خيراً، ولو أنفق عليه من مال نفسه يرجع ولو لم يشترط كوصي ثم رقم (مق) يرجع لو شرط وإلا لا ثم قال : وذكر في العدة الاستدانة لضرورة مصالح الوقف تجوز لو أمر الواقف وإلا فالمختار أن يرفع إلى القاضي ليأمر بها. ثم رقم (فط) الأحوط أن يرفع الأمر إليه إلا إذا تعذر الحضور لبعده فيستدين بنفسه، وقيل يصح بلا رفع ولو أمكن اهـ. وفي الرابع والثلاثين : قيم الوقف لو أنفق من ماله في عمارة الوقف، فلو أشهد أنه أنفق ليرجع فله الرجوع وإلا فلا .اهـ. وفي الحاوي ويجوز للمتولي إذا احتاج إلى العمارة أن يستدين على الوقف ويصرف ذلك فيها والأولى أن يكون بإذن الحاكم .اهـ. والحاصل أن هلالاً مانع من الاستدانة مطلقاً وحمله ابن وهبان على ما إذا كان بغير أمر القاضي وادعى أنه إذا كان بأمر القاضي فلا خلاف فيه، والظاهر كما ذكره الطرسوسي خلافه لما علمت من تعليله، وأما غير هلال فمنهم من جوز الاستدانة مطلقاً للعمارة كما في جامع الفصولين: والمعتمد في المذهب إن كان له منه بدلاً . يستدين مطلقاً، وإن كان لا بد له فإن كان بأمر القاضي جاز وإلا فلا والعمارة لا بد لها فيستدين لها بأمر القاضي، وأما غير العمارة فإن كان للصرف على المستحقين لا تجوز الاستدانة ولو بإذن القاضي لأن له منه بداً كما صرح به في القنية بقوله : لا لتقسيم ذلك على الموقوف عليهم وأن الاستدانة أعم من القرض والشراء بالنسيئة. وفي البزازية من كتاب الوصايا : لو استقرض المتولي إن شرط الواقف له ذلك وإلا رفع إلى الحاكم إن احتاج اهـ . لكن وقع الاشتباه في مسائل منها : هل يستدين للإمام والخطيب والمؤذن باعتبار أنه لا بد له من ذلك فيكون بإذن القاضي فقط أو لا؟ الظاهر أنه لا يستدين لهم إلا بإذن القاضي لقوله في جامع الفصولين لضرورة مصالح المسجد». وقال في خزانة الأكمل :
للوقف مال وحينئذ له الرجوع إن أشهد أنه أنفق ليرجع فيوافق ما سيأتي عن جامع الفصولين، والظاهر أن الإشهاد لازم قضاء لا ديانة فلا يخالف كان له أن يرجع قوله : (سواء كانت غلته مستوفاة أو غير مستوفاة الظاهر أنه مبني على رواية عدم اشتراط الأمر من قاض . قوله: (والحاصل أن هلالاً منع من الاستدانة مطلقاً قال الرملي أي بإذن وبغير إذن قوله : (لما علمت من تعليله) قال الرملي : أي تعليل هذا بقوله وليس للوقف ذمة اهـ . قلت : لكن ما مر عن الواقعات صريح في أنه لا خلاف فيما إذا كان بأمر القاضي.
Book of Waqf: He [the custodian] recoups from the produce of the waqf because the judge has this authority; if it has an alternative, the judge does not have this authority. In Waqi'at al-Natifi: The custodian if he wants to borrow on the waqf to spend on the price of seeds; if he wants that by the judge's order, he may do so without disagreement because the judge has the authority to borrow on the waqf, so the custodian has that by the judge's permission. And if he wants that without the judge's order, there are two narrations, and it is stated in al-Khulasa that the correct opinion is what Abu al-Layth said. And in al-Khaniyya: The custodian of the waqf if he buys something for the repair of the mosque without the judge's permission, they said: he does not recoup that from the wealth of the mosque, but he may spend on the repair from his own wealth like the guardian regarding the wealth of the orphan. And if the custodian inserts a beam from his own wealth into the waqf, it is permissible, and he has... end. In al-Khulasa concerning the matter of the beam: the precaution is that he sell the beam to someone else, then buy it for the sake of the waqf, then insert it into the waqf property. End. Qadikhan interpreted borrowing on the waqf with two interpretations. He said in the second: The interpretation of borrowing mentioned is only when there is nothing of the produce in his hand. As for if there is something of it in his hand and he bought something for the waqf and paid the price from his own wealth, it is permissible for him to recoup that from its produce, even if it was not by the judge's order, like the agent for purchase if he pays the price from his own wealth, then it is permissible for him to recoup it from his principal. And he said in the first: That the waqf has no produce, so it needs a loan and borrowing. As for if the waqf has produce, and he spent from his own wealth for the repair of the waqf, then he has the right to recoup from the produce of the waqf. End. In al-Qunyah, number (يو): The custodian spent on the repair of the mosque from his own wealth, then recouped an equivalent. End. Abu al-Layth: i.e., he does not have that except with the judge's permission in what is indispensable. [Ramli:] His statement: 'And in al-Khaniyya, the custodian of the waqf etc.' I say in the fatawa of our teacher al-Hanuti: If he bears witness at the time of spending that he spent to recoup from the waqf, he recoups. Its mention will come from Jami' al-Fusulain. [Ramli:] His statement: 'Then he buys it for the sake of the waqf' i.e., with the judge's permission to agree with what preceded from al-Khaniyya. Consider his statement: 'And Qadikhan interpreted borrowing etc.' I say: Qadikhan's expression is that after he mentioned that the custodian does not have the authority to borrow except with the judge's order, and the interpretation of borrowing is that he buys something for the waqf while nothing of the produce of the waqf is in his hand, to recoup thereby from what later occurs of the produce of the waqf. As for if there is something of the produce of the waqf in his hand, and he bought something for the waqf and paid the price from his own wealth, it is appropriate that he recoup that from the produce of the mosque even if that was not by the judge's order. Then he said after a page: And the custodian does not have the judge's order, and the interpretation of borrowing is that the waqf has no produce, so it needs a loan and borrowing. As for if the waqf has produce, and he spent from his own wealth for the repair of the waqf, he has the right to recoup that from the produce of the waqf. End. I say: It is taken from the sum of his two statements that if he spent from his wealth or bought while there is wealth of the waqf, he recoups even without a judge's order. And if there is no wealth of the waqf with him, and he bought or spent, he does not recoup except with an order. And it appears from that that by 'loan' he means giving a loan (i.e., borrowing) not taking a loan, because it falls under borrowing. According to this, his statement before this 'The custodian of the waqf if he buys etc.' means when there is no wealth in his hand for the waqf. And his statement 'And he may spend on the repair from his wealth' means if the waqf is permissible, whether its produce is collected or not. End. Then he said: The custodian has the authority to borrow on the waqf for the necessity of repair, not to distribute that among the beneficiaries. Then number (بنك): The custodian borrowed for the needs of mosques, it is on himself. Number (عك): I do not trust him in our time. Number (حم): He has that. Number (بق): He does not borrow except with the judge's order. Then he mentioned what Abu al-Layth chose. End. In Jami' al-Fusulain, from chapter twenty-seven: If the custodian took the dirhams of the waqf and spent dinars on the repair of the waqf, it is valid if good. And if he spent on it from his own wealth, he recoups even if he did not stipulate, like a guardian. Then number (مق): He recoups if he stipulates, otherwise no. Then he said: It is mentioned in al-'Iddah that borrowing for the necessity of the interests of the waqf is permissible if the wakif ordered it, otherwise the chosen view is that he should raise the matter to the judge to order it. Then number (فط): The more cautious is that he raise the matter to him unless attendance is impossible due to distance, then he borrows himself. And it is said it is valid without raising even if possible. End. In the thirty-fourth: The custodian of the waqf if he spent from his wealth on the repair of the waqf, then if he bears witness that he spent to recoup, he has the right to recoup, otherwise no. End. In al-Hawi: It is permissible for the custodian, if he needs repair, to borrow on the waqf and spend that on it. The better is that it be with the permission of the ruler. End. The summary: Hilal forbids borrowing absolutely. Ibn Wahban interpreted it as when it is without the judge's order, and claimed that if it is with the judge's order, there is no disagreement. And the apparent is as al-Tarsusi mentioned: contrary to that, due to what you know of his reasoning. As for other than Hilal, some of them permit borrowing absolutely for repair, as in Jami' al-Fusulain. The relied-upon in the madhhab is: If the waqf has an alternative, he borrows absolutely; if it is indispensable, then if it is with the judge's order, it is permissible, otherwise no. Repair is indispensable, so he borrows for it with the judge's order. As for other than repair, if it is for spending on the beneficiaries, borrowing is not permissible even with the judge's permission because it has an alternative, as stated in al-Qunyah by his statement: Not for distributing that among the beneficiaries. And borrowing is more general than a loan and credit purchase. In al-Bazzaziyyah from the book of wills: If the custodian borrowed, if the wakif stipulated that for him, otherwise he raises to the ruler if needed. End. But there is confusion in some matters: Is it permissible to borrow for the imam, khateeb, and mu'adhdhin considering that it is indispensable, so it is only with the judge's permission or no? The apparent is that he does not borrow for them except with the judge's permission, because of his statement in Jami' al-Fusulain 'for the necessity of the interests of the mosque'. And he said in Khizanat al-Akmal: The waqf has wealth, and then he has the right to recoup if he bears witness that he spent to recoup, which agrees with what will come from Jami' al-Fusulain. And the apparent is that bearing witness is necessary judicially, not religiously, so it does not contradict 'he has the right to recoup'. His statement: 'whether its produce is collected or not' — the apparent is that it is based on the narration that does not require an order from a judge. His statement: 'The summary: Hilal forbids borrowing absolutely' — al-Ramli said: i.e., with permission and without permission. His statement: 'due to what you know of his reasoning' — al-Ramli said: i.e., the reasoning for this is his statement 'the waqf does not have a legal personality (dhimma)'. End. I say: But what passed from al-Waqi'at is explicit that there is no disagreement when it is with the judge's order.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion (sharh) on the rulings concerning the authority of a waqf custodian (qayyim or mutawalli) to borrow money on behalf of the waqf and to recoup personal expenses from the waqf's income. The author, Ibn Nujaym, and the supercommentator, al-Ramli, discuss various opinions and conditions. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute with multiple positions from early scholars like Abu al-Layth, Qadikhan, and later authorities. The core issue is: under what conditions can the custodian borrow and ensure reimbursement? The text explains that borrowing with the judge's permission is universally valid because the judge has authority over the waqf. Without the judge's permission, there are conflicting narrations. The relied-upon view in the madhhab distinguishes between cases where the waqf has available income (ghalla) and where it does not. If the waqf has income, the custodian may spend his own money and recoup from that income even without the judge's permission, analogous to an agent. If there is no income and the expense is indispensable (like repairs), borrowing is only allowed with the judge's order. For non-indispensable expenses (like distributions to beneficiaries), borrowing is not allowed even with permission because there is an alternative. The passage also discusses the precautionary method for inserting one's own property into the waqf to avoid suspicion, and the importance of bearing witness (ishhad) when spending personal funds to secure the right of recoupment in court (qada'an), though not required religiously (diyanatan). The final summary clarifies that Hilal's absolute prohibition is interpreted as pertaining to cases without the judge's order, and that the operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that for indispensable repairs, borrowing requires judge's permission; for other cases, it is either allowed absolutely or not at all.

Key Terms
القيم (al-qayyim): The custodian or administrator of a waqf, responsible for its maintenance and distribution of income.
الاستدانة (al-istidānah): Borrowing money on behalf of the waqf, including both taking a loan and buying on credit.
الغلة (al-ghallah): The income or produce generated by the waqf property, such as rent or crop yield.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the passage, under what condition may a waqf custodian borrow on behalf of the waqf without the judge's permission?
A) When the expense is for repairs and the waqf has no income.
B) When the waqf has income (ghalla) and the custodian has spent his own money.
C) When the expense is for distribution to beneficiaries.
D) Never; judge's permission is always required.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi view distinguishes based on the presence of waqf income and the indispensability of the expense: with income, recoupment without permission; without income, only indispensable expenses with judge's permission.

Waqf — Endowment
intermediate
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Borrowing for Waqf Maintenance
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute on borrowing by the waqf custodian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on borrowing for waqf maintenance and apply the relied-upon position to a scenario involving repair needs.

Core Idea
Borrowing for waqf repair is allowed with the judge's order; borrowing for beneficiaries is never allowed.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether a waqf custodian may incur debt for the waqf. The custodian needs funds for repairs, but the waqf has no legal personality (dhimma). Scholars like Hilal prohibit absolutely, while others permit with conditions. The relied-upon view strikes a balance between necessity and accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Determine what exactly is being disputed: the permissibility of borrowing (istidānah) by the waqf custodian for maintenance and for distribution to beneficiaries.
2
List the Positions
Identify the three main positions: Hilal's absolute prohibition, Ibn Wahban's interpretation (only prohibited without judge's order), and the permissive views (some allow absolutely for maintenance, others conditionally).
3
Understand the Reasoning
Analyze the rationale: Hilal argues the waqf has no dhimma, so borrowing is invalid. Others argue necessity permits it, but with conditions to prevent abuse.
The author highlights Tarsusi's criticism of Ibn Wahban's interpretation.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Identify the relied-upon position: borrowing for maintenance requires judge's permission if there is no alternative; borrowing for beneficiaries is never allowed even with judge's permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوقف جاز، سواء كانت غلته مستوفاة أو غير مستوفاة .اهـ. ثم قال : وللقيم الاستدانة على الوقف لضرورة العمارة لا لتقسيم ذلك على الموقوف عليهم ثم رقم (بنك) استقرض القيم لمصالح المساجد فهو على نفسه وبرقم (عك) لا أصدقه في زماننا وبرقم (حم) له ذلك وبرقم (بق) لا يستدين إلا بأمر القاضي ثم ذكر ما اختاره الفقيه أبو الليث اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين من الفصل السابع والعشرين : ولو أخذ المتولي دراهم الوقف وصرف دنانير إلى عمارة الوقف صح لو خيراً، ولو أنفق عليه من مال نفسه يرجع ولو لم يشترط كوصي ثم رقم (مق) يرجع لو شرط وإلا لا ثم قال : وذكر في العدة الاستدانة لضرورة مصالح الوقف تجوز لو أمر الواقف وإلا فالمختار أن يرفع إلى القاضي ليأمر بها. ثم رقم (فط) الأحوط أن يرفع الأمر إليه إلا إذا تعذر الحضور لبعده فيستدين بنفسه، وقيل يصح بلا رفع ولو أمكن اهـ. وفي الرابع والثلاثين : قيم الوقف لو أنفق من ماله في عمارة الوقف، فلو أشهد أنه أنفق ليرجع فله الرجوع وإلا فلا .اهـ. وفي الحاوي ويجوز للمتولي إذا احتاج إلى العمارة أن يستدين على الوقف ويصرف ذلك فيها والأولى أن يكون بإذن الحاكم .اهـ. والحاصل أن هلالاً مانع من الاستدانة مطلقاً وحمله ابن وهبان على ما إذا كان بغير أمر القاضي وادعى أنه إذا كان بأمر القاضي فلا خلاف فيه، والظاهر كما ذكره الطرسوسي خلافه لما علمت من تعليله، وأما غير هلال فمنهم من جوز الاستدانة مطلقاً للعمارة كما في جامع الفصولين: والمعتمد في المذهب إن كان له منه بدلاً . يستدين مطلقاً، وإن كان لا بد له فإن كان بأمر القاضي جاز وإلا فلا والعمارة لا بد لها فيستدين لها بأمر القاضي، وأما غير العمارة فإن كان للصرف على المستحقين لا تجوز الاستدانة ولو بإذن القاضي لأن له منه بداً كما صرح به في القنية بقوله : لا لتقسيم ذلك على الموقوف عليهم وأن الاستدانة أعم من القرض والشراء بالنسيئة. وفي البزازية من كتاب الوصايا : لو استقرض المتولي إن شرط الواقف له ذلك وإلا رفع إلى الحاكم إن احتاج اهـ .
The endowment is valid, whether its revenue is collected or not. Then he said: The custodian may borrow on the endowment for necessary repairs, not for distribution among the beneficiaries. Then symbol (bank): if the custodian borrows for the interests of the mosques, it is upon himself. Symbol (ak): I do not believe him in our time. Symbol (hm): he has that right. Symbol (bq): he should not borrow except by order of the judge. Then he mentioned what al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose. In Jami' al-Fusulayn from the twenty-seventh chapter: if the trustee takes the endowment's dirhams and spends dinars on the repair of the endowment, it is valid if he is a good person; and if he spends from his own money, he may reclaim even without stipulation, like a guardian. Then symbol (mq): he may reclaim if stipulated, otherwise not. Then he said: In al-'Uddah, borrowing for necessary interests of the endowment is permissible if the endower ordered it; otherwise, the preferred view is to refer to the judge to order it. Then symbol (ft): the precaution is to refer the matter to him unless attendance is impossible due to distance, then he borrows himself; it is said it is valid without referral even if possible. In al-Rabi' wa al-Thalathin: if the custodian of the endowment spends from his money on its repair, if he bears witness that he spent to reclaim, he may reclaim; otherwise not. In al-Hawi: it is permissible for the trustee, if he needs repair, to borrow on the endowment and spend it on that, and it is preferable to be with the judge's permission. The conclusion is that Hilal absolutely prohibits borrowing, and Ibn Wahban interpreted that as when it is without the judge's order, claiming that when it is with the judge's order there is no dispute; but the apparent view, as Tarsusi mentioned, is contrary, due to his reasoning. As for others besides Hilal, some permit borrowing absolutely for repair, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn: the relied-upon in the madhhab is that if there is an alternative, he borrows absolutely; if there is no alternative, then if with the judge's order it is permissible, otherwise not; and repair has no alternative, so he borrows for it with the judge's order. As for other than repair, if it is for spending on the beneficiaries, borrowing is not permissible even with the judge's permission because there is an alternative for him, as stated in al-Qunyah: 'Not for distribution among the beneficiaries.' And borrowing is more general than lending and credit purchase. In al-Bazzaziyyah from the book of wills: if the trustee borrows, if the endower stipulated that for him, otherwise he refers to the judge if needed.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the permissibility of borrowing (استدانة) by the custodian (قيم) of a waqf. The author begins by quoting various rulings from different sources. The key point is that borrowing for the repair and maintenance of the waqf is allowed under certain conditions, while borrowing for distribution to beneficiaries is never allowed. There is a disagreement among Hanafi scholars: Hilal Ibn Yahya prohibits borrowing absolutely, but Ibn Wahban interprets his view as only when done without the judge's permission. The author (likely al-Marghinani or Ibn Nujaym) favors the view of Tarsusi that the prohibition applies even with the judge's permission based on the reasoning that the waqf has no legal personality (ذمة). However, the relied-upon position (المعتمد) in the madhhab, as mentioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn, is that borrowing for repair is allowed absolutely if there is no alternative, but only with the judge's permission if there is an alternative. For non-repair expenses like distribution to beneficiaries, borrowing is impermissible even with the judge's permission because the custodian can use the available revenue. The passage also notes that borrowing here includes both loans (قرض) and credit purchases (شراء بالنسيئة). The discussion ends with a reference to al-Bazzaziyyah that the trustee's borrowing is subject to the endower's stipulation or referral to the judge.

Key Terms
استدانة (istidānah): Borrowing by the waqf custodian on behalf of the waqf, including loans and credit purchases.
قيم (qayyim): The custodian or manager of the waqf, also called متولي.
عمارة (ʿimārah): Repair and maintenance of the waqf property to keep it functional.
مصالح المسجد (maṣāliḥ al-masjid): The interests and needs of the mosque, including repair, oil, mats, etc. There is disagreement on what is included.
ذمة (dhimmah): Legal capacity to incur obligations; the waqf has no dhimma, so borrowing is problematic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when is borrowing by the waqf custodian permissible?
A) For any purpose with the judge's permission.
B) Only for maintenance and repair, and only with the judge's permission if there is no alternative.
C) For distribution to beneficiaries with the judge's permission.
D) For any purpose without the judge's permission if the waqf has sufficient revenue.

Key Takeaway
Borrowing for waqf maintenance is permissible only for necessity and with judicial authorization; borrowing for beneficiaries is never allowed.

Borrowing for Mosque Personnel and Supplies: Defining مصالح المسجد
Defining مصالح المسجد for borrowing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between expenses considered مصالح المسجد and those not, and state the condition that borrowing requires judge's permission.

Core Idea
Borrowing for mosque expenses is allowed only if the expense is from مصالح المسجد and with judicial permission.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical dilemma for mosque custodians: when can they borrow money for the mosque's needs? The core issue is defining what qualifies as 'the interests of the mosque' (مصالح المسجد). While there is broad agreement on physical repairs, later Hanafi scholars split on operational costs like oil for lamps, mats, and fans. The preferred view includes oil and mats but excludes fans. Critically, even for permitted expenses, borrowing always requires the judge's authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify disputed expenses
List the specific items debated: imam's salary, khatib, mu'adhdhin, oil for lamps, mats for prayer, and fans (مراوح).
The imam, khatib, and mu'adhdhin are personnel; oil, mats, and fans are supplies.
2
Understand opposing views on مصالح المسجد
Compare the restrictive view (Rukn al-Din al-Sabbaghi: only physical repair) and the inclusive view (Abu Hamid: also operational needs like oil and mats, but not fans).
The inclusive view is considered closer to the waqf founder's purpose.
3
Apply the preferred view to each expense
Determine which expenses fall under مصالح المسجد according to the preferred view: personnel and oil and mats are included; fans are not.
The khatib is analogous to the imam in a congregational mosque.
4
Recognize the procedural requirement
Even for permissible expenses, borrowing is only allowed with the judge's permission. Witnessing the loan is required for judicial (qada') purposes, not religiously (diyanatan).
The custodian cannot borrow without judicial authorization even for valid needs.

But confusion has occurred in issues, among them: Should the custodian borrow for the imam, khatib, and mu'adhdhin, considering that he has no alternative for that, so it should be only with the judge's permission, or not? The apparent view is that he should not borrow for them except with the judge's permission, because of the statement in Jami' al-Fusulayn: 'For the necessities of the mosque's interests.' They said: No. Then he coded for al-'Ala' al-Turjumani who said: Oil, mats, and fans are not from the mosque's interests; its interests are only its repair. Then he coded for Abu Hamid who said: Oil and mats are from its interests, but not fans. The author (Mawlana Badi' al-Din) said: This is more correct and closer to the purpose of the waqf founder. So it is settled that the preferred view is that they are from the interests, so he borrows with the judge's permission.

Commentary
This passage applies the general ruling on borrowing to specific mosque-related expenses. The key question is whether expenses for imam, khatib, mu'adhdhin, oil, and mats are considered 'interests of the mosque' (مصالح المسجد) that justify borrowing if the waqf has no liquid revenue. The text records a disagreement among later Hanafi scholars: some restrict مصالح المسجد to physical repair (عمارة), excluding operational costs like oil, mats, and fans. Others, including the preferred view, include oil and mats but not fans. For personnel (imam, mu'adhdhin), the custodian may pay them from waqf revenue, but borrowing for that requires judge's permission. The passage also clarifies that the khatib is analogous to the imam in a congregational mosque. The overall ruling is that borrowing for such expenses is allowed only with the judge's permission.

Key Terms
مصالح المسجد (maṣāliḥ al-masjid): The interests and needs of the mosque, including repair, oil, mats, etc. There is disagreement on what is included.
خطيب (khaṭīb): The preacher of the Friday sermon; in the context of the waqf, whether he can be paid from the mosque's revenue.
مراوح (marāwīḥ): Fans (probably hand fans or coolers); not considered from مصالح المسجد according to the preferred view.

Key Takeaway
The definition of مصالح المسجد is contested among Hanafi scholars; the preferred view includes oil and mats but excludes fans, and borrowing always requires judicial authorization.

Third-Party Spending on Waqf Beneficiaries: Borrowing or Direct Spending?
Ruling on whether the custodian's claim of borrowing with judge's permission is accepted without evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the two positions in the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a third party's right to reclaim spending on waqf beneficiaries with the custodian's authorization, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Spending on waqf beneficiaries with custodial approval is treated as borrowing for beneficiaries, which is invalid; the spender cannot reclaim.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a recurring problem in waqf management: what happens when a well-intentioned third party spends on beneficiaries expecting reimbursement? The author, Ibn Nujaym, argues that such spending constitutes borrowing for the beneficiaries, which is not permitted, so the spender has no recourse. Al-Ramli disagrees, equating it to the custodian's own spending. This lesson dissects the legal reasoning behind each position and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario and legal question
Read the passage to understand the specific case: a custodian authorizes a third party to spend on waqf beneficiaries from his own money, expecting reimbursement when waqf revenue arrives. The question is whether this is considered borrowing for the beneficiaries (batil, no recourse) or like the custodian's own spending (recourse allowed).
The passage also includes the preliminary issue of the custodian's own claim of borrowing with judge's permission.
2
State the author's (Ibn Nujaym's) position and its basis
Ibn Nujaym holds that the third party cannot reclaim because the transaction is effectively borrowing (istiqrāḍ) for the beneficiaries, and borrowing for beneficiaries is invalid. He argues that borrowing is limited to loans and credit purchases, and the third party's payment is a loan to the beneficiaries.
The author uses the term 'istiqrāḍ' synonymously with 'istidānah'.
3
State al-Ramli's position and its basis
Al-Ramli disagrees, arguing that the third party is like a borrower from the custodian (mustaqriḍ minhu) and the custodian ordered him to spend, so it is equivalent to the custodian spending directly. Therefore, recourse is allowed.
Al-Ramli's view is cited from his fatwa commentary.
4
Analyze the key distinction: borrowing vs. direct spending
The crux of the dispute is whether the third party's relationship is with the beneficiaries (loan to them) or with the custodian (as his agent). Ibn Nujaym emphasizes that the expectation of repayment from waqf revenue makes it a loan to the beneficiaries, which is invalid. Al-Ramli focuses on the custodian's authorization, deeming it as if the custodian spent himself.
Note also the distinction between dīyānatan (religiously valid) and qaḍā'an (judicially enforceable) – the custodian's own expenditure on repairs is accepted dīyānatan but not without witnesses.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Ibn Nujaym's view is the mufta bihi: the third party has no judicial recourse without evidence. Al-Ramli's opinion is a recognized ikhtilāf but not the relied-upon position.
The passage ends with a reference to Fatawa al-Hanuti confirming that the custodian's own spending on repair is accepted dīyānatan only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن المتولي لو ادعى أنه استدان بإذن القاضي هل يقبل قوله بلا بينة؟ الظاهر أنه لا يقبل وإن كان المتولي مقبول القول لما أنه يريد الرجوع الغلة في إنما قبل قوله فيما بيده، وعلى هذا لو كان الواقع أنه لم يستأذن القاضي يحرم وهو عليه أن يأخذ من الغلة لما أنه بغير الإذن متبرع .اهـ. وقد علمت مما نقلناه عن قاضيخان أنه لو ماله أو أدخل جذعاً له في الوقف لا يكون من باب الاستدانة لأنها محصورة في القرض والشراء بالنسيئة وعلى هذا فلو صرف المتولي للمستحقين من ماله لا يكون من الاستدانة وله الرجوع، ولكن قاضيخان قيده بالإنفاق على المرمة وقيده في جامع الفصولين بأن يشهد أنه أنفق ليرجع فوقع الاشتباه في الصرف على المستحقين. وعلى هذا وقع الاشتباه في زماننا في ناظر أذن إنساناً في الصرف على المستحقين من ماله قبل مجيء ليرجع به إذا جاءت الغلة هل يكون من باب الاستدانة للموقوف عليهم فلا تجوز ولا رجوع له، أو أنه كصرف الناظر عليهم من مال نفسه فله الرجوع ؟ إن قلنا ،برجوعه فإن قلت إنه دفع لهم بشرط أن يأخذ معاليمهم فقام مقامهم قلت قال في جامع الفصولين من السابع والعشرين : الوكيل لو لم يقبض ثمنه حتى لقي الآمر فقال بعت ثوبك من فلان فأنا أقضيك عنه ثمنه فهو أنفق من الغلة قوله : (الظاهر أنه لا يقبل الخ يؤيده أنه لا يقبل قوله إذا ادعى أنه أنفق من ماله ليرجع كما سيأتي عن البزازية فدعوى الاستدانة بالأولى تأمل . قوله : (أو أنه كصرف الناظر عليهم الخ) قال الرملي رحمه الله : الوجه أنه كصرفه بنفسه من مال نفسه إذ هو مستقرض منه وقد أمره بالصرف عليهم تأمل اهـ . أقول: إذا كان مستقرضاً لا يكون كصرفه من مال نفسه لأن الاستقراض استدانة فلا رجوع تأمل. قوله : (إن قلنا برجوعه) أقول : في فتاوى الحانوتي بعد ذكر السؤال عن ذلك ما نصه : الذي وقفت عليه في كلام أصحابنا أن الناظر إذا أنفق من . مال نفسه على عمارة الوقف ليرجع في غلته له الرجوع ديانة لكن لو ادعى ذلك لا يقبل منه بل لا بد من يشهد أنه أنفق ليرجع كما في
Among them: If the trustee claims that he borrowed with the judge's permission, is his statement accepted without evidence? The apparent view is that it is not accepted, even if the trustee is generally accepted in his statements, because he wants to reclaim from the revenue; his statement is only accepted regarding what is in his hand. Based on this, if in reality he did not seek the judge's permission, it is forbidden, and he must take from the revenue because without permission he is a benefactor. Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: (1) Whether the custodian's claim of having borrowed with the judge's permission is accepted without evidence. The apparent view is that it is not accepted, because the custodian is seeking to reclaim from the waqf revenue, and his statement is only accepted regarding what is in his hands. (2) A contemporary problem: a custodian authorizes a third party to spend on beneficiaries from his own money, expecting reimbursement when the waqf revenue arrives. The author analyzes whether this is considered borrowing (which would be invalid for beneficiaries) or like the custodian spending directly (which would allow recourse). The author suggests it is borrowing, so the third party cannot reclaim. He cites al-Ramli who disagrees. The passage ends with a reference to Fatawa al-Hanuti that the custodian's spending on repair with intention to reclaim is accepted religiously but not judicially.

Key Terms
متولي (mutawallī): The trustee or custodian of the waqf, also called قيم.
استقراض (istiqrāḍ): Borrowing; in the context, it is a form of استدانة.
ديانة (diyānatan): Religiously (as opposed to judicially); a ruling that is valid before Allah but not enforced in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person claims to have spent on waqf beneficiaries with the custodian's permission expecting reimbursement, can he reclaim?
A) Yes, always.
B) Yes, if he has a witness.
C) No, because it is considered borrowing for beneficiaries which is invalid.
D) No, because the custodian's permission is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, borrowing for beneficiaries is invalid; therefore, a third party who spends with custodial authorization but without explicit terms of reimbursement cannot reclaim from waqf revenue, though a minority opinion allows it.

Dispute over Mosque Utilities: Mats, Oil, and Fans
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mosque utilities scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on whether mats, oil, and fans are considered mosque utilities (masalih al-masjid) and apply the relied-upon view for borrowing permission.

Core Idea
Oil and mats are mosque utilities; fans are not, per the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute over what qualifies as a mosque utility (masalih al-masjid) for the purpose of borrowing with the judge's permission. The dispute focuses on three items: mats, oil, and fans. By examining the opinions of key Hanafi scholars and their reasoning based on the endower's purpose, you will learn the practical consequences for mosque management.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage asks whether the caretaker (qayyim) may borrow money with the judge's permission to purchase mats and oil for the mosque. The answer depends on whether these items are considered part of the mosque's utilities.
2
List the competing views
Note the three positions: (1) Rukn al-Din al-Sabbaghi et al.: fans are not utilities. (2) al-'Ala' al-Tarjumani: oil, mats, and fans are not utilities; only maintenance is. (3) Abu Hamid: oil and mats are utilities, but fans are not.
These are all within the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The preferred view (Abu Hamid, endorsed by Badr al-Din) is 'closer to correctness and nearer to the purpose of the endower.' The talil considers the essential practical needs of the mosque: oil and mats are necessary for worship, while fans are less essential.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that oil and mats are from the utilities, so borrowing with the judge's permission is permissible for them. Fans are excluded.
5
Apply the practical difference
Under the restrictive view (Tarjumani), the caretaker cannot borrow for oil or mats; under the broader view (Abu Hamid), he can. The mufta bihi follows the broader view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو وقف على مصالح المسجد يجوز دفع غلته إلى الإمام والمؤذن والقيم .اهـ. ولم يذكر الخطيب. قال في شرح المنظومة : ولا شك أنه في الجامع نظير من ذكر في المسجد اهـ. فعلى هذا تخرج الأربعة من قول القنية الموقوف عليهم ومنها هل يستدين بإذن القاضي للحصر والزيت بالمسجد أم لا؟ فعلى أنهما من المصالح له ذلك وإلا فلا وقد اختلف في كونهما من المصالح ففي القنية رقم لركن الدين الصباغي وقال : كتبت إلى المشايخ ورمز للقاضي عبد الجبار وشهاب الدين الإمامي هل للقيم شراء المراوح من مصالح المسجد؟ فقالا : لا . ثم رمز للعلاء الترجماني فقال : الدهن والحصير والمراوح ليس من مصالح المسجد وإنما مصالحه عمارته. ثم رمز لأبي حامد وقال : الدهن والحصير من مصالحه دون المراوح قال ـ يعني مولانا بديع الدين ـ وهو أشبه للصواب وأقرب إلى غرض الواقف .اهـ. فقد تحرر أن الراجح كونهما من المصالح فيستدين بإذن القاضي.
If one endows on the utilities of the mosque, it is permissible to give its revenue to the imam, the mu'adhdhin, and the caretaker. The preacher (al-Khatib) was not mentioned. He said in Sharh al-Manzumah: 'There is no doubt that in the mosque it is analogous to those mentioned in the mosque.' So on this basis, the four [positions] from the statement of al-Qunyah [about] the beneficiaries are derived. Among them: Is it permissible to borrow with the permission of the judge for mats and oil for the mosque or not? If they are from the utilities, he may do so, otherwise not. And there is disagreement regarding whether they are from the utilities. In al-Qunyah, [there is a notation] for Rukn al-Din al-Sabbaghi, who said: 'I wrote to the shaykhs and alluded to Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and Shihab al-Din al-Imami: Is it permissible for the caretaker to purchase fans from the mosque's utilities?' They both said: 'No.' Then he alluded to al-'Ala' al-Tarjumani, who said: 'Oil, mats, and fans are not from the mosque's utilities; its utilities are only its maintenance.' Then he alluded to Abu Hamid, who said: 'Oil and mats are from its utilities but not fans.' He (meaning Mawlana Badi' al-Din) said: 'This is closer to correctness and nearer to the purpose of the endower.' It has been settled that the preferable view is that both (oil and mats) are from the utilities, so he may borrow with the judge's permission.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether certain items—mats, oil, and fans—are considered part of the mosque's utilities (masalih al-masjid) for the purpose of borrowing money with the judge's permission. The context is a discussion on the permissibility of using endowed revenues for mosque needs. The base matn (from Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that if one endows on mosque utilities, the revenue can be given to the imam, mu'adhdhin, and caretaker. The commentator then raises the question of borrowing for mats and oil. Several Hanafi scholars are cited: Rukn al-Din al-Sabbaghi reports that Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and Shihab al-Din al-Imami opined that purchasing fans is not allowed from mosque utilities; al-'Ala' al-Tarjumani excluded oil, mats, and fans altogether, restricting utilities to maintenance; Abu Hamid included oil and mats but not fans. Badr al-Din (probably Ibn al-Sa'ati or similar) preferred Abu Hamid's view as closer to the endower's intent. The relied-upon (rajih) position is that oil and mats are from the utilities, so borrowing for them with the judge's permission is permissible. This reflects the Hanafi principle of considering the endower's purpose (gharad al-waqif) and the practical needs of the mosque.

Key Terms
مصالح المسجد (masalih al-masjid): The utilities and necessities of the mosque, including items like mats, oil, and maintenance materials.
استدانة (istidānah): Borrowing money, specifically with the permission of the judge for mosque expenses.
قيم (qayyim): The caretaker or trustee of the mosque or waqf, responsible for managing its affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves intra-madhhab disputes on mosque utilities by prioritizing the endower's purpose (gharad al-waqif), leading to the inclusion of essential consumables like oil and mats but not non-essential items like fans.

Trustee's Right to Recoup Waqf Maintenance Expenses
Rulings on trustee spending own money and seeking reimbursement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mutawalli's right to recoup personal funds spent on waqf maintenance and identify the conditions under which recourse is valid.

Core Idea
A trustee may recoup waqf expenses if he intended recourse at the time, but his bare claim is not accepted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq consolidates several Hanafi rulings on trustees spending their own money for waqf maintenance. The central issue is whether and how the trustee can recover those expenses. The text distinguishes between cases where the trustee intended recourse, where there was a stipulation, and where the trustee is a mosque superintendent. Understanding these conditions is crucial for anyone managing endowments.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
From al-Bazzaziyya: if the mutawalli spends his money intending recourse in waqf revenue, he has recourse. However, his claim alone is insufficient.
Intention (niyyah) at the time of spending is key.
2
Identify the conditions for recourse
Recourse requires either a prior stipulation or the intention to recoup at the moment of expenditure. Without these, the trustee cannot claim reimbursement.
The text also mentions that if the trustee stipulates recourse, he has it; otherwise not.
3
Note the exception for the mosque superintendent
The qayyim of a mosque who spends without judge's permission has no recourse at all, whether he intended it or not.
This contrasts with the mutawalli of a private waqf.
4
Analyze the proof by analogy
Jami' al-Fusulayn analogizes the mutawalli using his own wood for repair to a guardian using his own cloth for an orphan. Both are permitted and can recover because they acted in the interest of the ward.
The illa (effective cause) is benefit to the waqf/orphan.
5
Conclude with the evidentiary rule
If the trustee later claims reimbursement without prior arrangement or evidence, his statement is not accepted. He must have intended recourse or obtained permission.
This protects the waqf from unfounded claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يملك المعاوضة من مال متطوع ولا يرجع على المشتري ولو قال أنا أقضيكه عنه على أن يكون المال الذي على المشتري لي لم يجز، ورجع الوكيل على موكله بما دفع وفي العدة يباع عنده بضائع للناس أمروه ببيعها فباعها بثمن مسمى فعجل الثمن من ماله إلى أصحابها على أن أثمانها له إذا قبضها فأفلس المشتري فللبائع أن يسترد ما دفع إلى أصحاب البضائع اهـ. قال في القنية : إذا قال القيم أو المالك لمستأجرها أذنت لك في عمارتها فعمرها بإذنه يرجع على القيم والمالك، وهذا إذا كان يرجع معظم منفعته إلى المالك، أما إذا رجع إلى المستأجر وفيه ضرر بالدار كالبالوعة أو شغل بعضها كالتنور فلا ما لم يشترط الرجوع .اهـ. ويدل له بالأولى ما في جامع الفصولين : المتولي صرف العمارة من خشب مملوك له ودفع قيمته من مال الوقف كان له إذ ثمنه يملك صرف ثوب مملوك إلى الصبي ودفع من مال نفسه كوصي الصبي، ولكن لو ادعى لا يقبل قوله وهذا يشير إلى أنه لو أنفق ليرجع له الرجوع في مال الوقف واليتيم من غير أن يدعي عند القاضي، أما لو ادعى عند القاضي وقال أنفقت من مالي كذا في الوقف واليتيم لا يقبل قوله . ثم رقم بعلامة (بق) ادعى وصي أو قيم أنه أنفق مال نفسه وأراد الرجوع في مال اليتيم والوقف ليس له ذلك إذ يدعي ديناً لنفسه على اليتيم والوقف فلا يصح بمجرد الدعوى ؛ ذكره في أحكام العمارة. وفي البزازية: قيم الوقف أنفق ماله من في الوقف ليرجع في غلته له الرجوع، وكذا الوصي مع مال الميت ولكن لو ادعى لا يكون القول قوله المتولي إذا أنفق من مال نفسه ليرجع في مال الوقف له ذلك، فإن شرط الرجوع يرجع وإلا فلا .اهـ وفيها أيضاً قيم المسجد اشترى شيئاً لمؤنة المسجد بلا إذن الحاكم بماله لا يرجع على الوقف اهـ. وظاهره أنه لا رجوع له مطلقاً إلا بإذن القاضي سواء كان أنفق ليرجع أو لا، سواء رفع إلى القاضي أو لا. سواء برهن على ذلك أو لا .
He owns the compensation from voluntary wealth and does not have recourse against the buyer. If he says: 'I will pay it for him on condition that the money owed by the buyer becomes mine,' it is not valid, and the agent has recourse against his principal for what he paid. And in al-'Udda: goods of people are sold by his direction, he sells them at a named price and pays the price from his own wealth to their owners on condition that their prices become his when he receives them, then the buyer becomes insolvent; the seller may reclaim what he paid to the owners of the goods. End. He said in al-Qunyah: If the trustee or owner says to the lessee: 'I permit you to repair it,' and he repairs with his permission, he has recourse against the trustee and the owner. This is if most of the benefit reverts to the owner. As for if it reverts to the lessee and there is harm to the property like a cesspool or occupying part like an oven, then no [recourse] unless he stipulated recourse. End. And it is supported, a fortiori, by what is in Jami' al-Fusulayn: The mutawalli uses his own wood for repair and pays its value from waqf funds, he has it because its price is owned. He [the mutawalli] can use his own cloth for the orphan and pay from his own wealth like the orphan's guardian. But if he claims [so], his statement is not accepted. This indicates that if he spent to have recourse, he has recourse in waqf and orphan funds without claiming before the judge. But if he claims before the judge saying 'I spent from my wealth such and such on waqf and orphan,' his statement is not accepted. Then he marked with a sign (بق): If a guardian or trustee claims that he spent his own wealth and wants recourse from orphan's or waqf's wealth, he does not have that because he is claiming a debt against the orphan and waqf, so it is not valid by mere claim; mentioned in Ahkam al-Imarah. And in al-Bazzaziyya: The waqf trustee spends his money on waqf intending recourse in its revenue, he has recourse. Likewise the executor with the deceased's estate. But if he claims, his saying is not accepted. The mutawalli if he spends from his own wealth intending recourse in waqf wealth, he has that. So if he stipulates recourse, he has recourse; otherwise not. End. And in it also: The mosque superintendent buys something for mosque expenses without judge's permission with his own money, he does not have recourse against the waqf. End. Its apparent meaning is that he has no recourse absolutely except with the judge's permission, whether he spent for recourse or not, whether he brought it to the judge or not, whether he gave evidence or not.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings concerning trustees and guardians who spend their own money on maintenance of waqf or orphan property. The passage type is a collection of rulings with supporting evidence from earlier Hanafi sources. The author begins by discussing a scenario where someone voluntarily pays off a debt expecting to recoup from the buyer, but if he stipulates that the debt becomes his, it is invalid. Then he cites al-Qunyah: a lessee who repairs with permission has recourse unless the benefit is mostly to the lessee and there is harm. Then Jami' al-Fusulayn strengthens this with an analogy: a mutawalli who uses his own wood for repair and pays the value from waqf funds has recourse. However, his bare claim is not accepted; he must have intended recourse at the time of spending or have a stipulation. The Bazzaziyya adds that if the trustee spends intending recourse, he has it, but his claim alone is insufficient. For the mosque superintendent, spending without judge's permission gives no recourse. The apparent meaning is that no recourse is possible without judicial authorization. Overall, the key principle is that a trustee may recoup expenses from waqf revenue if he intended reimbursement, but he cannot merely claim it; there must be evidence or a prior arrangement.

Key Terms
متولي (mutawallī): The administrator or trustee of a waqf.
قيم (qayyim): A guardian or caretaker, often of an orphan or waqf.
رجوع (rujūʿ): The right to claim reimbursement or recoup expenses from the waqf or orphan's funds.
عمارة (ʿimārah): Maintenance or repair of the waqf property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He has no recourse unless he obtained the judge's permission beforehand.
C) He has recourse if he intended to recoup at the time of spending, but his claim alone is not accepted.
D) He can only recoup if the waqf deed explicitly allows it.

Key Takeaway
A trustee's right to reimbursement for waqf maintenance depends on his intention at the time of spending; mere claims are insufficient.

The Exception for Stipulated Maintenance in Waqf
Explains an exception to the rule that no one initiates maintenance without order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that when maintenance is stipulated in a waqf, it takes precedence over the rent beneficiary's right, and identify the two conditions under which the beneficiary's right is prioritized.

Core Idea
Stipulated maintenance in waqf overrides the beneficiary's right to revenue, unless the right is fixed-term and no harm results.

In waqf management, a general principle holds that no one should initiate maintenance without authorization. However, this passage from al-Muhit, as reported in Jami' al-Fusulayn, carves out a critical exception: when the founder stipulates that maintenance be performed from the waqf revenue, that obligation takes precedence over the beneficiary's entitlement. But this exception itself has qualifications, especially when the beneficiary has a fixed short-term right. Let us examine the logic and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and the exception
Start by noting the general rule: no one may initiate maintenance on waqf property without authorization. The exception: if maintenance is stipulated in the waqf deed, it takes precedence over the beneficiary's right to revenue.
2
Understand the basis: stipulation (shart) in waqf
The waqf founder's condition is binding. Therefore, when the founder explicitly requires maintenance from the revenue, the trustee must fulfill that condition even if it delays payments to the beneficiary.
Hanafi law gives great weight to the founder's stipulations.
3
Recognize the qualification: fixed-term beneficiary
If the revenue is assigned to someone for a limited period (e.g., one or two years), the beneficiary's right is prioritized because their entitlement expires, whereas the waqf lasts forever and can be repaired later.
This qualification is based on the fear of voiding the beneficiary's right entirely.
4
Analyze the role of harm (darar) in determining precedence
Even with a fixed-term beneficiary, if delaying maintenance would cause clear harm to the waqf (e.g., destruction), then maintenance takes precedence to preserve the property's purpose.
Harm overrides even the fixed-term right.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars (e.g., al-Khassaf) argued that maintenance is always postponed unless delay causes destruction. The relied-upon position, however, gives priority to the stipulation itself, with the fixed-term and harm exceptions.
This shows the range of opinion within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : يستثنى من قولهم لا يقدم على العمارة أحد ما في المحيط : لو شرط العمارة في الوقف فإنه تقدم العمارة على صاحب الغلة إلا إذا جعلت غلتها لفلان سنة أو سنتين ثم من الرابع والثلاثين من جامع الفصولين وكلامهم هذا يقتضي أن ذلك ليس من الاستدانة على الوقف وإلا لما جاز إلا بإذن القاضي ولم يكف الإشهاد وحيث لم يكن من الاستدانة فلا مانع أن يكون الصرف على المستحق من ماله مساوياً للصرف على العمارة من ماله. نعم الاستدانة على الوقف لأجل الصرف على المستحق لا تجوز وإنما جوزوها لما لا بد للوقف منه كالعمارة هذا ما ظهر اهـ . قلت : انظر ما قدمنا في التوفيق بين كلام الخانية وجامع الفصولين. قوله : (ما) في جامع الفصولين) أي ذكره في الرابع والثلاثين قوله : (الخامسة يستثنى الخ) قيل : لا محل لهذا الاستثناء لأن محل قولهم الذي يبدأ به من غلة الوقف تعميره ما إذا كان في ترك العمارة ضرر بين، ومحل مسألة الخصاف ما إذا لم يكن في ترك تعمير الوقف هلاك الوقف يشعر بذلك قول الخصاف على وجه التعليل للحكم بعده للفقراء، أو شرط العمارة من الغلة فإنه يؤخر العمارة عن حق صاحب الغلة لأنا لو صرفنا الغلة إلى العمارة أولاً أدى إلى إبطال حق صاحب الغلة لأن حقه في الغلة في مدة مخصوصة فتنتهي بمضيها، ولو صرفناها إليه أولاً لا يؤدي إلى فوات عمارة الوقف لأنه يمكن عمارته في السنة الثانية إلا إذا كان في تأخير العمارة ضرر بين بالوقف فحينئذ تقدم العمارة لئلا يؤدي إلى إبطال مقصود الواقف .اهـ. وقيد بالسنتين لما في التتارخانية : وأما المشروط له الغلة في ثلاث سنين يؤخذ بالعمارة .اهـ
The fifth: An exception to their statement that no one initiates maintenance [without order] is what is in al-Muhit: If maintenance is stipulated in the waqf, then maintenance takes precedence over the rent beneficiary unless the revenue is assigned to someone for one or two years, then from the thirty-fourth [section] of Jami' al-Fusulayn. Their words here imply that this is not borrowing against the waqf, otherwise it would not be permitted except with judge's permission and witnessing would not suffice. Since it is not borrowing, there is no impediment to spending on the beneficiary from his own money being equal to spending on maintenance from his own money. Yes, borrowing against the waqf for spending on the beneficiary is not permitted; they only allowed it for what is indispensable for the waqf like maintenance. This is what appeared. End. I say: Look at what we presented earlier about reconciling the statements of al-Khaniyya and Jami' al-Fusulayn. His statement: '(what) in Jami' al-Fusulayn' i.e., he mentioned it in the thirty-fourth. His statement: '(the fifth: an exception etc.)' It is said: There is no place for this exception because the place of their statement that one begins with from the waqf revenue its maintenance is when there is clear harm in leaving maintenance, and the place of the issue of al-Khassaf is when leaving maintenance does not cause destruction of the waqf; this is indicated by the statement of al-Khassaf by way of reasoning for the ruling afterwards for the poor, or if maintenance is stipulated from the revenue, then maintenance is delayed behind the right of the rent beneficiary, because if we spend the revenue on maintenance first, it leads to voiding the right of the rent beneficiary since his right is in the revenue for a specific period ending with its passing. And if we spend it to him first, it does not lead to loss of waqf maintenance because it can be repaired in the second year, unless delaying maintenance causes clear harm to the waqf, in which case maintenance is given precedence to avoid voiding the waqf founder's purpose. End. And it is restricted to two years because of what is in al-Tatarkhaniyya: As for the one assigned the revenue for three years, he is required to do maintenance. End.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage discusses an exception to the general rule that no one should initiate maintenance on a waqf without authorization. The exception comes from al-Muhit: when maintenance is explicitly stipulated in the waqf deed, it takes precedence over the rent beneficiary's right to the revenue. However, if the revenue has been assigned to someone for a fixed term (one or two years), then the beneficiary's right is prioritized because their period is limited. The author explains that this is not considered borrowing against the waqf (which would require judge's permission), so the trustee may spend his own money on maintenance and recoup it from the revenue. The commentary then addresses a potential objection that the exception is unnecessary, clarifying that the original rule applies only when there is no clear harm from delaying maintenance. The ruling is further qualified: if the delay would cause destruction, maintenance is given precedence even over a fixed-term beneficiary. The number of years (one or two) is derived from al-Tatarkhaniyya, which states that a beneficiary for three years must undertake maintenance.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā): An exception to a general ruling; here it qualifies the rule that no one initiates maintenance without authorization.
غلة (ghallah): The revenue or yield generated by the waqf property.
صاحب الغلة (sāḥib al-ghallah): The person entitled to receive the revenue from the waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever the maintenance is stipulated in the waqf deed.
B) Only if delaying maintenance would cause clear harm to the waqf property.
C) Never, as the beneficiary's right is superior.
D) Only if the revenue is assigned for less than two years.

Key Takeaway
When maintenance is stipulated in a waqf, it takes precedence over the beneficiary's right to revenue, but a short-term beneficiary (1-2 years) receives priority unless delaying maintenance would cause harm.

Priority Among Waqf Beneficiaries: Imam, Teacher, and Others
Determines who among waqf recipients gets priority alongside maintenance expenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the priority of waqf revenue allocation among maintenance, imam, teacher, and other beneficiaries according to the Hanafi school, and explain the effect of the founder's condition.

Core Idea
In waqf, maintenance and essential personnel (imam, teacher) take priority over other beneficiaries, but the founder's specification can override this order.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the order of spending from waqf revenues. Beyond maintenance, the imam and teacher are prioritized because their roles are essential to the waqf's purpose. However, the founder's stipulations and the specificity of the waqf can alter this default hierarchy. We will analyze the default order, the condition of 'if not specified', and the scholarly dispute surrounding the priority of functionaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default priority order
From the text, determine the first charge on waqf revenue. Then, identify the next category of beneficiaries mentioned. Note the sequence: maintenance, then what is 'closest to maintenance' like imam and teacher, then lamps and mats.
The text uses the phrase 'ما هو أقرب إلى العمارة وأعم للمصلحة' to link imam and teacher to maintenance.
2
Understand the role of 'sha'air'
The term 'sha'air' refers to essential personnel serving the waqf. In context, it includes the imam and teacher. Recognize that they are considered integral to the waqf's function, hence their priority.
Later Hanafi writings use 'sha'air' loosely for functionaries.
3
Recognize the condition 'if not specified'
Al-Hawi's full statement conditions the default priority: 'هذا إذا لم يكن معيناً' (this is if [the waqf] is not specified). If the waqf is mu'ayyan (designated for specific beneficiaries), the founder's direction must be followed after maintenance.
This condition is crucial for resolving the apparent unconditional priority.
4
Analyze the dispute about unconditional priority
Some scholars interpret al-Hawi's earlier words as giving imam and teacher unconditional precedence. Others, citing the condition, argue that equality among all beneficiaries is possible if the founder so stipulates. Identify the difference in practical outcome.
Al-Nahr records this dispute; the author offers a reconciliation by considering equality derived from al-Fath's statement.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is that maintenance always comes first, then imam and teacher are prioritized unless the founder specified equality or other distribution. The founder's condition is honored as long as it does not contradict the waqf's purpose.
Authority: al-Hawi al-Qudsi, al-Fath, al-Nahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة في بيان من يقدم مع العمارة وهو المسمى في زماننا بالشعائر ولم أره إلا في الحاوي القدسي قال : والذي يبتدأ به من ارتفاع الوقف عمارته شرط الواقف أو لا، ما هو أقرب إلى العمارة وأعم للمصلحة كالإمام للمسجد والمدرس للمدرسة يصرف إليهم إلى قدر كفايتهم، ثم السراج والبساط كذلك إلى آخر المصالح اهـ. وظاهره تقديم الإمام والمدرس على جميع المستحقين بلا شرط، والتسوية بالعمارة الذي ذكره لأن تأخير العمارة سنة ليس مما يخرج الوقف عن حاله. قوله : (ولم أره إلا في الحاوي) فيه أنه قدم في الثالثة عن الفتح بيان ذلك ومفاده مساواة من خيف بقطعه الضرر للتعمير . قوله : (إلى آخر المصالح) تمام عبارة الحاوي : هذا إذا لم يكن معيناً، فإن كان الوقف معيناً على شيء يصرف إليه بعد عمارة البناء. قوله : (وظاهره تقديم الإمام والمدرس على جميع المستحقين بلا شرط) أي بلا شرط من الواقف أن الإمام والمدرس يقدمان على غيرهم، وقد علمت أن كلام الحاوي فيه حيث قال : هذا إذا لم يكن معيناً. قوله : (والتسوية بالعمارة تقتضي تقديمها (الخ المراد بالتسوية المستفادة من قوله ما هو أقرب للعمارة مع أنها معطوفة بـ «ثم» المفيدة للترتيب لكن ما قرب من الشيء يعطى حكمه، ويحتمل أن يراد التسوية المستفادة من كلام الفتح السابق في المسألة الثالثة، ثم إن ما ذكره من تقديم من ذكر ولو شرط الواقف الاستواء عند الضيق. قال في النهر : نازعه فيه بعض الموالي بقول الحاوي «هذا إذا لم يكن معيناً» اهـ. وعلى ما قلنا من احتمال أن المراد التسوية المستفادة من كلام الفتح تندفع المنازعة تأمل.
The sixth: Explaining who is given precedence alongside maintenance, which is called in our time 'al-sha'air'. I have not seen it except in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. He said: What is initiated from the waqf revenue is its maintenance whether the waqf founder stipulated it or not. Then what is closest to maintenance and most beneficial, like the imam for the mosque and the teacher for the madrasa; they are given up to their sufficiency. Then the lamp and mat likewise, up to other interests. End. Its apparent meaning is that the imam and teacher are given precedence over all beneficiaries without condition, and equality with maintenance because delaying maintenance for a year does not take the waqf out of its state. His statement: 'I have not seen it except in al-Hawi.' In it, he mentioned earlier in the third [section] from al-Fath a clarification of that, and its purport is that those whose exclusion would cause harm are equated with maintenance. His statement: 'up to other interests.' The complete phrase of al-Hawi is: 'This is if it is not specified. If the waqf is specified for something, it is spent on that after maintenance of the building.' His statement: 'Its apparent meaning is precedence of imam and teacher over all beneficiaries without condition' i.e., without a condition from the waqf founder that the imam and teacher are given precedence over others. You have known that al-Hawi's words are conditioned where he said: 'This is if it is not specified.' His statement: 'And equality with maintenance requires its precedence...' What is meant by equality derived from his statement 'what is closest to maintenance' even though it is conjoined with 'thumma' which implies order, but what is close to a thing is given its ruling. It is possible that what is meant is the equality derived from the statement of al-Fath earlier in the third issue. Then what he mentioned of giving precedence to those mentioned even if the waqf founder stipulated equality when [revenue] is insufficient. He said in al-Nahr: Some scholars disputed with him over the saying of al-Hawi 'this is if it is not specified.' End. Based on our possibility that what is meant is the equality derived from the statement of al-Fath, the dispute is repelled. Reflect.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage addresses the priority of disbursement from waqf revenue alongside maintenance. It cites al-Hawi al-Qudsi, stating that maintenance is the first priority, then the imam of the mosque and the teacher of the madrasa are given their due, followed by other necessities like lamps and mats. The author notes that this gives the imam and teacher precedence over other beneficiaries even without a specific condition from the waqf founder. However, al-Hawi's condition 'if it is not specified' indicates that if the waqf is designated for particular recipients, those designations must be followed after maintenance. The commentary discusses whether the imam and teacher are truly given unconditional precedence or whether they are equal to maintenance in priority. The author suggests that the phrase 'what is closest to maintenance' implies a gradation, but ultimately if the waqf founder stipulated equality among beneficiaries in times of scarcity, that condition should be respected. The passage ends with a note that al-Nahr records a dispute about this interpretation, and the author offers a reconciliation based on al-Fath's earlier ruling.

Key Terms
شعائر (sha'ā'ir): In this context, it refers to the personnel serving the waqf, such as imam, teacher, etc.
معين (mu'ayyan): Specified; a waqf whose beneficiaries are explicitly named or designated by the founder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hawi al-Qudsi as cited in the text, who must be given priority in waqf expenditure after maintenance?
A) All beneficiaries equally.
B) The imam of the mosque and the teacher of the madrasa.
C) The poorest among the beneficiaries.
D) The waqf founder's relatives.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, after maintenance, essential functionaries (imam, teacher) are prioritized over other beneficiaries, but the founder's explicit conditions can override this default order.

Competing Conditions in a Waqf: Haramayn vs Essential Functionaries
A fatwa request about a waqf with conflicting priorities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the competing priorities in a waqf when revenue is insufficient and state the Hanafi principle for resolving such conflicts.

Core Idea
Essential functionaries of a waqf take precedence over a condition for the Haramayn in times of scarcity.

This lesson presents a real fatwa question that tests the boundaries of founder's conditions in waqf law. A founder specified fixed shares for multiple local beneficiaries and a separate share for the Haramayn, with a condition that the Haramayn be prioritized if revenue falls short. When the revenue becomes insufficient, which obligation takes precedence? We will examine the Hanafi approach that balances the founder's intent with the necessity of preserving the waqf's core functions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the beneficiaries and shares
List all groups mentioned: khatib, imam, mu'adhdhins, doorkeepers, servants, teachers from four madhhabs, students, reciters, and also the Haramayn (Mecca and Medina). Each has a fixed amount.
The founder carefully allocated fixed sums for each.
2
Locate the conflict condition
Read the founder's stipulation: 'if the waqf falls short of the expenditures, what is assigned to the direction of the waqf for the two noble sanctuaries shall be given precedence.' This is the condition in question.
The condition explicitly prioritizes Haramayn over other expenditures.
3
Identify the practical dilemma
Assume revenue is insufficient to cover all fixed shares. The question is whether to implement the Haramayn priority, or to treat all beneficiaries equally, or to prioritize the essential functionaries (arbab al-sha'air) like the imam and teacher.
The arbab al-sha'air are vital to the waqf's purpose.
4
Recall the Hanafi principle
In Hanafi waqf law, the founder's conditions are generally binding, but they are subject to the condition that they do not defeat the waqf's essential purpose. The rule is that the necessary functions of the waqf (such as maintaining religious rites and education) must be sustained to prevent the waqf from becoming defunct.
This is a corollary of the principle 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by their objectives).
5
Apply the principle to derive the answer
Since the arbab al-sha'air are essential for the waqf to operate, their shares take precedence over the Haramayn condition, unless the founder explicitly made the Haramayn share a core part of the waqf's purpose (e.g., a condition that the waqf is primarily for the Haramayn). In this case, since the Haramayn is just one of many beneficiaries, the condition to prioritize them is overridden by necessity.
The answer in the text starts 'The revenue became insufficient...' indicating the ruling likely follows this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول الفقير جامع هذه الحواشي : رأيت بخط شيخنا المحشي رحمه الله تعالى في هذا المحل ما نصه : بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله وكفى وسلام على عباده الذين اصطفى وبعد، فقد رفع لعلماء الإسلام الأئمة الأعلام، سؤال على لسان أهل الحرمين الشريفين والمقامين المنيفين، وهو ما يفيد موالينا مشايخ الإسلام أدام الله تعالى الانقياد إليهم والاستسلام في واقف شرط في كتاب وقفه خطيباً وإماماً ومؤذنين وبوابين وخدمة ومدرسين من المذاهب الأربعة وطلبة وقراء وغير ذلك ، ثم شرط في كتاب وقفه المذكور أنه إذا الوقف عن المصارف قدم ما هو مرتب على جهة الوقف للحرمين الشريفين، والحال أن الواقف عين لكل من المذكورين قدراً معيناً، وشرط للحرمين الشريفين قدراً معيناً، فهل إذا ضاق ريع الوقف على الحكم المذكور تقدم جهة الحرمين بما شرط لهم عملاً بالشرط المذكور أو يلغى هذا الشرط ويسوى في هذا الوقف بين جميع المستحقين من أهل الحرمين ،وغيرهم أم تقدم أرباب الشعائر بما شرط لهم وإن شرط الواقف تقديم الحرمين؟ افتونا مأجورين أثابكم الله تعالى الجنة آمين . فكتب : الحمد لله ضاق ريع
The poor compiler of these marginal notes says: I saw in the handwriting of our teacher, the annotator, may Allah have mercy on him, in this place, the following text: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, sufficient, and peace upon His chosen servants. So if the revenue of the waqf becomes insufficient according to the mentioned ruling, does the direction of the Haramayn take precedence with what was stipulated for them, acting upon the mentioned condition, or is this condition invalidated and all beneficiaries from the people of the Haramayn and others are made equal, or do the people of the rituals (arbab al-sha'air) take precedence with what was stipulated for them even if the founder stipulated precedence for the Haramayn? Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents a formal question (istifta') addressed to Hanafi scholars about a complex waqf case. The founder created a waqf with multiple beneficiaries: a khatib, imam, mu'adhdhins, doorkeepers, servants, teachers from all four madhhabs, students, reciters, etc., each with a fixed share. Additionally, he stipulated a fixed amount for the Haramayn (Mecca and Medina) and a condition that if the waqf revenue falls short, the allocation for the Haramayn should be given precedence. The question asks: when revenue is insufficient, should the Haramayn condition be honored (giving them priority), or should all beneficiaries be treated equally, or should the 'arbab al-sha'air' (the essential functionaries like imam and teacher) be prioritized despite the founder's condition? The answer is cut off but begins with 'Praise be to Allah. The revenue became insufficient...' indicating a ruling. This case illustrates the tension between the founder's explicit conditions and the general Hanafi principle that essential functions (shaha'ir) may take precedence in times of scarcity. The answer likely resolves this by evaluating the founder's intention and the necessity of maintaining the waqf's purpose.

Key Terms
شعائر (sha'ā'ir): Here, 'arbab al-sha'air' refers to the essential functionaries of the waqf, like the imam, khatib, teachers, etc.
الحرمين الشريفين (al-Ḥaramayn al-Sharīfayn): The two noble sanctuaries: Mecca and Medina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whether to allocate funds to the Haramayn or to the other beneficiaries proportionally.
B) Whether the founder's condition to prioritize the Haramayn should override the need to sustain local functionaries like the imam and teacher.
C) Whether the waqf can be dissolved and the proceeds redistributed.
D) Whether the revenue should be saved for future years.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, the founder's condition to prioritize a specific beneficiary may be overridden by the necessity to preserve the waqf's essential functions when revenue is insufficient.
